ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUN21ARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        The livestock subsector represents an important force in the future
economic development of Ethiopia. The rangelands in particular have an im-
mense potential for the sustained production of livestock and livestock pro-
ducts, and they are unique in that this potential could be exploited with
only relatively small additional capital inputs. In the past, however, the
total investments have not been commensurate with the magnitude of the devel-
opment potential, and insufficient account has been taken of the need for a
balanced investment program. This has reinforced the normal cyclical over-
stocking and over-grazing of the range areas brought about by traditional
nomadic range and livestock management practices. Priority therefore has been
given by Government to the development of a large-scale program aimed at the
expansion of livestock production, and the rationalization and modernization
of the traditional livestock sector.
ii.       The Project would over a five year period provide for the develop-
ment and rehabilitation of three lowland range areas inhabited mostly by
nomadic pastoralists engaged in extensive livestock production. The Project
would consist of three separate and distinct sub-projects, each with its own
organizational structure. These sub-projects - The Southern Rangelands,
Jijigga and Northeast Rangelands Development Projects - would each provide an
integrated program of range management and veterinary services along with
improved roads and water facilities. Through the establishment of ranches,
a feedlot and smallhclder fattening programs they would also aid in the stra-
tification of the Ethiopian livestock industry, thus helping to lay the found-
ation for the sound future development of the livestock economy in the range
areas. Finally, provision would also be made for a program of trials and
studies and for training of Project staff, livestock owners and tribal leaders.
In the short term the Project would attempt to restructure the traditional
system of extensive livestock production, thus substantially raising the value
of marketed livestock production and resulting in significantly increased
incomes for the nomadic producers. One of the primary goals would be the
eventual establishment of a comprehensive system of range use controls under
which the nonads would voluntarily adjust their overall stock numbers to meet
the carrying capacity of the ranges. As all the three areas differ markedly
with regard to geographic and climatic conditions and are inhabited by peoples
with widely varying languages and tribal customs, the individual sub-projects
would necessarily adopt a slightly different strategy in each region. The
overall objective would in each case remain the same, however, and in all three
areas a concerted effort would be made to actively involve the nomads in the
planning and execution of Project activities.
iii.      Overall direction of the Project would be the responsibility of the
Livestock and Meat Board. Each of the three sub-projects would have a separ-
ate organizational unit based at a headouarters within the individual project



- ii -
areas. These units - The Southern Rangelands Development Unit (SORDU), The
Jijigga Rangelands Development Unit (JIRDU), and the Northeast Rangelands
Development Unit (NERDU) - would each be under direction of a Project Manager
directly responsible to the head of LMB's Rangeland Development Department.
The Project Manager would have immediate responsibility for the coordination
of all Project development activities in his area.  In order to ensure coordi-
nation of these activities with other programs currently underway, each Manager
would set up a Project Development Committee, whose membership would include
the Head of the (Awraja) Administration, other local representatives, as
well as representatives from the livestock owners in the area. LMB would
help coordinate the activities of all three projects and would provide certain
centralized administrative and technical services through its headquarters
in Addis Ababa.
iv.       An IDA credit of US$27.0 million is proposed which would cover about
US$14.0 million of the foreign exchange component of the Project and 54% of
local costs over the five year period. This would represent 63% of total
Project costs of Eth$ 88.8 million (US$42.9 million), including import taxes
and duties estimated at Eth$ 0.6 million (US$0.3 million). The proposed African
Development Fund (ADF) contribution would total 5.0 million African Development
Fund units of account (about US$5.5 million); this would finance the remaining
US$2.9 million of the Project's foreign exchange costs and US$2.6 million of
local costs. Together the ADF and the IDA contributions would finance 76%
of total Project costs, and the remaining 24% would be contributed by Govern-
ment. In addition, farmers participating in the smallholder fattening programs
would make a small cash contribution toward the cost of fattening and purchase
of each animal; these contributions, totalling about US$0.40 million over the
five year period, were not included in the cost estimates. The ADF, IDA and
Government contributions would be channelled directly by Government to LMB,
which would establish separate bank accounts for each of the three sub-projects.
Procurement of vehicles would be subject to international competitive bidding.
Headquarters buildings and senior staff housing would be constructed according
to normal Government procedures. All other buildings and field constructions
would be built by the Project's ownl resources through force account or by local
contractors hired by LMB. Veterinary equipment, medicines, and fertilizer
would be procured through normal commercial channels.
v.        At full development (year 7) the Project would result in increased
marketed production from the ranges of 100,000 head of cattle, 48,500 sheep
and goats, and 3,000 camels annually. In addition to this increased offtake,
the ranches, feedlot and smallholder fattening programs would result in signi-
ficant increases in total marketed meat production. The total incremental
meat production attributable to the Project at full development would be
equivalent to 15,000 tons of meat, representing about 14% of present estimated
meat production. The Project would also result in increased production of
milk from range cattle totalling about 7.8 million litres of milk annually at
full development. Finally, incremental crop production from the water spreading
program in the Northeast Rangelands area would total about 1.1 million kg of
cotton, 320,000 kg of sorghum or maize, and 120,000 kg of groundnuts annually
at full development. The total annual value of incremental production attri-
butable to the Project at full development would be Eth$ 23.6 million.



- iii -
vi.       The overall economic rate of return for the Project over twenty
years is estimated at 24%. Individual rates of return were calculated for
each of the three sub-projects: these are estimated at 22% for Southern
Rangelands, 25% for Jijigga, and 26% for Northeast Rangelands. Rates of
return were also calculated for certain specific components within these
sub-projects; these were estimated at 17% for the ranches, 29% for the feedlot
and 13% for the water spreading/irrigation program. The Project would ensure
permanent employment for some 500 persons, and around 4,000 workers would be
given temporary employment during the implementation period. Overall the
Project would affect an estimated 100,000 families in the rangeland areas; the
average per capita income of these pastoralists would be increased from Eth$
60 (US$29) to about Eth$85 (US$41) over the life of the Project, thus sub-
stantially raising the incomes of one of the poorest segments of Ethiopia's
population.
vii.      This would be the Bank Groups' twelfth lending for agricultural
development in Ethiopia since the beginning of 1969. rwo of these IDA pro-
jects have been for livestock development - the Addis Ababa Dairy and the
Second Livestock Development Projects. The remaining projects in the agri-
cultural sector included the Lower Adiabo Development Project, two Wolamo
Development Projects, and the Humera, Amibara Irrigation, the Coffee Process-
ing, the Minimum Package, and the Drought Areas Rehabilitation Projects;
there was also one agricultural credit project - the Agricultural and Industrial
Development Bank (AIDB) Project.
viii.     The proposed Project would be suitable for an IDA credit of US$27
million on standard terms to the Government of Ethiopia.






ETHIOPILA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The livestock subsector represents an important force in the future
economic development of Ethiopia. The rangelands in particular have an immense
potential for the sustained production of livestock and livestock products,
and they are unique in that this potential could be exploited with only rela-
tively small additional capital inputs. In the past, however, the total
investments have not been commensurate with the magnitude of the development
potential, and insufficient account has been taken of the need for a balanced
investment program. This has reinforced the normal cyclical over-stocking and
over-grazing of the range areas brought about by traditional nomadic range
and livestock management practices. Priority therefore has been given by
Government to the development of a large-scale program for the expansion of
livestock production. The proposed Project would support this strategy by
providing an integrated program of range management and expanded veterinary
services along with improved roads and water facilities; through the estab-
lishment of ranches and cattle fattening programs it would also aid in the
stratification of the livestock industry in an effort to provide an even flow
of quality meat both for sale on local markets and for export.
1.02      This would be the Bank Group's twelfth lending for agricultural
development in Ethiopia since the beginning of 1969. Two of these IDA projects
have been for livestock development - the Addis Ababa Dairy and the Second
Livestock Development Projects. The Dairy Project provided US$4.4 million in
1971 for the expansion of milk production; after some organizational and
pricing difficulties the project is now in the process of being re-structured.
The Second Livestock Project for US$5.0 million in 1973 was designed to set
up an integrated market and stockroute system in three important livestock
producing areas of the country. It has, however, experienced serious cost
overruns and management difficulties and is now well behind schedule; some
stockroutes have already been eliminated from the proposed system, and further
restrictions in the construction of markets and slaughter facilities may be
necessary in the future. The remaining projects in the agricultural sector
included the Lower Adiabo Development Project, two Wolamo Development Pro-
jects, and the Humera, Amibara Irrigation, the Coffee Processing, the Minimum
Package, and the Drought Areas Rehabilitation Projects;  nhere was also one
agricultural credit project - the Agricultural and Industrial Development
Bank (AIDB) Project.
1.03      The proposed Project was prepared by the Livestock and Meat Board
and by a consultant firm working on the Southern Rangelands Project; assist-
ance was also provided by FAO and by the Bank's Permanent Mission in Eastern
Africa (PMEA). This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission
which visited Ethiopia in January/February 1975 comprising Messrs. G.R.
Henderson, T. Baddar, Its. J. Maguire, Messrs. H. Sanger and P. Sihm (IDA),
M. Negrin (ADB) and H. Cooke and M. Creek (Consultants). A follow-up mission
consisting of Messrs. D. Argyle, G.R. Henderson and J. Hendry visited Ethiopia in
June 1975.



- 2 -
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      Ethiopia with a total land area of about 1.2 million km  lies in
the northeastern horn of Africa just north of the Equator. The central part
of the country is mostly high plateau, 1 ,500 to 3,000 meters above sea level
and dissected by gorges and broad valleys; this plateau is surrounded by
extensive lowlands, most of which are inhabited only by nomadic pastoralists.
The country is divided into six basic ecological regions which vary consider-
ably with regard to topography, climate, soils, natural vegetation and agri-
cultural potential. While no detailed land use data is available, it is
estimated that about 50% of the land is permanent pasture, 9% is cultivated,
21% is barren desert or swamps, 10% rivers and lakes and 9% is forests. The
country's extremely rugged terrain is a serious obstacle to internal trans-
portation, communication and economic development.
2.02      The total population, estimated at 26.5 million (in mid-1973), is
growing at an annual rate of about 2.4%. Total monetary and non-monetary
Gross Domestic Product (GDP), estimated at US$2.1 billion in 1973, is growing
at about 4.3% per year. Over 90% of the people live in rural areas, and
overall per capita income is estimated at US$80.
The Agricultural Sector
2.03      The agricultural sector is distinguished by wide climatic and
ecological diversity which allows a large variety of crops to be grown. The
peasant farmers possess a high level of traditional agricultural skill, but
they lack an efficient marketing system, access roads, security of tenure,
credit, extension and veterinary services and improved seeds, as well as
fertilizer and other farming inputs. Although 8 of every 10 Ethiopians are
peasant farmers, agriculture contributed only about 52% of GDP in 1972.
Average total annual family output (at farm prices) is less than Eth$ 300
(US$140), and 75% of this represents subsistence consumption. The total
value of agricultural output in 1972 was about Eth$ 2,200 million; cereals,
coffee and other crops accounted for 71% and livestock and livestock products
for the remaining 29%.
2.04      A serious three-year drought culminated in widespread famine in many
areas of the country by 1973. To date, it has not been possible to quantify
the economic impact of this disaster, but the effects are unquestionably
grave. With adequate rainfall and a maximum effort, it will take perhaps as
long as three to five years for agriculture to reach pre-drought levels of
production. The proposed massive redistribution of land and the ensuing
fundamental changes in the production and power structures in the rural areas
are expected to have in the short term a detrimental effect on agricultural
production.



- 3 -
B.  The Livestock Subsector (Aniex 1)
2.05      Prior to the drought, Ethiopia's vast livestock resources (the
largest of any country in Africa) included an estimated 1/ 27 million cattle,
24 million sheep, 18 million goats, 7 million horses, mules and donkeys, and
1 million camels. The true dimensions of the losses attributable to the
drought are not yet certain, but it is estimated that in the Northeast Range-
lands - which was the most severely affected area - some 80% of the cattle,
50% of the sheep, and 30% each of the nomadic camels and goats died over a
three-year period. It is also estimated, however, that other things being
equal, the herds should regenerate themselves over a 7-10 year period.
2.06      Given the large domestic herds and the fact that over 50% of the
country's land area is devoted to livestock production, the subsector makes
a disproportionately small contribution to national production and to export
earnings. In 1972, livestock products contributed about 29% of total agri-
cultural output (beef 10%, other meat 5%, milk 8%, hides and skins 1% and
others 5%). It is estimated that the total official 2/ exports of livestock
and livestock products from Ethiopia was about Eth$ 23.3 million in 1972,
an increase of about 300% from 1968. In spite of this increase, however,
the total quantity and value of exports were relatively low in relation
to the existing potential. The major constraints to increased output are
endemic diseases and an underdeveloped internal marketing system which
depresses the prices paid to the producer; in addition, uncontrolled grazing
and stock breeding have denuded vast areas of rangeland. Another constraint
is that cattle are traditionally viewed as a source of prestige and a hedge
against future uncertainty rather than as a potential source of cash income;
the nomadic owners in particular are reluctant to sell an animal unless it
is absolutely necessary.
Rangeland Areas
2.07      The Ethiopian rangelands cover some 500,000 km2, or about 42%
of the country's total land area. While no accurate figures are available,
it is estimated that they contain a human population of 2.5 million, 5.5
million cattle (21% of the country's total), 4.5 million sheep (18%), 2.5
million goats (15%), and 1 million camels (100%). In addition to providing
subsistence for the largely nomadic population, the rangelands provide the
(highland) cultivated areas with livestock and livestock products, and they
are presently the only areas with a net surplus of immature cattle for finish-
ing for domestic consumption or for export. The carrying capacity of the
ranges is severely limited by the lack of adequate water facilities and by
1/   Livestock and Meat Board, 1974.
2/   In addition to the livestock exported through recognized trade channels,
a substantial number also cross the borders into Somalia and Kenya with-
out going through customs or paying official export taxes.



inefficient utilization of existing vegetation; consequently, the large in-
creases in both the human and livestock populations over the past five decades
have contributed to a significant deterioration in the condition of the grazing
areas. This situation has been aggravated by the recent drought, and by the
increasing encroachment of settled farming in the most productive dry season
pastures. The land reform proclamation recently issued by Government specifi-
cally recognized the traditional rights of the nomadic pastoralist over the
ranges. The future of these areas will, however, depend most directly on the
ability of the livestock owners to limit the number of their animals and to
institute a voluntary and comprehensive system of range use controls.
2.08      The ranges are informally divided into a number of grazing reserves,
each controlled by a particular tribe; tribal agreements regulate the seasonal
migrations of the nomadic livestock owners as they travel with their herds in
search of water and grazing. The incomes of these nomads are low as compared
with the national average. The annual per capita income in these areas is
probably about Eth$ 60 (US$29).
2.09      The composition of the livestock herds in different range areas
within Ethiopia will vary with the ecology of the region and the traditions
of the nomadic people living there (see Annex 19, p. 3). In the Southern
Rangelands, cattle comprise the greater proportion of the nomadic herds, and
they are valued highly for both milk and meat production. Cows culled
for age are sold for slaughter, and male cattle are sold mainly to finance
major purchases such as food and clothes. In Jijigga where lower annual
rainfall produces less favorable range conditions, cattle (although important)
are greatly outnumbered by sheep and goats. The latter are kept in separate
flocks looked after by the women; their milk comprises an important part of
the family diet, and a limited number are sold to satisfy immediate cash
needs. Camels are mainly used for transport, but they are more important in
the drier areas of the lower Ogaden. In the Northeast Rangelands area which
is characterized by extremely arid semi-desert conditions, camels are
probably the most important type of livestock, as they are used for trans-
port and traded for use in the Danakil salt trade; although larger in numbers,
sheep and goats are somewhat less important to the nomadic economy. Very
few cattle are found in the driest parts of the region.
Livestock Marketing
2.10      The existing livestock markets in Ethiopia are for the most part
poorly developed.  With few exceptions they usually comprise an open place
with no facilities, situated in or close to a town or village to which live-
stock are brought in small lots at frequent intervals (often daily) for
sale. Animals are normally traded by direct and time-consuming bargaining
with limited public awareness of actual price levels. In the smaller markets
the buyers, apart from a few local butchers, are usually small livestock
traders sometimes purchasing on behalf of bigger traders and sometimes purchas-
ing with the sole objective of re-trading the stock in another market further
down the stockroute. In a few markets, auctioneers have established them-
selves and operate on a tribal basis, but this method of selling is not widely



- 5 -
practiced. Direct bargaining is also the rule in the bigger markets near
consumer centers where the buyers are predominantly butchers and butchers'
agents. Municipalities throughout the country charge a fee for the use of
the market, commonly Eth$ 2.00 per head for cattle and camels and Eth$ 0.30
for sheep and goats, but only a proportion of the fees are actually collected.
Sales of slaughter stock to the meat factories are sometimes made direct by
the producer, but the more usual system is for the factory to support a few
major traders who themselves buy at the bigger markets and have arrangements
with smaller traders and agents at smaller markets.
2.11      There is at present an urgent need both to stimulate the offtake
from the national herd and to channel a greater percentage of the trade
through Ethiopian channels (para 2.06). Progress will depend on the rate
at which organized marketing facilities are introduced, and on the ability
of the various extension services to bring about a fundamental change in the
traditional nomadic approach to livestock production. These problems were
given initial attention under the IBRD Second Livestock Development Project
initiated in 1973. This project provided an integrated market and stock-
route system in three important livestock producing areas of the country;
it also included provision for the construction of slaughter facilities,
cattle transport and technical and administrative assistance. In the range
areas the improved facilities are expected to lower marketing costs, improve
competition and ultimately result in higher prices for local producers. The
project is also expected to have the added benefit of increasing the offtake
from the national herd, thereby reducing the threats of over-grazing and
insufficient water supplies. Construction of project infrastructure is only
now beginning to get underway, but it is expected that these facilities would
be capable of handling the expected increased offtake from the proposed Project.
C. Government Livestock Services
2.12      Several Government agencies provide a range of services to the
livestock industry; these include the Livestock and Meat Board (LMB), the
Ministry of Agriculture and the Dairy Development Agency. On the whole,
the level of services is far short of the industry's overall requirements,
and other services, e.g. extension services, are very restricted. However,
through a reorganization of LMB and a consolidation of various Government
programs, Government is now making a substantial effort to improve the
organization and effectiveness of its services. The major constraint in
this task are a lack of trained Ethiopian staff and the necessity of allocat-
ing limited financial and manpower resources among the various programs.
2.13      Livestock and Meat Board (Annex 2).  The Livestock and Meat Board
(LMB) is the institution primarily responsible for the development of the
livestock industry. It was established in 1964 as an independent body to
coordinate policy matters relating to livestock, poultry, meat, eggs, and
by-products including hides and skins. The overall policy of LMB is
determined by a Board of Directors chaired by the director of the Depart-
ment of Animal Resources in the Ministry of Agriculture and the
day-to-day operations are the responsibility of the General Manager.



-6-
LMB was given extremely wide powers under its enabling legislation, but
thus far it has mainly been concerned with the licensing of exports, produc-
tion of general monitoring and background studies on the livestock industry,
development of a feed mix plant and of a poultry industry. In addition,
LMB has had primary responsibility for execution of the Second Livestock
Project. Recently, LMB has been given a broad range of additional responsi-
bilities, including that of supervising 10 recently nationalized abattoirs.
Also, in order to concentrate all livestock production activities within one
agency, the Livestock Production Division of the Ministry of Agriculture was
transferred to LMB's control. Government is presently considering a plan
for the reorganization of LMB which would consolidate the existing lines of
responsibility and thus would better enable it to carry out its designated
tasks.
2.14      Ministry of Agriculture.  Overall responsibility for animal health
services is vested in the Department of Veterinary Services within the Ministry
of Agriculture. The veterinary staff now comprises 45 Ethiopian veterinarians,
40 expatriate veterinarians supplied under technical assistance, 250 Animal
Health Assistants and 700 trained vaccinators.  These staff are, however,
insufficient to give satisfactory veterinary cover throughout the country,
and consequently the services supplied in many areas are at best rudimentary.
The effectiveness of the staff is further hampered by lack of sufficient
vehicles, veterinary supplies and other materials. In general, the efficiency
of Ministry staff is also reduced due to the fact that a disproportionate
number of trained staff are presently located in Addis Ababa and other urban
centers.
III. PROJECT AREA
A. Southern Rangelands
3.01      The Southern Rangelands cover a gross area of some 160,000 kmi
stretching over all or parts of Sidamo, Gemu-Gofa, Bale and Harar Administrative
Regions; the bulk of the Project investments would, however, be concentrated
in an area of about 95,000 km2 on the Borana plateau in Sidamo. There are
some 480,000 persons now living in the area, more than 350,000 of which are
nomadic livestock owners. The amount of cultivated area is relatively small
and most of this is concentrated in a strip along the mountains in the north
of the area. The only town of any size is Neghelli (total population about
8,000), and the other main settlements are Yabello, Mega and Teltalle. The
road network throughout the area is uneven and many parts are completely cut
off during the rainy season. The mean annual rainfall varies from 550 to
650 mm in some parts of the range to around 440 mm per annum in other areas.
3.02      The range area is divided into a number of grazing areas dominated
by a particular tribe; tribal agreements regulate the seasonal migration of



- 7 -
the nomadic herdsmen from the wet to the dry season grazing areas and from
one zone of influence to another. The main tribes established in the area
are the Borana, Gujji, Arrussi, the Somali and the lower Gemu-Gofa pastoralists.
These tribes rely almost exclusively on livestock for subsistence. There are
now approximately 1.0 million cattle, 300,000 sheep and goats, and 90,000
camels within the Project area. Most of the cattle are Borana zebus, a breed
with the best potential of all Ethiopian cattle. The carrying capacity of the
ranges is severely limited by the lack of adequate water facilities, and by
poor range management practices; due to the uneven water supplies, some areas
are very much overgrazed during the dry season, while other vast regions are
under-grazed or only partially grazed.
B. Jijigga
3.03      The Jijigga Project area covers approximately 33,000 km2 in the
Harar Administrative Region on the eastern edge of Ethiopia; this includes
the whole of Jijigga and parts of Gursum and Dagabar Administrations. The
largest town is Jijigga, with a permanent population of about 25,000. The
main all-weather road from Harar through Jijigga to Dagabur runs through the
area, and connects it with the railway and air services at Dire Dawa; there
are also a number of other motorable tracks. The mean annual rainfall in the
area ranges from 600-700 mm down to 320 mm or lower.  The rainfall pattern
is bimodal. The area's total population is estimated at between 300,000 and
500,000 persons, the majority of whom are semi-nomadic pastoralists. There
are in addition about 22,000 semi-settled farmers with a total of about
300,000 ha under crops; these are mostly small farmers (less than 2-3 ha),
although there were about 150 large mechanized farmers (over 40 ha) operating
near Jijigga. The main crop grown is sorghum, and the large farmers also
grow some wheat and maize.
3.04      The vast majority of the nomads living in this area are of Somali
origin, belonging to thirteen major clans and some minor ones. While each
pastoral clan tends to have its own "territory", they migrate with their
livestock herds over long distances in search of water and grazing, using
the territories of other clans according to a system of contracts and agree-
ments. The number of livestock located within the project area fluctuates
drastically from season to season. Aerial surveys estimate that the number
of cattle ranges from 400,000 in the dry season to almost 700,000 in the wet
season; likewise the number of sheep ranges from 500,000 to 900,000 and goats
from 150,000 to 450,000. Range studies indicate that 84% of the grazing
areas show signs of over-use, and there is some evidence that the overall
condition of the range is deteriorating. The traditional range use pattern
has been tending to break down both because of the increase in cultivation
and settlement around the permanent water points, and because the introduction
of concrete water tanks has greatly prolonged the grazing period in some
border areas.



C. Northeast Rangelands
3.05      The Northeast Rangelands Project would be located in a 75,000 km2
area in the northeast of Ethiopia and would include parts of both Wollo and
Tigre Administrative Regions. The area is characterized by low rainfall, is
largely inaccessible and has few external communications. The only main road
runs from Bati to Assab, and the few settlements of any size are located along
this route. Limited if any marketing, veterinary and other services are
available to the pastoralists. Apart from the very western edge of the area
in the foothills where attempts have been made at dry land cotton cultivation,
irrigation is required for successful cultivation. Profiting by the example
of the successful commercial irrigation program in the Awash Valley, some of
the Afars have settled there and are cultivating irrigated crops.
3.06      There are an estimated 30,000 families living in the area.  These
pastoralists are for the most part Afars with closely related social struc-
tures and a long nomadic tradition. The hardships brought about by the recent
drought seem to have given the Afars a new perspective, and they appear to
be increasingly willing to consider alternative livelihoods such as farming
and manual labor. Also inter-tribal hostilities have resulted in the serious
disruption of the traditional economy, and they are now for the most part
deprived of their highland grazing areas as well as the traditional livestock
markets located there. As a result of the drought the number of livestock
has dropped substantially. The number of cattle in a surveyed portion of the
area fell from 355,000 head in September 1972 to 65,000 head in January 1974;
sheep and goats declined less dramatically from 585,000 to 362,000, and camels
from 100,000 to 64,000. Although the livestock economy is gradually being
restored, the marketed offtake will remain low for the next few years.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
4.01      The Project would over a five year period provide for the develop-
ment and rehabilitation of three lowland range areas inhabited mostly by
nomadic pastoralists engaged in extensive livestock production. The Project
would consist of three separate and distinct sub-projects, each with its
own organizational structure. These sub-projects - The Southern Rangelands,
Jijigga and Northeast Rangelands Development Projects - would each provide
an integrated program of range management and veterinary services along with
improved roads and water facilities. They would also aid in the stratifi-
cation of the Ethiopian livestock industry, and help lay the foundation for
the sound future development of the livestock economy in the range areas.
Specifically the Project would provide for:



(a) the institution of land planning and range management
programs in conjunction with a program for the gradual
control of livestock numbers;
(b) the establishment of a basic veterinary and livestock
extension service designed to reach the nomadic pasto-
ralists;
(c) construction of water facilities including both permanent
and rainy season water ponds as well as a small number of
boreholes and concrete water tanks; a limited water spreading
program would be carried out in the Northeast Rangelands
area;
(d) construction of trade roads and administrative access
tracks;
(e) the development of backgrounding and fattening ranches and
a feedlot (Southern Rangelands only) and smallholder
fattening schemes;
(f) a program of trials and studies with emphasis on improved
range use and grazing control practices;
(g) training of Project staff, livestock owners and tribal
leaders; and
(h) provision of supplemental staff within LMB in order to
improve its overall efficiency and to enable it to provide
technical and administrative services to the three head-
quarters units.
Overall direction of the Project would be the responsibility of the Livestock
and Meat Board. Each of the three sub-projects would have a separate organiza-
tional unit based at a headquarters within the individual project areas.
These units - The Southern Rangelands Development Unit (SORDU), The Jijigga
Rangelands Development Unit (JIRDU), and the Northeast Rangelands Development
Unit (NERDU) - would each be under the direction of a Project Manager directly
responsible to the head of LMB's Rangeland Development Department.
4.02      In the short term the Project would attempt to restructure the
traditional system of extensive livestock production in the Project areas,
thus raising the value of marketed livestock production and resulting in
increased incomes for the nomadic producers. One of the primary goals would
be the eventual establishment of a comprehensive system of range use control
under which the nomads would voluntarily adjust their overall stock numbers
to meet the carrying capacity of the ranges. This would in turn help pre-
vent the now ever-recurring cycle of a gradual build-up of herds, over-
stocking, and consequent heavy losses in the face of the first sustained
drought. In the long term, however, the Project would seek to provide a basis



- 10 -
for a fundamental change in the system prevalent in these areas whereby indi-
vidually owned livestock graze lands traditionally allocated to the tribal
community as a whole. Project services would be directed to all types of
livestock. In the Southern Rangelands area where cattle are most important
(para. 2.09), the main veterinary effort would be directed at Rinderpest.
The ranches, feedlot and smallholder fattening programs would be introduced
to support the further stratification of the Ethiopian beef cattle industry.
In Jijigga where sheep and goats predominate, the veterinary effort would be
directed at tick-borne diseases (such as Babesiosis) and internal parasites.
Although the main emphasis in the smallholder fattening programs would ini-
tially be directed toward cattle, sheep would be included after practical
trials for the fattening of sheep were carried out in both the Jijigga high-
land area and on the Northeast Rangelands water spreading dispersion areas
(para. 4.14). Any improvement in sheep husbandry would show substantial
benefits as the value of the meat is substantially higher than that of beef.
In the Northeast Rangelands area where there is a high proportion of camels,
the main veterinary effort would be directed at Trypanosomiasis; little is
known of other aspects of camel husbandry, but it is expected that the improve-
ment of the ranges and the collection of data by the Project would help
alleviate this gap.
4.03      As all of the three areas differ markedly with regard to geographic
and climatic conditions and are inhabited by peoples with widely varying
languages and tribal customs, the individual sub-projects would necessarily
adopt a slightly different strategy in each region. The overall objective
would in each case remain the same, however, and in all three areas a
concerted effort would be made to actively involve the nomads themselves
in the planning and execution of Project activities. In any case, no
development activities would be undertaken without the full cooperation
and approval of the tribes affected.
B. Detailed Features
Range Management (Annex 3)
4.04      A central element of the overall Project strategy would be the
development of a comprehensive range management program for each of the
three Project areas. The ultimate objective of this program would be the
gradual transformation of the traditional nomadic livestock economy where
the animals are largely held as a source of subsistence and of prestige and
as a hedge against future uncertainty. A Range Management Division would
be established within each of the three sub-projects to formulate and coor-
dinate the strategy in each area. It would be initially responsible for the
development of both an overall land use plan and a detailed range management
plan specifically adapted to conditions in that area. Since this would
involve changes in the living patterns and traditions of the nomads as well
as technical changes in animal husbandry methods, it is recognized that the



- 11 -
program must be introduced gradually and only with the full cooperation of
the tribal authorities; although the objectives will only be realized over
a much longer period, it is expected that during the Project period the
initial impetus for change can be developed.
4.05      Although the range management programs for each of the three areas
would necessarily be somewhat different, the basic thrust and objectives
of each program would be much the same. Particular attention would be given
to control of livestock numbers, the limitation of burning and tree cutting,
and to the introduction of controlled grazing and closure of pastures. These
activities would be most carefully integrated with the veterinary and the
water development programs (paras 4.09 - 4.14), and indeed the successful
inter-relation of these would be crucial to the success of the Project.
4.06      Southern Rangelands.  The range management program in the Southern
Rangelands Project area would center around the establishment of the four
backgrounding and fattening ranches (para. 4.15) where improved range manage-
ment techniques would be introduced in relatively concentrated areas; it is
hoped that once the success of these practices has been demonstrated on the
ranches, they would then be adopted for extensive use by the nomads them-
selves. Agreement has not yet been obtained for setting aside dry season
grazing reserves, for although certain groups. more or less control specific
grazing areas, they are also utilized by other groups (particularly in times
of drought); these agreements would be negotiated during the course of the
Project. The SORDU Range Management Division would coordinate the activities
of the range extension agents, the water scouts, and the operation of the
four ranches; this coordination would ensure that all the activities directly
affecting the status of the range areas would be fully integrated.
4.07      Jijigga.  The range management program to be initiated in the
Jijigga area would be somewhat more intricate than that being implemented in
the Southern Rangelands. This would be possible as the Somali tribes in the
area are relatively sophisticated livestock keepers who are able to exercise
a relatively high degree of control over range use. Therefore, a somewhat
different approach is called for. In the first year a land use plan for the
Jijigga area would be drawn up and the land zoned into areas suitable for
cultivation, rangeland, fodder reserves and for settlement. 1/ Within the
area to be gazetted as natural range, dry season grazing reserves would be
established based on both existing and new permanent watering points; these
reserves would be selected by the tribal groups in collaboration with JIRDU,
and agreements to this effect have already been signed. In principle these
reserves would be closed to all livestock except during the dry season, and
the grazing restrictions would be enforced by range guards selected from
1/   While this system can be enforced for the most part under existing
legislation, some supplemental legislation may also be necessary.



- 12 -
among the tribes. As part of an effort to involve the individual clans in
range development, a system of range associations of stockowners would be
established; the recommended range policies would be discussed with these
groups and they would be made increasingly responsible for the overall devel-
opment. Finally two pilot fodder banks would be established to serve as a
fodder reserve and to test the efficacy of selected planting materials.
These activities would be carried out by the JIRDU Range Management Division.
4.08      Northeast Rangelands.  The Northeast Rangelands area is only now
emerging from the recent drought which brought about widespread famine and
a drastic reduction in total livestock numbers. The drought also severely
damaged the rangelands themselves; large areas are bare of any vegetation,
and other areas are only now gradually beginning to recover with the aid
last year's rains. With the large reduction in the total stock numbers,
the ranges are now able - even with their greatly reduced carrying capacity -
to support the existing herds without undue pressure. This situation will
not prevail for long, however, as it is estimated that within 10 years the
herds will once again have been built up to their former levels. Consequently,
unless some controls are achieved within this time, the ranges will once
again be over-stocked. This area thus offers a unique opportunity to
institute improved range management practices at a time when the immediate
control of numbers is not the most important consideration. As part of this
strategy, an inventory of each tribal grazing area would be made to serve as
a guide in the selection of range reserves. At full development some 15 dry
season grazing reserves totalling about 8,000 km2 would be established. All
decisions with regard to the opening and closing of these reserves would be
made by tribal authorities in cooperation with the Project Range Management
Division. Eight fodder banks averaging 10 ha in size to be used as fodder
reserves in time of drought would also be established. Finally, the NERDU
Range Management Division would carry out various studies on the use of
enclosures, vegetation, the development of fodder reserves, etc.
Veterinary Services (Annex 4)
4.09      Animal diseases represent a major constraint to the development of
the Ethiopian livestock industry, and (except for the ongoing JP-15 campaign)
the limited veterinary services now available have had almost no success
in reducing herd losses and controlling the spread of outbreaks. A more com-
prehensive Project veterinary extension service would therefore be established
in each of the three Project areas in order to reduce livestock losses
attributable to disease and to improve the condition of those animals
marketed from the ranges. The level of services would be fairly intensive,
for in view of the high inter-dependence of the various Project components,
the provision of adequate veterinary cover is essential to the long-term
success of the Project. The staff would be organized in such a way as to
ensure the active participation and cooperation of the existing veterinary
authorities, the tribal leaders, and the nomadic stockowners.
4.10      The veterinary staff in each area would be headed by a Senior
Veterinarian assisted by a Junior Veterinarian (in two of the three areas),
Animal Health Assistants (AHA's), vaccinators, and veterinary scouts. Field



- 13 -
stations under the direction of an AHA would be established in up to 6
locations in each area. These field stations would be equipped with mobile
sheep dips, hand sprays, scales, veterinary drugs and other equipment. A
diagnostic unit also under an ABA would be set up in the Southern Rangelands
and Jijigga areas. 1/ The AHA's and vaccinators would work with the veteri-
nary scouts in carrying out not only regular veterinary services but also the
training of the local livestock owners in herd and range management tech-
niques; the ratio of AHA's to veterinary scouts would be around 1:20, with
one scout to 400 families. The veterinary scouts would be chosen from among
the pastoralists and would live and work with them, reporting periodically
to the field stations for instructions and supplies. They would receive
special training in both animal health care and range management and other
extension activities at the Project training centers (para 4.24). They would
provide instruction on the value of modern but simple methods of disease
control, and would provide drugs and chemicals for sale to the livestock
owners. The primary objectives of the veterinary program would therefore
be to develop the interest and capability on the part of the stockowners
themselves to institute both better disease control and improved livestock
management practices and to introduce range use controls adapted to their
own needs and traditions.
Water Development (Annex 5)
4.11      Three types of water development would be provided for under the
Project: boreholes, permanent water ponds with a capacity of 60,000 m3,
and rainy season surface catchment ponds with a capacity of about 7,500 m3.
The boreholes would be provided on the ranches and on grazing areas where
surface water catchment is unsuitable due to high soil permeability.
Because of the relatively high cost of drilling and pumping these would be
kept to a minimum. The large water ponds hold water throughout the dry
season and are important in opening up new areas for dry weather grazing.
The smaller ponds hold sufficient water to prolong the wet weather grazing
for one or two months into the dry season before the stock must move on;
these ponds are grouped around the larger ones in a ratio of 10:1 to
facilitate grazing control. The water programs in each area would vary
considerably, as both the present levels of infrastructure and the future
needs are quite different. No specific water development program would be
undertaken by the Project in the Northeast Rangelands area, as in those areas
where sufficient grazing is available, there are enough wells and other
water points available to meet current livestock needs. The Government water
authority is, however, instituting a tube well boring program to provide
water for human consumption in the few existing population centers. The
National Water Resources Commission has agreed that once the Project Units
are established in all three areas, they would be appointed by Government as
the water development authority for the entire area, and that all water devel-
opment within their sphere of influence would be coordinated by them.   Assur-
ances to this effect were obtained at negotiations.
1/  There is an adequately equipped unit already available in the Northeast
Rangelands area.



- 14 -
4.12      Southern Rangelands.  It is presently estimated that about one-third
of the range is under utilized due to lack of stock water, and the water
provided in conjunction with the Project would therefore be designed to in-
crease the carrying capacity of this area. The water facilities would include
the construction of some 15 large water ponds and upgrading or improvement
of some 220 smaller rainy season ponds. The water development would be closely
integrated with the range management program in order to prevent over-grazing
of hitherto protected areas; a further safeguard would exist by virtue of the
fact that the number of water points being provided is only about one-third
of the number required for full development. An additional 10 large water
ponds initially included within the Project are now under construction by a
Ministry of Agriculture program supported by USAID.
4.13      Jijigga.  Under the Project permanent water supplies would be
established in both the cultivated areas (to provide water for the smallholder
stock feeder program, para 4.21) and in the dry season grazing areas (sub-
ject to agreement on the boundaries of the dry season grazing reserves); it
is also proposed that a few additional water supplies be located outside the
dry season grazing reserves to protect them from an over-use in times of
inadequate rainfall. As part of this program the Project would take over
and re-equip four existing Government boreholes at Aware, Coralei, El Amhar
and Lafaissa. Up to 75 shallow wells would be dug in cultivated areas near
the farms involved in the stocker feeder program. Four boreholes, two birkas
(concrete-lined water tanks) and 11 shallow wells would be provided in the
grazing reserves. The water program would be administered by the JIRDU Range
Management Division; construction would be carried out by the JIRDU Technical
Division, and in the case of the boreholes by contract.
Irrigation/Water Spreading (Annex 6)
4.14      Water spreading for simple irrigation would be carried out within
the Northeast Rangelands Project area on the basis of experience gained from
similar activities supported under the IDA Drought Areas Rehabilitation
Project. The program would involve the diversion of flood water under gravity
to bunded plots of land; the irrigated sites would range from 5 to 500 ha
in size and would average about 100 ha. These would be divided into one
hectare plots, each allocated to a different farmer. It is expected that
up to 2,000 Afar farmers would be allocated individual plots by the end of
the Project period. The main crops produced would be cotton, maize, sorghum
and groundnuts. Livestock would be grown out or fattened on the surplus
water dispersion areas of the irrigation sites (para 4.22). In addition
to the water spreading program, provision would also be made for general
hydrological studies and for two feasibility studies; the latter would include
an investigation of the possibilities of irrigated crop development in the
Teiru Depression.
Ranches (Annex 7)
4.15      Under the Project provision would be made for the establishment of
up to four 25,000 ha fattening and backgrounding ranches in the central part



- 15 -
of the Southern Rangelands area. These ranches would provide an assured out-
let for the surplus stock from the surrounding range areas, and would represent
a first step in the stratification of the Ethiopian livestock industry. By
offering an even flow of animals both for possible fattening by the feedlot
(para 4.17) and by smallholders in the Awassa area (para 4.20), and for sale
on local markets, they would be able to offer higher prices to local livestock
owners. These higher prices would provide a strong incentive for the nomads
to increase the present low offtake from the ranges, and would also result
in a significant increase in the cash incomes of one of the poorest segment
of the Ethiopian population.
4.16      Three of the ranch sites have been identified thus far (Did Hara,
Dedertu, and Kajima), and the site of the fourth would be finalized at a
later date; agreements have been reached with the nomadic tribes having
traditional grazing rights over the ranch areas. The ranches would purchase
young male cattle from the pastoralists in the surrounding area; a first
payment equal to the current market price plus a small bonus would be made
at the time of the sale, and a second payment would be made after the dis-
position of the animal by the ranch. On average each animal would spend one
year on the ranch, with an average weight gain of 100 kg, thus bringing
the animal to a final weight of approximately 250 kg. At full development
(in year 5) a total of about 16,000 head would be handled by the four ranches
each year; about 8,000 of these would be channelled through the feedlot
operation, and the remainder would be either fattened by smallholders in the
Awassa area, or grazed on stover on the AAAISC farm. A limited number of older
animals would be sold on local markets or - if this should prove feasible -
exported through Assab. Investments on the ranches would include provision
for offices, housing, stores, fencing, farm tracks and access roads, stock-
ponds, vehicles and equipment. A Ranch Manager under the general supervision
of the Head of the Range Management Division would be responsible for the
operation of each ranch. The development program has been made intentionally
flexible so that if it is feasible to develop four separate ranches during
the development period this could be done; if, however, the rate of development
were slower than expected, the money allocated for the fourth ranch could be
transferred to other operations.
Feedlot (Annex 8)
4.17      A feedlot unit would be established at the Awassa Agro-Industrial
Share Company (AAISC) farm in the Southern Rangelands area. The feedlot
would be designed to add value and weight to immature cattle through an
intensive feeding regime. It would thus assist in the stratification of the
Ethiopian livestock industry by providing a regular supply of high value
meat for both export and local markets. This would in turn result in higher
prices through the establishment of new outlet for quality meat. These
higher prices would eventually accrue to the nomadic cattle owners, as up
to 75% of the profits of the feedlot would be returned to the producers
in the form of improved prices or as bonuses on cattle already sold. The
feedlot would also serve as a pilot operation to provide basic data on the
feasibility of establishing similar operations elsewhere in Ethiopia.



- 16 -
4.18      The AAISC farm (now owned by Government) would lease the land for
the feedlot to the Project and would provide a number of administrative and
other services to the feedlot operation such as the growing of forage and
supply of feed grains.  The farm would also undertake construction of the
unit to specifications provided by the Feedlot Manager.  One unit with a
capacity of about 2,700 animals at any one time would be constructed initially;
each animal would spend about 16 weeks in the feedlot, giving an annual
throughput of about 8,000 animals. 1/  The main investments in the feedlot
would be for water, fencing, levelling and drainage, troughs, an access
road and trench silos; some silage making machinery would also be provided
for the AAISC farm. Cattle for the feedlot would be purchased mainly from
the ranches, although other sources of supply would not be precluded. The
feedlot would produce between 150 and 180 head of cattle per week. A large
proportion of these would be slaughtered either for sale on the Addis Ababa
market or for export to Arab countries; the remainder would be exported
live through Assab.
Smallholder Fattening (Annex 9)
4.19      Smallholder fattening program would be undertaken in each of the
three Project areas whereby livestock would be sold on credit to smallholders
for fattening on crop residues and other fodder. This system of low cost
growth is variously known as stocker feeding, backgrounding or the production
of store animals. Each program would be tailored to meet the specific
conditions in that area. The Southern Rangelands program, for example, would
(along with the ranches and the feedlot) be an integrated part of a clas-
sically stratified livestock industry. In the Jijigga area the program would
supplement and expand an existing fattening industry where work oxen are used
for 2 to 3 years on a farm and then fattened for a year before sale on crop
residues and produced fodders. In the Northeast Rangelands area, credit would
be provided to families now without any stock to purchase animals for fat-
tening on the ranges.
4.20      Southern Rangelands.  At full development up to 2,000 cattle from
the ranches would be shipped to the Awassa area where they would be sold on
credit to smallholders for fattening on crop residues. About 2,000 addition-
al animals would be fed on low value sorghum stover on the MAISC farm. The
entire fattening operation has, however, been made flexible so that the number
of animals sold by the ranches for fattening either by the feedlot, the
smallholders or on the AAISC farm could be easily adjusted in view of the
number and quality of animals available (Annex 10, Table 2).
4.21      Jijigga.  A stocker feeder program involving about 4,500 small
farmers would be established in the Jijigga area. Each of the participating
1/   With a minimum of additional investment the feedlot could later be
expanded to allow for an annual throughout of 12,000 head if this
should prove desirable.



- 17 -
farmers would purchase two animals on credit from JIRDU for fattening on
sorghum stover or other crop residues, giving an annual throughout of 9,000
animals per year (Annex 10, Table 1). The cattle would initially be purchased
by private traders under contract to JIRDU.
4.22      Northeast Rangelands.  A livestock marketing program would be
introduced under the Project to stimulate the volume of marketed offtake and
to reduce the irregularity of supply that is presently a major constraint
to the proper functioning of existing markets. Eight markets recently closed
by tribal hostilities would be reopened and provided with perimeter fencing;
a Project Marketing Unit (PMU) would be responsible for the initial function-
ing of these markets. The PMU would also set up a pilot grazier scheme where-
by it would purchase young male animals, treat them for disease and then sell
them on credit to pastoralists for feeding over one year. The stockowners
would be required to resell the animals to the PMU, and they would be paid a
guaranteed minimum price per kg liveweight. It is also expected that sheep
would be fattened using the surplus water dispersion areas of the irrigation
sites (para 4.14).  About 100 pastoralists fattening an average of 10 live-
stock units each would participate in the first three years of the program;
by year 5 the total throughput of the program would be about 3,000 livestock
units (Annex 10, Table 1).
Roads (Annex 11)
4.23      Ethiopia's transport facilities are poorly developed, with many
areas of the country almost completely isolated from the main marketing and
administrative centers. The existing road network in the nomadic rangelands
is insufficient to meet both the specific needs of the Project and to sustain
the long term development of the area. Two immediate requirements are
apparent:
(a) the creation of trade roads to open up the area for
administrative and trade purposes; and
(b) the establishment of dry season access tracks to allow
access for veterinary, extension and other Project Staff
and to facilitate communications on the ranges.
The trade roads would be built to take a 6-10 ton two-wheel drive lorry in
dry weather, and access tracks built to take a four-wheel drive vehicle in
reasonable weather. Both types of roads would be built by labor intensive
methods, although a limited amount of machinery would be provided for the
grading of roads in particularly difficult terrain. The road program,
which would be the responsibility of the three Project implementation units
(para. 4.27), is detailed below:



- 18 -
Trade        Access
Roads        Tracks
------- km
Southern Rangelands              540           460
Jijigga                          765           740
Northeast Rangelands             165           380
1,470        1,580
In addition to this construction and/or improvement program, provision would
also be made under the Northeast Rangelands Project for a limited amount of
communications equipment (mostly transceiver radios) to compensate for the
particularly poor communications facilities in that area.
Training (Annex 12)
4.24      A program would be set up in each of the Project areas to train
both local Project staff and to educate and integrate the various tribal
groups in the development process. For this purpose, training centers would
be established in each area under a Project Training Officer, and provision
would be made for a mobile training center. All senior Project staff would
be sent for short courses in their various fields, and a limited number of
fellowships (both in East Africa and abroad) would be made available. Tribal
elders would be brought to the training centers for periods of up to 5 days
to familiarize them with the objectives of the Project. In the Southern
Rangelands area additional activities would include the establishment of a
small training and demonstration herd of about 200 cattle at the training
center. In all three areas the training units would also prepare programs
for, among others, the regular broadcasting station at Harar and for a new
station to be established at Alemaya under the IBRD Fourth Education Project;
these would enable the stations to give short daily broadcasts on specific
items of stockowner interest. In the Northeast Rangelands area training
centers would be set up at two of the Project Development Centers (para 4.28).
Specialized training would be provided to give selected laborers the opportunity
to train as foremen and supervisors in irrigation and road construction programs.
Training in irrigated crop production would also be provided to the 2,000 farm-
ers to be included in the water spreading program (para 4.14).
Trials and Studies (Annex 13)
4.25      The Project would include provision for a number of technical and
socio-economic trials and studies both in order to provide answers to technical
problems and to assess the results achieved by the various Project components.
As this would be the first full-scale range development project carried out
in Ethiopia, it is important that the maximum amount of data be compiled so



- 19 -
that these experiences can prove a guide to the development of similar
projects either in Ethiopia or elsewhere in East Africa. Individual studies
would be carried out in each of the Project areas, and areas of investigation
would include: improved range management techniques, the integration of
arable farming with livestock production, use of enclosures, development of
fodder banks, and range ecology. Socio-economic studies would continue and
enlarge efforts already underway to estimate herd numbers and composition,
offtake, migration patterns of nomadic livestock owners, and changing tribal
structures and traditions. Project evaluation would be carried out by special
units within each of the three Project units.
Services and Supplies
4.26      The Project would provide for the distribution of a number of
essential goods and services through stores set up at the Project Development
Centers in the Northeast Rangelands area (para. 4.28). These would include
veterinary and medical supplies, agricultural inputs and some grain. All
of these would be sold for cash to the pastoralists in the area, although
a limited amount of credit would be made available for the purchase of agri-
cultural inputs. Once the communication and other market facilities within
the Northeast Rangelands area have been upgraded, it is expected that private
traders would take over this distribution function. A Supplies and Dis-
tribution Unit within NERDU's Finance and Administration Department would
supervise the operation of these stores.
Project Administration
4.27      The Project would be implemented by three separate units established
at separate project headquarters in each of the three Project Areas - the
Southern Rangeland Development Unit (SORDU), the Jijigga Rangelands Development
Unit (JIRDU) and the Northeast Rangelands Development Unit (NERDU). These
units would each be headed by a Project Manager responsible to the Head of
the LMB Rangeland Development Department. Additional Staff would include
Heads of the Finance and Administration and the Technical Services Divisions,
a Chief Accountant, a Procurement Officer, clerks, and other supporting
personnel. Provision would also be included for housing, vehicles and equip-
ment.
4.28      Although the headquarters units would be reasonably similar in
all three areas, special conditions prevailing in the Northeast Rangelands
would necessitate a certain amount of additional investment in this area.
There are very few permanent settlements in this region, the terrain is
difficult, and there is almost no communications network of any kind. In
order to help offset these difficulties, eight Project Development Centers
(PDC's) would be established at strategic points throughout the Project area.
These centers would facilitate contact with the pastoralists, and would
provide a focal point for the development activities. The facilities would
in each case include a supply store, grain and fuel storage, radio communica-
tions equipment, and staff accommodations.



- 20 -
LMB Headquarters Staff (Annex 14)
4.29      Additional staff would be provided under the Project to help enable
the Livestock and Meat Board (LMB) to successfully undertake overall respon-
sibility for the implementation of this Project. First, a Rangeland Develop-
ment Department would be established within LMB to coordinate the activities
of all three sub-projects, and to provide administrative and technical assist-
ance to them. Staff provided for this Department would include: an inter-
nationally recruited Department Head (who would serve as overall Project
Coordinator), a Personnel Officer, a Water Engineer, a Sociologist and a
Mass Media/Training Specialist. Secondly, LMB would also provide certain
other financial and procurement services to the three range projects; in
order to supply this capability and to improve the overall efficiency of LMB,
supplemental staff would be provided within the Finance Department. These
staff would include an internationally recruited Financial Controller, an
Internal Auditor, two Accountants, as well as a Senior Procurement Officer
and one Junior Procurement Officer; a limited amount of supporting staff
and some equipment and vehicles would also be provided. It was estimated
at appraisal that approximately 12 high level staff would be internationally
recruited (para 5.02), and these positions were costed accordingly in the
relevant tables. At negotiations it became clear that suitable local candidates
were available for a number of these positions; it was however agreed that there
was a need to compensate for the lack of administrative experience on the part
of otherwise qualified Ethiopian candidates. Therefore, two new posts were
created within the Rangelands Development Department - Technical Manager for
Project Administration and Technical Manager for Veterinary Services; these
staff would be internationally recruited and would be paid for by the Project.
Adequate savings would be available through the increased employment of local
staff costed at international rates. Funds would also be provided under the
Project for the construction of a second veterinary laboratory building at
Debre Zeit in order to allow this facility to expand its production of vaccine
as well as its diagnostic services. Finally, provision would be made for funds
to support the continued preparation of future projects within the livestock
sector by LMB. One third of the total cost of the entire component would be
attributed to each of the three sub-projects.
