Costs have been estimated at prices prevailing at the time of appraisal
plus estimated price escalations to December, 1975. A physical contingency
of 10% has been included. In addition price contingencies of 35% for local
and 29Z for foreign costs have been included to allow for expected increases
in foreign and local costs; these are equivalent on average to a cumulative
annual price contingency of about 10%. Price contingencies are based on the
estimated level of price increases for Project items in Ethiopia. Import
taxes and duties on Project goods are estimated at Eth$ 0.6 million (US$0.3
million) and have been included. The Project should commence well in that
retroactive financing has been provided for the recruitment of key staff and
other initial related expenditures; these staff are expected to be in post
by the time of the signing of the credit agreement. However, it is considered
that it will take about a further six months for most Project staff to be
recruited, for construction to commence and for goods to be received.
The Government believes that it can implement the Project more quickly as it
intends to give the Project a high priority; this would of course be very
desirable but a more prudent estimate of implementation has been used by the
mission.
D. Financing
4.31      The financing of Project costs would be shared in the following
amounts and proportions:
US$ million          %
IDA                         27.00             63
ADF                          5.50             13
Government                  10.50             24
Total            43.00             100%
The proposed IDA credit would be on standard terms to Government. This
would cover about US$11.1 million of the total foreign exchange costs
(US$14.0 million) and about 54% of local costs, or about 63% of total Project
costs. The proposed African Development Fund (ADF) contribution would total
5.0 million African Development Fund units of account (about US$5.5 million);
this would finance the remaining US$2.9 million of the Project's foreign
exchange costs and US$2.6 million of local costs. In order to ensure that
the total available ADF funds would be utilized, ADF would finance up to 76%
of the costs of the ranches, the feedlot and the smallholder fattening



- 24 -
program and up to 36% of the costs of the roads in the Southern Rangelands
sub-project. Together the ADF and the IDA contributions would finance 76%
of total Project costs. The remaining 24% would be contributed by Government;
this would be offset to the extent that Project costs include about US$0.3
million in taxes and duties. In addition to the costs shown above (para 4.30),
farmers participating in the mali1holder fattening programs would make a small
cash contribution for the fattening and purchase of each animal; these contri-
butions would total about US$0.40 million during the Project implementation
period. The ADF, IDA and Government contributions would be channelled
directly by Government to LMB, which would establish separate bank accounts
for each of the three sub-projects. It would be a condition of credit effect-
iveness that separate accounts for each of the three sub-projects had been
established and that a deposit of funds had been made therein to cover the
expenditures for the first quarter of the Project implementation period
starting January 1, 1976, such deposit to be made on the basis of a reasonable
estimate of the expenditures for such period. In order to facilitate this
initial estimate and to ensure that adequate funds are made available to
the sub-projects over the implementation period, LMB would prepare an annual
work plan and budget at the beginning of each fiscal year and such plans and
budgets would be forwarded to IDA and ADF for review and comment. The
Project would provide up to US$500,000 in retroactive financing to cover
Project expenditures for salaries and other administrative costs incurred
from July 1, 1975 to the date of the signing of the credit agreement. A
suggested list of items to be included in this retroactive financing is
given in Annex 16.
E. Procurement
4.32      Procurement of vehicles and road construction equipment (US$2,376,600)
would be subject to international competitive bidding according to IDA guide-
lines; orders would be bulked whenever possible. These orders would be exe-
cuted through LMB's Procurement Division acting as the agent for all three
projects. In order to facilitate the early operations of the Project, pro-
vision would be made for the purchase of up to fifteen vehicles (US$138,700)
following quotations obtained from normal commercial channels. The buildings
and structures to be constructed under-the Project would be required at
scattered locations over the three range areas. Therefore all Project Head-
quarters buildings and senior staff housing would be constructed using normal
Government procedures which are satisfactory to the Association. However,
in order to reduce the delays involved in the construction of all other
Project structures, special buildings and field constructions, these would
be built by the Project's own resources through force account (by AAISC in
the case of the feedlot construction), or by local contractors hired by LMB.
Assurances were obtained at negotiations that the Ministry of Public Works
would delegate authority to LMB to approve plans and provide for the con-
struction of such structures up to a limit of Eth$ 20,000 per unit and would
make arrangements satisfactory to the Association to reduce delays on the
approval of plans and execution of works for all other Project constructions.



- 25 -
Various items of veterinary equipment, medicines, chemicals and fertilizer
(US$600,000) would also be required in small amounts at scattered locations
Various items of veterinary equipment, medicines, chemicals and fertilizer
(US$600,000) would also be required in small amounts at scattered locations
and at varying times throughout the Project period. Therefore, as they
would not be suitable for either international or local competitive bidding,
procurement of these items would be through normal commercial channels in
accordance with Government procedures satisfactory to the Association.
F. Disbursement (Annex 17)
4.33      The proceeds of the Credit would be disbursed over five years on
the basis of:
(a) 100% of the foreign or 76% of the total costs of
vehicles and technical assistance; and
(b) 76% of the costs of all other items financed by IDA.
Disbursements against (a) would be fully documented. Disbursements against
(b) would be made against statements of expenditure certified by the sub-
project managers and by the Financial Controller; these would be kept by LMB
and made available to IDA for inspection. Any funds remaining in the credit
account upon completion of the Project would be subject to cancellation, since
their use to cover similar expenditures would require a new project preparation
and appraisal. ADF parallel financing of the Southern Rangelands ranches,
feedlot, smallholder fattening and road construction components would be
subject to similar arrangements. An estimated schedule of Project disburse-
ment is presented in Annex 17. Should the Project be implemented more quickly,
as Government believes possible, the rate of disbursement would be adjusted
accordingly. A Project implementation schedule is also presented in Annex 17.
G. Accounts and Audit
4.34      The accounting system of LMB is barely adequate for its present
level of activities and its accounting system is mainly a set of double entry
books from which a trial balance is extracted at the end of each month. LMB
particularly suffers from a shortage of qualified and experienced staff in
the accounts section. To allow for future development and the wide geographical
area IMB's activities would cover, the Second Livestock Development Project
provided for the appointment of consultants to design an effective accounting
and internal control system; this has become even more important at this stage
given LMB's increased level of activities. The consultants have completed
the manual of accounts and IMB is now making plans for the gradual implemen-
tation of the new system. In order to better enable LMB to carry out the new



- 26 -
duties created as a result of the proposed Project, provision has been in-
cluded under the Project for the employment of a Financial Controller, an
Internal Auditor and two Accountants (para 4.29). LMB's accounts are
audited annually by the firm of Mann Judd & Co., who are associated with
the London firm of the same name. Assurances were obtained during negotia-
tions that the accounts of LMB would continue to be audited by independent
auditors acceptable to IDA and that LMB would submit its annual accounts
and the auditor's report to IDA within six months of the end of its financial
year.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
Livestock and Meat Board
5.01      The Livestock and Meat Board (LMB) under the direction of its
General Manager would be responsible for the overall execution of the Project.
At present, there are nine operating departments reporting directly to the
General Manager, and LMB has also been given responsibility for a number of
commercial enterprises recently nationalized by Government. In an effort to
consolidate the existing lines of authority and to better enable LMB to carry
out its designated tasks, Government is presently considering a reorganization
plan submitted by management consultants retained under the Second Livestock
Development Project. Under this proposal, as amended by Government (Annex 2,
Chart: World Bank 9814), LMB would have four operational and two service
departments: Rangeland Development; Animal Industry Development; Marketing;
Livestock II; Finance; and Administration and Personnel. Two of these
departments - the Rangeland Development Department and the Finance Department -
would be particularly important to the successful implementation of the pro-
posed Project. The Rangeland Development Department would help coordinate
the activities of the three sub-projects, and would provide a number of common
services to them; the Finance Department would provide certain financial and
central procurement services for the Project. Both departments have now
been formally established within LMB, and internationally recruited senior
staff acceptable to IDA are being recruited to serve as Department Heads
(para 4.29).
Project Development Units
5.02      Each of the three sub-projects would have a separate organizational
unit based at one of the three Projiect headquarters. These three units -
the Southern Rangelands Development: Unit (SORDU), the Jijigga Rangelands
Development Unit (JIRDU), and the NIortheast Rangelands Development Unit
(NERDU) - would be headed by a Project Manager directly responsible to the
Head of LMB's Rangeland Development: Department. Each sub-project would have



- 27 -
its own bank account, and would oversee its own accounting, disbursement,
procurement and personnel activities; as noted above, where necessary these
services would be supplemented by a number of central services to be pro-
vided by LMB (see Annex 14). Within each sub-project unit, the following
major divisions would report directly to the Project Manager:
(a) Range Management Division;
(b) Veterinary Division;
(c) Fattening Division (SORDU and JIRDU only);
(d) Finance and Administration Division;
(e) Technical Division (construction of roads and
water facilities); and
(f) Training and Information Division.
In addition to these, the Northeast Rangelands unit would have three add-
itional divisions or units to help it administer special programs in that
area; these would include a Marketing Unit, a Supplies and Distribution Unit
and an Irrigation Division. The duties of each division are explained
in the relevant annexes. These three projects would require about 57 high
level staff, and it is expected that about 14 of these would be international-
ly recruited (about half are expected to be Ethiopians); a detailed list of
all Project staff is given in Annex 18. A condition of disbursement for any
one of the three sub-projects taken individually would be that the Project
Manager, the Senior Range Officer, the Senior Financial Officer and the Senior
Veterinarian had been recruited and that the candidates for these posts and
the terms and conditions of their employment would be subject to IDA approval.
5.03      The Project Manager would have immediate responsibility for the
coordination of all Project development activities within his area. In order
to assist him in this effort, and to ensure coordination of Project activities
with other programs currently underway, each Project Manager would set up a
Project Development Committee; the membership of the committee would include
the Head of the (Awraja) Administration, other local representatives, as
well as representatives of the livestock producers in the area. As it
would also be essential that the Project establish a good working relationship
with the stockowners, Range Councils would be established; Project management
would work with these councils in setting up range management and water
control programs, and the councils would become increasingly responsible
for the policing of the system. In the Southern Rangelands and Jijigga
areas, the main contacts between Project staff would be effected through
the veterinary service and the Range Management Department, and these units
would increasingly serve as the focus of the development activities. The



- 28 -
approach would be somewhat different in the Northeast Rangelands area, however,
where the main contacts would be made through the eight Project Development
Centers (para. 4.28); thus in an area where uneasy inter-tribal relations,
as well as communications and geographic difficulties make any contact with
the pastoralists particularly complicated, the added facilities and staff
available at these centers would greatly facilitate these efforts.
Other Development Activities
5.04      The Project development units would be assisted in their task by
several other organizations and Government authorities now operating in
these areas. The Awassa Agro-Industrial Share Company would construct the
feedlot unit from specifications provided by the Feedlot Manager, and would
provide a number of other services to the feedlot unit (para. 4.18). SORIDEP,
the French bilateral extension program now operating around Awassa, would
help in identifying suitable smallholders to participate in the Southern
Rangelands fattening program (para. 4.20). Likewise EPID would provide
much the same type of assistance in the Jijigga area (para. 4.21). The IDA-
financed Drought Areas Rehabilitation Project now underway in the Northeast
Rangelands area has served in many respects as pilot project to test develop-
ments being undertaken in that area by the proposed Project (e.g. the water
spreading program). The National Water Resources Commission is now carrying
out water programs in all three Project areas.
VI. PRODUCTION, MARKETS AND PRICES
A. Production (Annex 19)
6.01      The Project would result in a substantial increase of marketed
production of livestock and livestock products from the three range areas;
this increase would be attributable both to an increase in the liveweight
of all animals marketed from the ranges as well as an increase in the
total marketed offtake. The relevant statistics for livestock marketed
from the range area are given below:

/1   The original offtake rate was estimated at 6%. As a result of the
JP-15 campaign, the offtake rate increased to 9%. Without the con-
tinuation of this campaign, the offtake rate would rapidly drop again
to 6%. The follow-up of this campaign will be financed by the Project,
so that the incremental offtake will be fully credited to the Project.
(See Annex 19)
/2   These numbers are derived from static herd estimates and ignore the
currently low offtake and marketed rates attributable to the recent
drought.
At full development (year 7), total increased marketed production from the
ranges attributable to the Project would be about 100,000 head of cattle,
48,500 sheep and goats and 3,600 camels annually. This would represent 16%
and 3% of the estimated marketed production of cattle and sheep and goats in
1974. These estimates are based on available statistics for the Project areas
and on experience gained in similar areas elsewhere in East Africa. In
addition to the increased production attributable to improved services in the
range areas, the ranches, feedlot and smallholder fattening programs would
also result in significant increases in total marketed production. The
estimated 16,000 animals handled by the ranches annually at full development
would show an average liveweight gain of 100 kg/head (from 150 to 250 kg
liveweight). The feedlot would process about 8,000 head annually, with an
estimated average gain of 100 kg/head (from 250 to 350 kg/head). Increases
attributable to the three smallholder fattening schemes show varying results
due to the significantly different quality of the animals concerned. In
Southern Rangelands, the total of about 2,000 animals included in the program
each year would show average liveweight gains of 100 kg/head. In Jijigga
the fattening and background operations would cover a total of about 9,000
head annually at full development; these would show average liveweight
gains of 210 kg/head (from 140 kg/head to 350 kg/head) and 110 kg/head (from
140 to 250 kg/head) respectively. Gains from the grazier scheme in the
Northeast Rangelands area would be somewhat less as the cattle would be
kept on the natural ranges; a total of about 3,000 head each year at full
development would gain an average of 100 kg/head (from 140 kg/head to 240 kg/



- :30 -
head). The incremental meat production at full development would be equiv-
alent to 15,000 tons of meat, representing about 14% of the present estimated
marketed meat production 1/.
6.02      In addition to these increases in total livestock production, the
Project would also result in increased production of milk from range cattle
in the three areas 2/; this increased output would total about 7.8 million
liters of milk annually at full development, adding about 80 liters of milk
to the annual consumption of a stockowner's family. Finally, incremental crop
production from the water spreading program in the Northeast Rangelands
area would total about 1.1 million kg of cotton, 320,000 kg of sorghum or
maize, and 120,000 kg of groundnuts annually at full development.
6.03      The total annual incremental production attributable to the Project
at full development would be valued at Eth$ 23.6 million. The value of
the incremental marketed production of range cattle would be based on (a)
the incremental value of meat over current marketed production attributable
to liveweight gains as a result of the Project 3/, and (b) the value of the
increased number of animals marketed; the total value of this production is
estimated at Eth$13.6 million at full development (year 7). The value of in-
cremental sheep and goat and camel production is likewise estimated at Eth$ 1.9
million and Eth$ 1.2 million respectively. The stratification of industry
through the establishment of ranches, the feedlot, and the smallholder
fattening programs would also result in significant liveweight gains for an
estimated 30,000 head of cattle; the incremental value of this increase is
estimated at Eth$1.6 million for the ranches, Eth$1.8 million for the small-
holder fattening programs, and Eth$1.3 million for the feedlot.  Incremental
milk production would be valued at Eth$ 1.2 million and crop production
at Eth$0.9 million.
B. Markets and Prices (Annex 20)
Markets
6.04      Total production of cattle was about 1.9 million head in 1972/73,
and that of sheep and goats about 14.6 million head in the same year. Of
1/   Recorded marketed production only.
2/   It was assumed that each cow would show an incremental milk production
of 15 kg of milk annually.

- 31 -
this total about 90% of the cattle and 95% of the sheep and goats were con-
sumed locally, while the remainder were exported through both official and
unofficial channels. About 50% of all consumption consists of beef, and the
rest is largely sheep, goat and poultry meat. Rural consumption accounted
for some 71% and 87% respectively of total cattle and sheep production in
this same year, as compared with 80% and 90% respectively in 1967/68. These
figures indicate that the relative importance of urban consumption has been
increasing - at least in the case of beef - at a fairly rapid rate, and it is
expected that this trend will become even more pronounced over the coming
decade. 1/ Thus, a growing population with an increasing percentage located
in higher income urban markets has ex-erted considerable pressures on availa-
ble meat supplies. This situation was further aggravated by a 53% increase
in total exports of livestock and livestock products in the same five years.
These factors contributed to a 9% decline in per capita consumption of meat
in that period, 2/ and projections indicate that this trend is expected to
extend until the mid 1980s, as demand continues to exceed available supplies.
Recent LMB studies indicate that over the period 1974-1984 beef production
is expected to increase at an annual rate of 2.08% annually and that of sheep
and goats at a rate of about 0.4% per annum, as compared with an annual average
population growth of 2.4%.
6.05      The rapidly growing economies of various Middle Eastern countries
have opened up more favorable export markets for Ethiopian beef, putting
an additional strain on domestic supplies. The growing needs of domestic
markets - particularly in urban areas - could, however, become a major
constraint to the development of an expanded export trade. In early 1975
Government instituted a 15% export tax on livestock and meat products which,
if enforced, could have resulted in a considerable reduction in official
exports. However, this tax has now been abolished.
6.06      Most of the incremental production attributable to the Project
would be marketed domestically through existing channels (see Chart, World
Bank 9816). These sales would be made through local traders operating out
of established rural markets (para. 2.10); animals would then be either
sold live on rural or urban markets or slaughtered for ultimate sale on
mainly urban markets. It is also estimated that a small percentage of the
total incremental production would be exported live or as chilled meat to
nearby countries, although the exact proportion of exports is difficult to
estimate, and would depend to some extent on future Government policy (para.
6.05). Up to 75% of the annual incremental production of sheep and goats
from the Jijigga area would most likely be exported live to neighboring Arab
1/   In these years the urban population is expected to increase at a rate
of 6.5% annually, as compared with a rate of 2.1% for the rural popula-
tion.
2/   From 15.3 kg/head to 13.9 kg/head.  These figures are, however, well
beyond consumption levels in most other African countries.



- 32 -
countries. In addition to this, about 6,000 head of cattle annually from
the feedlot (75% of total output), and 3,000 head from the smallholder
fattening program (25% of the total) would also be exported either live
or as chilled beef. The additional stratification of the livestock in-
dustry introduced by the Project would provide new and more profitable
market outlets for nomadic producers, thus helping to increase competition
in existing markets. Considering these factors, the existing network of
markets and traders is reasonably efficient and would be adequate to meet
the increased volume generated by the Project.
Prices
6.07      Average prices for good quality cattle increased by about 75% over
the period 1968/69-1974/75. In 1974 this price was about Eth$ 0.60/kg
liveweight, although limited quantities of high quality beef might sell for
as much as Eth$1.50/kg liveweight during peak demand periods. Sheep prices
generally averaged about twice the price paid for good quality beef. Domestic
prices for cattle, sheep and goats are expected to increase annually between
1% and 2% up to 1980 and 2% to 3% from then until 1984. Prices of exported
meat and meat products significantly increased in the period up to 1972/73
following world market trends. Since then, while prices for exports to
European markets fell dramatically in 1974, price levels for live exports from
Ethiopia (mainly to Saudi Arabia) have remained fairly stable. Although
Ethiopian export prices have tended to rise in line with world prices, price
levels are below world market prices on average, due to the higher risk of
disease. In particular, Ethiopian prices are significantly lower than those
now being paid in Kenya, reflecting higher costs and larger trading margins.
However, with the expected improvement of the marketing system, veterinary
services and the production of better quality animals, it is likely that
Ethiopia will participate in the higher valued trade and that export prices
will increase at a greater rate during the coming years so as to reach world
levels. Current projections forecast an annual increase in world market
prices of 2.2% during the period 1978-1985. Whether these price increases
will be passed on to the producer will depend on a better integration of
stockowners into the market economy and Government's export and pricing policies.
6.08      Faced with a growing consumer demand and rising meat prices,
Government has instituted a system of price control. However, these controls
only apply to three broadly defined categories of animals, and permit prices
to be negotiated between buyer and seller over a range from Eth$ 150 to Eth$
350 per head. In addition, both smaller and larger animals have recently
been exempted from these restrictions. Thus it is considered that actual
prices will be determined by market conditions. It is in fact essential that
projected price increases for meat be passed on to the producer in order to
stimulate offtake from the national herd and to channel it into the market
stream; moreover, the small producers who are presently reluctant to sell
their animals may be influenced to change their outlook given greater financial
incentives. Hence, improved producer prices are not only an essential factor



- 33 -
in the development of the Ethiopian livestock industry, but are also parti-
cularly important to the success of the proposed Project. Therefore, assur-
ances were obtained at negotiations that in taking any measures or adopting
any regulations concerning the production and marketing of livestock, Govern-
ment shall pay due consideration to the need to continue to increase pro-
duction and stimulate the sale of livestock.
C. Government Cash Flow (Annex 21)
6.09      At full development the Project would generate about Eth$ 3 million
of tax revenues annually; these would cover the Government's debt service
payments to IDA and ADF as well as the operating costs of the three implemen-
tation units, and would also allow Government to recuperate part of its initial
investment. Because of the large investment expenditures required by the
three sub-projects, the Government overall cash flow would, however, remain
negative. Consequently, Government is now giving consideration to the re-
commendation that it levy a special development tax in the form of a head tax
on the inhabitants of the Project areas; such a tax would be instituted only
at the end of the five-year implementation period. As long as the tax is set
at a moderate level, it is believed that this method of raising of additional
Government revenues in the rangelands areas would be justified.
VII. ECONOMIC BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
7.01      The Project would constitute a first step in the long term develop-
ment of the nomadic rangelands areas. It would provide basic infrastructure
such as roads and water facilities as well as veterinary and extension ser-
vices in an area which had previously received little if any direct Govern-
ment assistance. Through the restructuring of the traditional system of
extensive livestock production, the Project would increase the presently low
marketed offtake from the ranges and would substantially raise the value of
marketed livestock production; this would result in significantly increased
cash incomes for the nomadic producers, thereby integrating them more fully
into the local market economy. Finally, the improved animal husbandry
practices and the gradual stratification of the livestock industry would
increase exports of live animals and would provide an even flow of quality
meat both for export and for sale on local markets.
7.02      The main quantifiable benefits stemming from the Project would be
a substantial increase in marketed livestock and crop production (para. 6.03);
at full development (Year 7), this incremental production would be valued at
Eth$ 22.4 million annually. 1/ The incremental production would increase
foreign exchange earnings by enabling increased exports of live animals as
1/   This figure does not include the value of milk production, as this figure
was not included in the calculation of the economic rate of return.



- 34 -
well as chilled and frozen meat (para. 6.06). The Project would also have a
number of additional benefits which are more difficult to quantify. These
would include: (a) better nutrition for about 100,000 pastoral families
through increased milk production; (b) more sanitary and more accessible water
supplies for about the same number of pastoral families; (c) greater safeguard
against future droughts, thus reducing the extent of both human and livestock
deaths as well as the required level of Government expenditures for future
relief programs; and (d) rationalization and modernization of the traditional
livestock sector to safeguard the potential for livestock production of
Ethiopia's rangelands, thus laying the basis for further development of the
livestock industry.
Economic Rate of Return and Sensitivity Analysis (Annex 22)
7.03      The overall economic rate of return for the Project over 20 years
is estimated at 24%. 1/ This rate of return is satisfactory, but is of course
subject to the risks noted below (paras. 7.05 and 7.06). Individual rates of
return were calculated for each of the three sub-projects; these are estimated
at about 22% for Southern Rangelands, 25% for Jijigga, and 26% for Northeast
Rangelands. Rates of return were also calculated for certain specific com-
ponents within these sub-projects; these are estimated at 17% for the ranches,
29% for the feedlot, and 13% for the water spreading/irrigation program.
Unskilled labor was shadow priced at Eth$ 300 per man year 2/ because of the
substantial underemployment in the range areas.  Foreign exchange was shadow
priced at a rate of Eth$ 2.70 per US$1.00 (as compared with an official rate
of Eth$ 2.07/US$1.00) to reflect more accurately the value of foreign exchange
to Ethiopia. If market prices had been used in the analysis, the rates of
return would be very similar to those noted above, with the exception of
the rate of return for the feedlot which would drop to 9%. 3/
7.04      The Project would not be particularly sensitive to changes in the
basic parameters. A 10% increase in costs would reduce the rate of return
from 24% to 21%, while a reduction of 10% in production would yield a rate
of return of 21%. A 25% increase in costs would give a rate of return of
187%, while a 25% reduction in benefit would reduce the return to 17%; a
combination of both assumptions would yield a rate of return of 12%. Thus
even under relatively unfavorable circumstances, the Project would yield a
reasonable rate of return.
1/   Including meat and crop production.  If milk production were included
the rate of return would be 27% overall.
2/   This represents approximately the income of a family in the subsistence
sector.
3/   The high rate of return for the feedlot (29%) is explained by the shadow
pricing of its foreign exchange earnings, as these represent 75% of
total production revenues.



- 35 -
7.05      The major risks facing the Project would be the ability and deter-
mination of Government to simultaneously staff and implement three large
projects, and the willingness of the nomads to accept changes in traditional
livestock management practices and to institute grazing and water controls.
LMB's management capability will in fact be one of the crucial determining
factors in the success of the Project. Thi5 management risk is however
difficult to quantify, and was therefore not included in the sensitivity
analysis. The risks are expected to be minimized through the provision of
supporting staff for LMB under the Project, and it is also believed that
LMB's planned reorganization should greatly enhance its operating efficiency.
One solution would have been to reduce both the size and the scope of the
Project by either curtailing individual components or by postponing one of
the three sub-projects; another solution might have been to reduce the area
covered by the Project. Both of these alternatives would, however, be self-
defeating. The scope of the individual components was greatly reduced and
the Project simplified in the course of appraisal, and as all the activities
are highly inter-dependent, any further reductions would endanger the effec-
tiveness of the entire effort. It was also felt that the activities in each
area should be initiated at basically the same time, for unless these develop-
ments were undertaken more or less simultaneously and were each of sufficient
size, there was a real danger that in times of stress the nomads would inun-
date those areas which offered improved water, veterinary and other services.
7.06      The Project would also be difficult in that it would require a
conservative nomadic people to institute substantial changes in their tradi-
tional practices. In fact, without these changes in the nomad traditional
livestock practices and cultural outlook, the rangelands would continue to
deteriorate as a result of overgrazing and other poor livestock management
practices. Hence, the main objective of the Project would be to achieve a
better balance between livestock herds and the carrying capacity of land by
means of persuasion, education and training and by providing greater financial
incentives to stockowners. Considerable efforts would be made to involve the
tribal authorities in the development effort, and the ability of the Project
management to ensure their active cooperation would be of prime importance to
the success of the Project. Overall, the risks associated with the Project
are relatively large, but in the long term they are substantially outweighed
by the significant benefits to be achieved.
Employment and Income Effects
7.07      The Project would ensure permanent employment for some 500 persons;
these persons would be directly employed by the Project for five years in
veterinary, range management, water development and other programs which
would be continued thereafter by Government: around 4,000 workers would be
given temporary employment during the Project implementation period. In addi-
tion, some 2,000 Afar families would obtain either permanent or seasonal em-
ployment on individual 1 ha plots as part of the Northeast Rangelands water
spreading program. These families would be chosen from among those now re-
ceiving famine relief under various drought rehabilitation programs now being



- 36 -
carried out in that area. It is estimated that these families would receive
the equivalent of an income of up to Eth$ 236 (US$114) as a result of this
program. Overall, the Project would affect an estimated 100,000 families in
the rangeland areas; the average annual per capita incomes of these pastoralists
would be increased from Eth$ 60 (US$29) to about Eth$ 85 (US$41) over the life
of the Project, thus substantially raising the incomes of one of the poorest
segments of Ethiopia's population.
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
8.01      During negotiations, agreement was reached with Government on the
following principal points:
(a) the National Water Resources Commission would designate the
three Project Units as the water development authority for their
respective areas (para. 4.11);
(b)  the Ministry of Public Works would delegate to LMB the
authority to approve plans and provide for the construction of
certain Project buildings and field structures costing up
to Eth$ 20,000 per unit either through force account or by
local contractors hired by LMB, and the Ministry would make
arrangements satisfactory to the Association to reduce
delays in the approval of plans and execution of works for
all other Project constructions (para 4.32);
(c)  the accounts of LMB would continue to be audited by
independent auditors acceptable to IDA and that LMB
would submit its annual accounts and the auditor's
report to IDA within six months of the end of its finan-
cial year (para. 4.34);
(d)  in taking any measures or adopting any regulations
concerning production and marketing of livestock, Govern-
ment shall pay due consideration to the need to continue
to increase production and stioulate the sale of livestock
(para 6.08).
8.02      It would be a condition of credit effectiveness that separate
accounts for each of the three sub-projects had been established and that
a deposit of funds had been made therein to cover the expenditures for the
first quarter of the Project implementation period starting January 1,
1976, such deposit to be made on the basis of a reasonable estimate of
the expenditures for such period (para 4.31).



- 37 -
8.03      A condition of disbursement for any one of the three sub-projects
taken individually would be that the Project Manager, the Senior Range
Management Officer, the Senior Financial Officer, and the Senior Veterinarian
had been recruited and that candidates for these posts and the terms and con-
ditions of their employment would be subject to IDA approval (para. 5.02).
8.04      The proposed Project would be suitable for an IDA credit of US$27
million on standard terms to the Government of Ethiopia.

ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Livestock and the Economy
A. General
1.        In the absence of a detailed survey, it is estimated that the
national livestock population is made up of some 27 million cattle; 24 million
sheep; 18 million goats; 7 million horses, mules and donkeys; and one million
camels. There are also said to be about 48 million poultry and 13,000 pigs.
Cattle, sheep and goats are increasing slowly in number, probably at a rate of
around 4Z over the last ten years. About 60% of all livestock are owned by
highland farmers, and the remaining 40% by the nomadic pastoralists in the low-
land areas.
2.        About six to seven million (23% to 27%) of the national cattle
herd are work oxen for use in the highland farming areas and about ten mil-
lion (38%) are females of breeding age. Individual herd sizes are usually
larger in the nomadic areas; some 66% of nomadic owners have more than 9 head
as compared with only 10% to 20% in the case of highland livestock owners.
In the highland areas, females of breeding ages make up 20% to 25% of the
herd, whereas in the nomadic regions the comparable figures are 40% to 50%.
The highlands have more favorable natural conditions for livestock produc-
tion than do the lowlands, but in the highlands livestock must compete with
crops for resources, and this is usually possible only with a joint crop-
ping/livestock mixed farming system. On balance, the opportunities for live-
stock development are significantly greater in the nomadic areas.
B. Livestock Breeds
3.        By and large Ethiopian Livestock is indigenous.  Although it is
possible to identify, for each species, different breeds with many local
sub-types, most of the livestock population is the result of indefinite
crossbreeding. The local breeds all pertain to the Zebu species; however,
all the intermediate types between the true short-horned Zebu with a well
developed thoracic hump and the Sanga type with a small cervic-pthoracoc
hump and long lyre-shaped horns can be found. The main breeds located in
the Project areas are described below.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
Cattle
4.        The Boran Zebu is found in the dry country of south and southeast-
ern Ethiopia. This short-horned Zebu has a good potential for beef produc-
tion and is extremely well-adapted to its semi-arid environment. Govern-
ment experimental studies show that with average management techniques, in
high potential rangeland a liveweight of 400 kg has been reached at 2.5 years.
Recent experiments in Kenya have also shown that native unimproved Boran cattle
raised on the range by traditional pastoralists are able to put on 800 g/day
when placed in feedlots. Milk production is relatively low.
5.        Short-horned Zebu are found in the administrative regions of Shoa,
Arussi and Hararge, and are very similar to the Boran type. The 122 local
Arussi cows kept at the Cobe breeding farm (CADU project) have produced an
average of 215 kg milk during the 1970-71 recording year; 11.4 percent of these
give more than 500 kg, with the maximum yield being 1,228 kg/year. Growth
potential in feedlot seems rather limited and even under improved manage-
ment young heifers have not usually gained more than 350-400 g/day.
6.        Adal (or Danakil), the only representatives of the Sanga type in
Ethiopia, are rather light frame cattle with a typical small cervic-thoracic
hump and long lyre-shaped horns. They are well adapted to the arid climate
prevailing in the far northeast of Ethiopia and in the lower and middle
Awash Valley. They are poor milk producers (200-300 kg milk in 150 days
lactation) but their potential for beef production in feedlot is reasonable;
when adequately fed, the average gain can be up to 500 g/day.
Sheep
7.        Sheep breeds are all indigenous and they are mostly exploited for
their meat. Along with goats, sheep provide most of the meat eaten by the
rural community, although in the highlands cash crops often provide sufficient
returns to the planters to allow them to buy some beef. All the lowland sheep
are of the hair-type, and skins are an important by-product. The ewes also
provide nomadic families with a regular supply of milk. Although the high-
land sheep population is mostly of a very mixed type due to frequent and
indiscriminate inter-breeding, two well-differentiated types can be noted.
8.        The Black-headed Somali fat-rumped sheep of the eastern and north-
eastern lowlands are also known as Ogaden or Harar sheep. They have a rather
high adult liveweight (35 kg), giving a carcass of the quality requested by
Arab importing countries. This breed seems to offer good prospects for genetic
improvements. It is a hairy type and the animals are never shorn. Milk poten-
tial seems to be satisfactory and ewes are usually milked during the second
and third months of lactation. Lambs are weaned at 6-7 months of age. Females
are bred at 18 months but sometimes as early as 6-7 months. Ewes are normally
bred once a year and culled when 7 years old. A 65-70 percent lamb crop seems
normal with a twinning rate of 4-5 percent under the traditional management
system. Castrated males are usually sold at 3 years old. Most of the sheep
involved in the Project would be of this type.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
9.        The Highland Type is found mostly in the highland areas north of
Addis Ababa. This fat-tailed breed is small and matures slowly. Most sheep
are therefore slaughtered at a liveweight of 22-23 kg. Ewes can be bred twice
a year and they are culled at 8-10 years of age. The fleece is inferior in
both quality and quantity. Where sheep are shorn (which is quite an uncommon
practice) average fleece weight is 0.250 kg/head. Nevertheless, this type of
sheep represents close to 75 percent of the Ethiopian sheep population and
produces mutton which finds an excellent outlet on the internal market (mainly
in Addis Ababa).
Goats
10.       Unlike sheep, most of the goat population (75 percent) is spread
over the arid lowlands. Goats are kept mainly for local meat consumption
and to a lesser extent for milk production. Skins are the source of a pro-
fitable export trade. Goats are not given sufficient attention by the
official services and hence information on them is scanty.
C. National Herd Production Parameters
Vital Statistics
11.       Vital statistics on livestock in Ethiopia are of questionable
reliability owing to the absence of carefully collected data. Nevertheless
figures are available which appear to have some validity - especially in the
case of the cattle population.
12.       Cattle.  Calving rates are very low.  Cows calve for the first time
when 3-1/2 years old and thereafter about every two years. Calf mortality in
the first year is extremely high - about 30 percent in an average year.-
Mortality rates are still substantial in the later years: 8 percent between
1 and 2; 5 percent between 2 and 3; and 7 percent for 3 year-old cattle and
older. This means that losses amount to approximately 8.5 percent of the
total herd.
13.       Nearly all the cattle are slaughtered without passing through a
finishing period. This is confirmed by the low dressing percentages ob-
served, i.e. 45 percent on average and even below 42 percent for the older
animals. At the Dire Dawa packing plant, the average liveweight of the
slaughtered cattle (exclusively adults) was 280 kg, with a dressing percentage
of 50 percent and a boneless meat yield of 71-72 percent. These figures imply
a carcass weight of 140 kg. The small size of the local Zebus can be
attributed to the high incidence of internal parasites and to the generally
poor grazing conditions prevailing over most of the year. Liveweight and
carcass yield of the native Zebus could be increased considerably if the
animals were to pass through a 3-5 month fattening phase before slaughter.
One finishing system used by the traditional sector produces the so-called
"Harar beef" which is sold at significantly higher prices on the Addis
Ababa market.



ANNEX 1
Page 4
14.       Sheep and Goats.  Vital statistics for sheep and goats are even
scarcer than for cattle. Breeding females represent 37 percent and 33 per-
cent of the total flock for sheep and goats respectively. The average carcass
weight for highland sheep is estimated at 10 kg. The Somali Black-head breed
has substantially different characteristics. Liveweight is higher (28-29 kg
for mature ewes and 30-35 kg for 3-7 year-old castrates), but fertility is
lower (a 65-70 percent lamb crop is the average, with 4 percent of births
being twins). Longevity is relatively shorter and ewes are usually culled
when over 6 years old. Females are normally bred for the first time at 18
months. Adult mortality has been estimated at 7-8 percent/year in the absence
of major disease outbreaks. Mortality at birth is high (10-20 percent) and
the death rate remains high for up to six months.
15.       Goats are kept mainly for subsistence.  No reliable information is
available regarding their number and performance. They are generally of
comparatively small size; LMB estimated an average carcass weight of 8.5 kg.
The fertility rate is about 90 percent, and each goat produces 1.5 kids per
annum. Mortality rates are slightly lower than for sheep.
16.       Ethiopian livestock show a low standard of feeding, breeding,
health and managment. Under-nutrition, malnutrition and disease are major
constraints to increased production in the greater part of the country, and
also have a serious detrimental effect on exports of livestock and livestock
products. The combination of these factors makes the Ehtiopian livestock
industry one of the less efficient in the world. On the other hand, the
country does have a tremendous potential for increased livestock production,
particularly from the range areas. The country's large livestock population
constitutes an invaluable resource which should be exploited.
D. Value of Livestock Production and Exports

1970 totalled about 800,000 sheep and 60,000 cattle. Including these figures,
total exports of livestock and livestock products would have been around
Eth$59 million in 1970 - equivalent to about 20% of total exports.

ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Livestock and Meat Board
1.        The Livestock and Meat Board (LMB) was established in 1964 to
function as a specialized autonomous Government agency aimed at developing
the production, processing and marketing of livestock and livestock products.
Its objectives and purposes also include support of the Ministry of Agricul-
ture's national veterinary program and the encouragement of production and
marketing of livestock through the dissemination of information and the
stimulation of research. To carry out its objectives, LMB was given broad
controlling and regulatory powers to construct or establish stockroutes,
watering points and marketing facilities. Its powers also include the
imposition and collection of fees on all livestock using facilities provided
by the Board and on livestock and livestock products being exported from
Ethiopia.
2.        The activities of LMB are wide and varied; they include the inspec-
tion of hygiene standards at export processing plants and the issue of export
permits, the operation of a feed mix plant, the Shola poultry center and ten
recently nationalized meat processing plants. LMB also carries out studies
and surveys (e.g. covering ways and means of increasing the export of live
sheep, poultry production and consumption etc.) and its Project Office is
engaged in the preparation of a number of projects for possible presentation
to IDA. Responsibility for the implementation of the proposed Nomadic Range-
lands Development Project would be given to IMB; this would greatly broaden
the scope of its activities and put its operations on a much larger scale
than has hitherto been the case.
Organization and Staffing
3.        The LMB now has a Board of Directors under the chairmanship of the
Minister of Agriculture, or his duly authorized representative; its members
comprise one representative of each of the Ministries of Public Health,
Commerce and Industry, Finance, and National Community Development desig-
nated by the respective ministries. Membership of the Board of Directors
also includes one representative of each of traders, processors and producers
designated by the Minister of Agriculture after consultation with representa-
tive bodies of these sections of the industry. This organizational structure
may be substantially revised under a proposed reorganization of the Ministry
of Agriculture. If this plan is put into effect the Board of Directors would
be abolished, and the General Manager of LMB would be responsible directly to
the head of the Livestock Resources Development Department to be established
within the Ministry.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
4.        The General Manager is the executive head of the LMB.  As the Board
is now organized, all heads of departments (Planning, Research and Information
Department, Marketing Department, Administration Department, Accounts Depart-
ment, Project Office) and heads of subsidiaries and subsidiary activities
(Second Livestock Project, Shola poultry center, feed mix plant and ten meat
processing plants) are in direct line responsibility to the General Manager
(see Chart, World Bank 9815). Given LMB's varied responsibilities, the
position of the General Manager is rapidly becoming an untenable one, as far
too many activities are expected to be coordinated directly by him. If the
responsibility of implementing and operating the Nomadic Rangelands Development
Project is to be added to all these activities, it is obvious that LMB will
have to be reorganized if it is to be able to properly carry out its designated
tasks.
5.        In accordance with the terms of the credit agreement for the Second
IDA Livestock Development Project, LMB employed a firm of Management Consult-
ants (McLintock Mann and Whinney Murray, London) to assist the Board in the
design and introduction of an effective accounting system with respect to
expenditures under the Project and with respect to the accounts of the
Board as a whole. It is expected that the consultants' proposal for changes
in LMB's accounting procedures will be adopted. The consultants also proposed
to restructure LMB on the basis of current operations and likely future devel-
opments. These suggestions have been amended somewhat by Government, and the
current proposal is contained in an attached Chart (World Bank 9814). This
new organization would limit the number of senior officers reporting directly
to the General Manager to five. Two new posts would be created: the Head
of the Rangeland Development Department (who would serve as the coordinator
of the three sub-projects) and the Head of the Animal Industry Development
Department (with responsibility for the feed-mix plant, the poultry center,
and the meat processing plants). The Finance Department headed by a quali-
fied Financial Controller would be divided into three divisions: Accounts,
Budgeting and Procurement. The remaining two Departments would be a Livestock
II Department with responsibility for the Second IDA Livestock Project, and
an Administration and Personnel Department. The offices of the Legal Adviser
and the Internal Auditor would be directly attached to the General Manager's
Office. The above plan is at present under consideration by Government.
July 24, 1974
