ETHIOPIA
RANGELAND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
The Ethiopian Rangelands:  Prospects for Development
1.        Rangelands are defined as those areas which grow natural (wild)
vegetation and which for some ecological reason--normally low rainfall, but
in some cases poor soils or extreme temperatures--are unsuited for cultiva-
tion. In Ethiopia the ranges cover some 500,000 km2 or about 42% of the
total land area of the country. The vast majority of these lands are found in
areas below 1,700 meters at an altitude where the rainfall is less than 600
mm per annum. While no accurate figures are available, it is estimated that
the ranges contain a human population of 2.5 million, 5.5 million cattle,
4.5 million sheep, 2.5 million goats and 1 million camels. In addition to
providing subsistence for the largely nomadic population the rangelands
provide the (highland) cultivated arQas with livestock and livestock products,
and they are presently the only areas with a net surplus of cattle for
domestic consumption or for export.
2.        The rangelands have an immense potential for the sustained produc-
tion of livestock and livestock products, and they are unique in that this
potential could be exploited with only relatively small additional capital
inputs. Such a program would, however, necessitate stringent restrictions
on the number of animals using these areas and the imposition of a voluntary
and comprehensive system of range use controls. If this could be achieved,
the rangelands could make a significant contribution to the future develop-
ment of the agricultural sector.
A. Present State of the Range Areas
3.        Over the centuries nature has maintained a precarious balance
between man, his domestic livestock, and the wild herbivores. The number
of people and animals utilizing the ranges has tended to increase gradually
over the good years until they reach a point beyond which the land can support
them. At this point the lack of adequate grazing and water supplies result
in widespread livestock losses from disease and starvation; these in turn
often lead to deaths and disease among the nomadic livestock owners, as they
depend almost entirely on their animals for subsistence. In the drought
years, losses often reach catastrophic proportions. The major factor which
has contributed to the recurrence of this cycle is the traditional system
whereby communally owned lands are grazed by individually owned livestock.
Under this system no individual gains from controlling the numbers in his
own herd, for any vegetation or water thus "saved" would be immediately
consumed by his neighbor's stock. The ever-recurring cycle is reinforced



ANNEX 3
Page 2
by the deaths and livestock losses attributable to inter-tribal friction;
this friction generally develops over access to grazing and water, and
consequently becomes even more serious during the drought periods.
4.        A secondary contributing factor to the deterioration of the ranges
has been the increasing encroachment of settled agriculture on the best dry
season grazing areas. The settlement was mainly the result of Government's past
practice of allocating land to Government officials and pensioners, and this
practice was further aggravated by the fact that in many cases basically
nomadic people have taken up a sort of "defensive" cultivation to preserve
their claims to specific areas. These excisions have disturbed the cyclical
transhumant movement of the stockowners and have exerted intolerable pressure
on other areas within the grazing pattern. Additional pressure then results
as the sedentary farmers build up their own flocks and herds and maintain them
throughout the year around water points in areas which previously suffered
only seasonal grazing pressure. Over the past five decades, these factors
have all contributed to the significant deterioration of the Ethiopian range
areas. Moreover, as the nomads are increasingly restricted within an ever-
tightening boundary, the situation is gradually worsening and is now approach-
ing the point where the damage to the existing vegetation will become perma-
nent unless immediate remedial action is taken.
B. Future Range Development
5.        It is crucial to the future of the rangelands that all development
efforts affecting these areas are coordinated and integrated into an overall
range management program. While ordinarily uncoordinated efforts may result
in duplication of effort and a waste of resources, in the range areas such
an approach could well aggravate the existing situation. For example, the
provision of extra water facilities without the imposition of grazing controls
can lead to over-stocking and destruction of vegetation; likewise improved
disease control without the provision of improved marketing facilities and
stratification of the livestock industry can lead to an excessive build-up
of stock numbers. Also, unless stringent controls are imposed, improved
road access may lead to the excision of essential dry season grazing areas
for settled cultivation.  All development activities it these areas must
therefore be carefully evaluated to determine their long term consequences
and positive efforts made to ensure the preservation of the natural grazing
areas.
6.        As a first step a land use plan should be drawn up for each of the
range areas; these will necessarily vary from one region to another due to
differences in climate, tribal customs, vegetation, water supply, existing
facilities, etc. Such a plan would help to protect the interests of the
nomadic pastoralists and at the same time it would allow the identification
of pockets of land within the range areas which could be cultivated without
doing damage to the productivity of the range as a whole. Once the land use
plan has been drawn up, an integrated range development program can then be
undertaken. Such a program would include but not be limited to:



ANNEX 3
Page 3
(a) Development of water supplies. Any water program must
necessarily include a concomitant agreement by the nomads
to limit the number of livestock using these facilities.
(b) Demarcation of grazing areas and the imposition of controls
with regard to the period each area would be open for use.
(c) Regulation of stock numbers. Support for this would be
generated by an extension program designed to encourage
an increased offtake and by the provision of additional
market outlets.
(d) Restrictions on burning of the range and on the cutting
of trees and shrubs.
(e) Strict regulation of the excision of any additional part
of the range for cultivation.
(f) Construction of roads to allow improved access to remote
areas.
(g) Disease control through an extended veterinary program.
(h) Training of nomadic livestock owners in improved husbandry
techniques, range management, disease control practices, etc.
C. ProJect Range Management Program
Range Management - Different Systems and their Consequences
7.        The purpose of the range management program proposed under the
Project is to:
(a) Enable existing users of the rangelands to continue to meet
their subsistence needs from their livestock herds, but with
a switch away from the direct consumption of livestock products
to trading these products for other foods.
(b) Maximize the net sustainable marketed output of livestock
from the Project area.
(c) Lay the foundations for a range management system to meet
present and future social, economic, and ecological requirements.
By range management is meant the whole complex of land tenure, livestock
movements, the adjusting of stocking rates to feed availabilities, the rota-
tion and resting of pastures, reseeding or the introduction of new varieties,
the control of burning and cutting, and the development of water supplies.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
8.        There is a traditional system of range management in the Project
areas which grew up to meet specific ecological, economic and socio-political
conditions. 1/ It was a fairly efficient and largely self-sufficient and self-
regulating system, which has, however, come under increasing strain during
the last century due to increasing human and livestock numbers. Under the
traditional system the movements of livestock and therefore the adjustment
of stock numbers to the availability of grazing were governed by:
(a) The regular climatic seasons.
(b) The stochastic incidence of rainfall both within seasonal
patterns and between adjacent areas.
(c) The distribution of water at different seasons.
(d) Requirements for different types of pasture or water (e.g.
short grass for lambing sheep, salt water for camels) at
certain times of year for reasons of good animal husbandry.
(e) Random socio-political conditions.
Under the traditional system rapid adjustment can be made in response to
both regular climatic seasons and to the stochastic incidence of rainfall.
Livestock are moved two or three hundred kilometers according to the onset
of seasonal rains in different areas. Advantage can be taken of scattered
showers and thunderstorms which fall on some areas while adjacent areas
remain dry. Because of the absence of restrictions to mobility (e.g. bound-
aries of landholdings) the herbage can be exploited when its nutrient value
is highest, and the "risk" arising from rainfall is limited to that arising
from the uncertain total quantity falling on the whole area within seasons
and is not affected by uncertainty as to exactly where within the area the
rainfall will be precipitated. As a consequence the traditional system
permits a much higher overall stocking rate at any point in time than do
less flexible systems.
9.        Even before the onset of current strains on it, the traditional
system had defects. Adjustments in stock numbers followed rather than pre-
ceded or accompanied the events whlich made them necessary (e.g. random or
cyclical changes in rainfall amount, or changes in herd numbers arising from
the incidence of disease), for where ownership of livestock is by individuals
but grazing is communal, no individual gains from his own destocking. Inade-
quate incentives existed to improve the conditions on communal rangelands,
since the returns from doing so were not related to the individual's efforts.
Water supplies were not distributed according to the availability of grazing
(and hence the need for water) but according to the technical capacity which
1/  Government of Ethiopia, LMB Southern Rangelands Development Project,
Part II Volume 2, Sociology and Pastural Economy, Agrotec, 1974.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
existed to locate water and to raise it to where animals can drink it. Never-
theless stock numbers were kept in rough balance with (or at any rate not much
above) the capacity of the rangelands to feed them by the haphazard incidence
of disease, by recurring droughts and the consequent death of livestock (and
subsequently humans) from starvation, and by the limitation imposed on stock
numbers by inadequate water supplies.
10.       The strains on the traditional system are increasing.  Anti-Locust
and Anti-Rinderpest campaigns have reduced, or are reducing, these effective
curbs on stock numbers. Technology, in the form of concrete lined water tanks
(Berekedus), is increasing the availability of water. The traditionally tribally
imposed range discipline is crumbling. The area of the rangelands is decreasing
as the area of cultivation increases. Consequent deterioration in the
condition of the rangelands is appearing. Above all, the loss of livestock
and of human life in cyclical readjustments of stocking rates to range capacity
is no longer acceptable. In the light of these changes, and given the con-
tinuing growth of human population and the pressure for higher per capita
incomes, modification of the old system, or a new system, is required. A
minimum rate of growth in net output of 4% per annum from the rangelands is
needed to give even the most modest growth in per capita incomes.
11.       Whatever overall system is adopted, some progress can be made in
accommodating more people at higher incomes simply by a switch in diet wtlch
substitutes cereals, traded for livestock output, for the direct consumption
of the livestock products themselves. The prospective rise in relative
prices of livestock products during the next decade, and the reduction in
transport costs which can be brought about by improvements in roads, will
make this switch easier. The rise in the price of livestock products will
also give an incentive to the needed rise in the physical productivity of
the land.
12.       There are five major broad alternatives to existing traditional
systems of range management. One alternative would be to split the communal
rangelands into individual holdings. Such a system would give the greatest
incentive to individuals to improve the productivity of their land, and some
incentive to keep stock numbers down to the land's carrying capacity (although
the existence of individual forms of land tenure has not been sufficient
incentive to do these things in some other parts of Ethiopia and Africa).
This system would, however, make the provision of facilities showing economies
of scale (water supplies, fencing, dips) prohibitively expensive. It would
also greatly reduce the stocking capacity of the land at any point in time,
for the same reasons that the transhumant system maximizes it. This is
particularly true of the lower rainfall parts of the Project areas.
13.       Another alternative would be the formation of group ranches (along
lines tried in other countries), where groups of individuals own land in
common and their livestock either in common or individually, with these
livestock being kept throughout the year on the same block of land. Such
a system would make the basic units "creditworthy," and would give some in-
centive to individuals to increase the productivity of land and to preserve
proper stocking rates (not as big an incentive as in the case of individual



ANNEX 3
Page 6
holdings however). It would also be a method by which "authorized" stocking
rates could be enforced, by making it possible for the authorities actually
to count stock, and to associate those stock with particular people. Econo-
mies of scale in the provision of facilities (water, fencing, etc.) could
be realized. No advantage, however, could be taken of the seasonality of
rainfall in different parts of the Project area, and only a small reduction
in "risk" over the system of individual holdings would be achieved in respect
of the uncertain distribution of total rainfall in any season between different
sections of the Project area. At any point in time, therefore, the stocking
capacity of such a system would be lower than the corresponding capacity at
that time of the traditional transhumant system.
14.       A third system would be to associate groups of people with several
pieces of land in different climatic zones within the Project area (so as to
take advantage of the different vegetation types and rainfall seasons), which
their livestock, and their livestock alone, would be allowed to graze. Such
a system would secure many advantages of the present transhumant system, and
would also give some incentive to increase land productivity and to control
stock numbers. It would make it moderately easy for the Project administration
to check on whether authorized stocking rates were being observed. It would,
however, involve a complete abandonment of traditional tribal territories,
and considerable difficulties in arranging a fair distribution of land of
different qualities in different places.
15.       A fourth system would be to associate a particular group of people
with one (and only one) piece of land, but to allow other people--with the
permission of the "owning" group and on payment of a grazing fee--to graze
their livestock on this bit of land. While in theory giving the owning group
the incentive to improve the land and to control stock numbers at an optimum
rate, such a system is very close to the present system and the incentive
may not be strong enough to lead to the sort of changes needed.
16.       A final alternative is for Government to assume complete respon-
sibility for land improvement and for imposing the appropriate stocking rate
in conjunction with the continuance of the present transhumant system.
Although this would make the administration of land and pasture improvement
work easy, the task of counting livestock and associating these livestock
with a particular group of people in the program in order to limit stock
numbers is almost impossible to carry out.
The Proposed Long Term Policy for the Project Area
17.       The time is not yet ripe for irrevocable steps to be taken towards
implementing any one of the first four broad alternative systems outlined in
the preceding paragraphs as a final solution to the problems of the rangelands
in the Project area. Much information still needs to be collected and pro-
cessed before irrevocable decisions can be made. Foremost among this inform-
ation is:
(a) a complete inventory of permanent water points with their capacity
at different times of the year;



ANNEX 3
Page 7
(b) a reliable inventory of vegetation resources, present trends and
potential carrying capacity of different vegetation zones under
different management systems;
(c) a list of that part of the human population of the Project area
which is mainly dependent on the rangelands for its livelihood,
by tribe and sub-tribe as a preliminary step to a registration
of right holders. An estimate should be made of the total
livestock holdings (by kind) of each group;
(d) more detailed information than has been obtained so far on
migration patterns, their causes, extent, timing and frequency.
As far as can be judged, neither official nor private opinion in the Project
area is yet ripe for the allocation of rights in a particular piece of
rangeland along the lines of the first four broad systems enumerated above.
The indigenous inhabitants of the Project area are frightened lest any fur-
ther plans for allocation of land rights, however ostensibly aimed at the
benefit of the indigenous people, should merely be a device for opening the
door to further immigration and land-grabbing by outsiders. However, opinions
will change over the period of the Project, as the local inhabitants gain
confidence in the integrity of the Project organization, and when officials
are brought to see both the limited scope for further immigration from outside
the area, and the utility of proper range development.
18.       With the information at present available, the appropriate long-
term policy for the true rangelands area appears to be the continuation of a
transhumant system of range management, with livestock herds moving both
according to the broad general patterns indicated by the seasons, and oppor-
tunistically to take advantage of random events within these broad patterns.
In order to give an incentive for development, and to relieve Government of
the administrative burden of being responsible for the execution of all the
details of range management, blocks of land should be allocated to groups of
pastoralists, under appropriate conditions, for them to develop, manage, and
obtain the benefits from. Access to these blocks should not be limited to
the livestock of the owning group, but should be open to those of other groups
also, under suitable contractual arrangements. The general range management
policy should be to reintroduce order and discipline to the transhumant
system, so that the seasonal grazing pattern is established, and so that
adequate rest is given to the range. The conservation--of adequate forage
for dry season grazing reserves is the most urgent need. Attention should
for this reason be given to the establishment of dry season reserves around
existing permanent water points, and to the renovation of areas of depletion
by rest and rotation. Water development should be used to increase the dry
season range areas (but only within a system of management) and to take some
of the pressure off the existing water points.
19.       While attention should be given to the dry season reserves, all
other rangeland used in other seasons should also gradually be brought under
a management plan which will include both the setting of stocking rates and
rotation deferred and rest rotation grazing systems. Stocking rates should
be set to maintain the correct number and class of livestock on the range in



ANNEX 3
Page 8
order to establish reserve-stocking which will permit a sustained yield of
livestock and livestock products to be harvested while maintaining range
condition. Burning, lopping and destruction of range browse, shrubs and
trees should be controlled. The application of range management must be
affected with the full cooperation of the vast majority of range users, else
it will be unenforceable; legal sanctions must be introduced for the measures
to be adopted.
20.       Investment requirements (water supplies, access tracks, dips, etc.)
should be determined and the investment carried out. Those families should
be identified whose livestock holdings have fallen below the minimum viable
level required to partake in the transhumant system, and either means provided
to bring them up to the required level, or these families should be removed
from the ranges and settled on land identified under the land use plan as
being most suitable for cultivation.
21.       Since it is not possible to regard the Project area as a closed sys-
tem, long-term policy and land allocation to groups should also take into ac-
count the needs of pastoral groups who are not present in the Project area
throughout the year, but who migrate to and from other parts of Ethiopia.
It will be necessary to extend development into adjacent areas in the not too
distant future if the Project is not to act as a magnet attracting livestock
from those areas.
Project Range Management Activities
22.       The range development activities initiated under the Project would
support this long-term term policy through concentrated investments in water
and expanded veterinary services, as well as through the introduction of
improved range management techniques. Each of the three sub-projects would
have a well-staffed Range Management Department which would be responsible
for the implementation of range development policies in its area. The head
of the department would be an experienced range management expert; he would
in turn be assisted by a number of range assistants stationed throughout the
Project area. These staff would be complemented by range guards, enclosure
guards and water scouts, all of whom would be selected from among the communi-
ties in which they would work and who would be trained for several months by
the Project; together with the veterinary scouts, they would be responsible
for Project extension activities among the livestock owners. The Project
Range Management Departments would devise range use plans, establish maps
of land use categories, and plan grazing systems. By means of a constant
dialogue between the Project staff, the tribal authorities and the livestock
owners, it is expected that commttnity decisions regarding the establishment of
the ranches, the setting aside of grazing reserves and the development of stock
water can be made without undue difficulty. In essense therefore the
Project would involve a comprehensive non-formal educational effort based on
a constant interchange of ideas between the beneficiaries of Project services
and those charged with the responsibility of implementing them. It is hoped
that once the tribal communities in the range areas come to a better under-
standing of the forces which regulate their environment, they will increas-
ingly be able to assume control of the development activities.



ANNEX 3
Page 9
23.       Southern Rangelands.  The range management program in the Southern
Rangelands Project area would center around the establishment of the four back-
grounding and fattening ranches where improved range management techniques
would be introduced in relatively concentrated areas; it is hoped that once
the success of these practices has been demonstrated on the ranches, they would
then be adopted for extensive use by the nomads themselves. The development
of additional water resources would be given particular attention, as over 50%
of the range is now without stock water; in order to prevent uncontrolled de-
velopment, the water program would provide only about 1/3 of the resources
necessary for maximum use, and the program would be carefully, integrated with
the range management activities. Agreement has not yet been obtained for set-
ting aside dry season grazing reserves in the area, for although certain groups
more or less control specific grazing areas, they are also utilized by other
groups (particularly in times of drought); these agreements would be negotiated
during the course of the Project. The SORDU Range Management Department would
coordinate the activities of the range extension agents, the water scouts, and
the operation of the four ranches; this coordination would ensure that all the
activities directly affecting the status of the range areas would be fully
integrated.
24.       Jijigga.  The range management program to be initiated in the
Jijigga area would be somewhat more intricate than that being implemented in
the Southern Rangelands. This would be possible as the Somali tribes in the
area are relatively sophisticated livestock keepers who are able to exercise
a relatively high degree of control over range use. Therefore a somewhat
different approach is called for. In the first year a land use plan for the
Jijigga area would be drawn up and the land zoned into areas suitable for
cultivation, rangeland, fodder reserves and for settlement 1/. Within the
area to be gazetted as natural range, dry season grazing reserves would be
established based on both existing and new permanent watering points; these
reserves would be selected by the tribal groups in collaboration with JIRDU,
and agreements to this effect have already been signed. In principle these
reserves would be closed to all livestock except during the dry season, and
the grazing restrictions would be enforced by range guards selected from among
the tribes. As part of an effort to involve the individual clans in range
development, a system of range associations of stockowners would be established;
the recommended range policies would be discussed with these groups and they
would be made increasingly responsible for the overall development. Finally
two pilot fodder banks would be established to serve as a fodder reserve and
to test the efficacy of selected planting materials. These activities would
be carried out by the JIRDU Range Management Department.
1/ While this system can be enforced for the most part under existing
legislation, some supplemental legislation may also be necessary.



ANNEX 3
Page 10
25.       Northeast Rangelands.  The Northeast Rangelands area is only now
emerging from the recent drought which brought about widespread famine and
a drastic reduction in total livestock numbers. The drought also severely
damaged the rangelands themselves; large areas are bare of any vegetation,
and other areas are only now gradually beginning to recover with the aid
last year's rains. With the large reduction in the total stock numbers, the
ranges are now able - even with their greatly reduced carrying capacity - to
support the existing herds without undue pressure. This situation will not
prevail for long, however, as it is estimated that within 10 years the herds
will once again have been built up to their former levels. Consequently, un-
less some controls are achieved within this time, the ranges will once again
be over-stocked. This area thus offers a unique opportunity to institute im-
proved range management practices at a time when the immediate control of
numbers is not the most important consideration. As part of this strategy,
an inventory of each tribal grazing area would be made to serve as a guide
in the selection of range reserves. At full development some 15 dry season
grazing reserves totalling about 8,000 km2 would be established. All decisions
with regard to the opening and closing of these reserves would be made by
tribal authorities in cooperation with the NERDU Range Management Department.
Eight fodder banks averaging 10 ha in size to be used as fodder reserves in
time of drought would also be established. Finally, the NERDU Range Manage-
ment Department would carry out various studies on the use of enclosures,
vegetation, the development of fodder reserves, etc.
October 31, 1975



ANNEX 4
Page 1
ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Veterinary Services
A. The National Veterinary Service
1.        Overall responsibility for veterinary and animal health services
rests with the Department of Veterinary Services within the Ministry of
Agriculture. The Department has three main Divisions: Field and Extension,
Research and Laboratory and a Specialist Service (policy and financing) under
the Director General; there are also two additional service units - the
Tsetse Control Unit and the Meat Inspection Unit. The Field and Extension
Division administers the national veterinary field service; it has one or
two veterinary offices stationed in each province, supported by Animal Health
Assistants and vaccinators at the Awraja and Woreda level. Eight veterinary
clinics provide diagnostic facilities for this field staff. The Research and
Laboratory Division administers the central veterinary laboratory at Debre
Zeit and a regional laboratory in Asmara. These laboratories - particularly
the central unit - provide support for the diagnostic field units and produce
vaccines against Rinderpest, Bovine Pleuropneumonia, Pasteurellosis,
Buccellosis, Anthrax and (in Asmara) horse sickness. The Meat Inspection Unit
administers a national inspection service at all abbatoirs and at the port
at Assab, and also provides training for the meat inspectors.
2.        The national veterinary service now has a staff comprised of:
45   Ethiopian veterinarians;
40 expatriate veterinarians supplied under
technical assistance;
150 animal health assistants; and
700 vaccinators trained by the field service.
This level of staff is not sufficient to provide adequate veterinary services
throughout the country, and consequently the services provided in most areas
are at best rudimentary. The operating efficiency of this field staff is
constantly strained by insufficient operating funds, including inadequate
provision for transport vehicles and veterinary drugs and supplies. At
present, the staff are often unable to move from their stations due to lack
of transport, inadequate petrol or a lack of repair and maintenance facilities.
The annual budget for the national veterinary service has increased from



ANNEX 4
Page 2
Eth$ 1.5 to Eth$ 5 million over the past seven years, but this level of fund-
ing is still far from adequate to meet current needs. Operating budgets
will be further strained after 1976 when the UNDP JP-15 Rinderpest campaign
in Ethiopia is completed. At that time the campaign would have vaccinated
an estimated 20 million cattle, and made a serious contribution to the
eradication of this disease. However, unless the local veterinary service
is given sufficient manpower and funding to carry on the program, the
beneficial effects of the campaign would be totally lost within 3-5 years.
3.        In 1973, the Ministry of Agriculture issued a 20-year development
plan for the national veterinary service. Among the specific targets are:
(a) an increase in the number of field stations and diagnostic
facilities and the establishment of a field investigation
service;
(b) enlargement of the research and laboratory facilities at
Debre Zeit and Asmara with the establishment of a national
diagnostic unit, a Foot and Mouth Disease Institute and the
expansion of vaccine production capabilities;
5c) the establishment of a veterinary faculty and an expansion
of the Animal Health Assistants School at Debre Zeit;
(d) completion of the JP-15 Rinderpest control campaign as
well as the intensification of the Contagious Bovine
Pleuropneumonia survey and the start of a selective vaccina-
tion campaign against this disease; and
(e) completion of a Tsetse and Trypanosomiasis survey.
However, the successful implementation of this program will require a sig-
nificant expansion of the financial resources allocated to the veterinary
service, and in view of the large number of competing programs within the
agricultural and other sectors, it is not yet clear whether such an ambi-
tious program can be implemented.
B. Livestock Diseases
4.        Animal diseases represent one of the most important constraints to
increased livestock production in Ethiopia; the veterinary services provided
under the Project would therefore to a large extent be concerned with the
prevention and control of the major animal diseases, as well as the training
of the nomadic livestock owners in improved animal health practices. The
animal diseases now prevalent in the three sub-project areas are listed below:



ANNEX 4
Page 3
Cattle
(a) Rinderpest. In recent years, some 2.5% of the national herd
has been affected annually by this disease. The Africa-wide
JP-15 Joint Campaign for the eradication of rinderpest by a
series of compulsory but free vaccinations is progressing
and can be expected to cause a reduction in mortality rates.
The program has already been completed in Sidamo and
Jijigga Provinces, and is currently underway in the Northeast
Rangelands area.
(b) Contagious Bovine Pleuropneumonia (CBPP) is widespread and its
incidence continues to be aggravated by the movement of live-
stock without veterinary control. To the extent that vaccine
is available, vaccinations against CBPP are being given with
those for rinderpest during the JP-15 program, and this will
help control the disease.
(c) Foot and Mouth Disease is endemic to Ethiopia and types A, 0
and C have been identified. Mortality from this disease is
limited to non-immune young stock, but considerable production
losses and some abortions must be assumed. Typing and
experimental vaccine production is undertaken by the Debre Zeit
Laboratory, and a Foot and Mouth Disease Institute has been
commissioned for production on a larger scale. Due to the cost
involved, routine vaccination of cattle is now limited to high
grade dairy stock and stock entering certain meat factories,
and under the Project would be limited to animals moving into
backgrounding and fattening operations and stockroutes.
(d) Buccellosis has been investigated in the Southern Rangelands
area, and tests have been positive in 20% of the animals
examined. Indications are that the disease is not common in
the other two Project areas, and any breeding cattle moved out
of the Southern Rangelands should consequently be examined.
(e) Trypanosomiasis appears to be a special problem in the Southern
Rangelands area. In times of drought livestock owners, well
aware of the problem, have had to bring their cattle to the
river valleys where they have become infected. Surveys of the
affected areas bordering on the Project have been undertaken
and the Project veterinary service should therefore be alerted
to the problem.
(f) Endo- and Ectoparasites are widespread in all areas, but thus
far East Coast Fever has not been identified. Heavy infestations
can best be limited by treatment carried out by the livestock
owner himself, with advice from the veterinary scouts and with
the Project veterinary service providing the necessary drugs
and chemicals for sale to him.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
(g) Anthrax, Blackwater, Haemorrhagic Septicaemia are present
and sporadically cause locally severe losses.
(h) Internal parasites are a major cause of poor productivity
throughout the country. Eighty to ninety percent of all
livers at slaughterhouses are contaminated; probably 25%
of the national herd carries beef measles (with some 2% of
commercial slaughterings being totally condemned), and the
incidence of round worns is widespread. Campaigns by the
Veterinary Department to encourage farmers to treat their
cattle with available low cost drugs are unsuccessful be-
cause of the limited field service and inadequate distribu-
tion outlets for the drugs.
(i) Skin diseases. There are a variety of the common skin diesease
in Ethiopia contributing to reductions in the value of hides.
Sheep and Goats
(a) Tick-bone Diseases such as Babesiosis and Nairobi-Disease
have been identified in Jijigga and the Northeast Rangelands
and present a serious problem, with death losses rising to
about 70% of affected animals. Since individual treatment
cannot be considered under range conditions, the rational
approach is hand-dressing, spraying and dipping of the animals;
this is best carried out by the livestock owners themselves
assisted by the veterinary scouts with supplies of chemicals
sold by the veterinary service. Where large numbers of sheep
and goats move at the same time, collective dipping would be
organized (using mobile dip baths) by the district animal
health assistant.
(b) Haemorrhagic Septicaemia has been identified and a vaccine is
in production and may be effective in herds with a frequent
occurrent of outbreaks.
(c) Internal Parasites are a major contributor to poor production
levels with liver fluke being particularly serious in
certain areas (not including the Jijigga area). Nasal worms
are also a problem in some areas.
(d) Lameness. Continuous night paddocking in contaminated ground
perpetuates foot-rot and contributes to poor weight gains.
(e) Predators. Jackals and hyenas are serious problems in many
areas.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
Camels
(a)  Trypanosomiasis is by far the most serious diesease affecting
camels in the Project area. The effects of this disease are well
recognized by the nomads, who buy the drugs necessary to treat
their animals at very high costs; the drugs are, however, often
of low quality due to adulteration.
C. Project Veterinary Services
Expansion of the Laboratory at Debre Zeit
5.        Over the next five years it will be necessary to increase the
vaccine production capacity of the laboratory at Debre Zeit from the present
level of 30 million doses annually to 48 million doses; in addition to this
about 15 million doses of Foot and Mouth vaccine will be required. It is
also evident that a Central Diagnostic Unit is urgently needed. Government
has thus far allocated Eth$200,000 for the construction of a Foot and Mouth
Vaccine Institute, and is prepared to greatly increase (by about four times)
the annual budget for the Debre Zeit laboratory. French bilateral aid has
already provided the necessary equipment for both the FMD Vaccine Institute
and the Diagnostic Institute (valued at about Eth$500,000). Government has
requested that financing be included under the Project for the building for
the Diagnostic Institute; this would enable Government to concentrate its
available resources on the production of additional vaccine. In view of the
importance of assuring an adequate supply of vaccines to the Project, it
would be advisable for the Project to include assistance for the expansion
of the Debre Zeit facilities. Consequently, a sum of Eth$800,000 was included
under the Project for construction of the diagnostic laboratory at Debre Zeit.
This provision has been included under the capital costs of LMB Headquarters
Staff and one third of the cost attributed to each sub-project (Annex 15,
Tables 19, 38 and 59).
Project Veterinary Field Services
6.        A comprehensive Project veterinary service would be established
in each of the three sub-project areas in order to help to reduce livestock
losses attributable to disease and to improve the condition of those animals
marketed from the ranges. One of the most important functions of this
service, however, would be to develop the interest and capability on the part
of the livestock owners themselves to institute both better disease control
and improved livestock management practices. The veterinary staff at the
lower levels would also provide extension services aimed at introducing
range use controls adapted to the traditions and needs of the various tribal
units involved. The staff would be organized in such a way to best ensure
the active cooperation and participation of the tribal leaders and the stock-
owners.



ANNEX 4
Page 6
7.        The veterinary field staff in each sub-project area would consist
of a Senior Veterinarian, assisted by a Junior Veterinary Officer, Animal
Health Assistants, vaccinators and veterinary scouts. Thee Animal Health
Assistants would be located at field stations scattered throughout the Project
area, and would supervise the activities of the vaccinators and veterinary
scouts. The field stations would ensure that the veterinary staff had
sufficient mobility and supplies to adequately monitor their immediate
territory; the staff would identify outbreaks of disease, set up both routine
and emergency vaccination campaigns and organize the collective dipping of
small stock. The field station would be equipped with a vehicle and trailer
with a radio transceiver, along with sufficient equipment and supplies (See
Table 1). The stations would be one of the most important focal points in
the implementation of the entire Project, as these would be the primary
outpost in direct contact with the stockowners.
8.        One of the most important tools in the Project veterinary program
would be the veterinary scout - a new level of staff not previously utilized
by the national veterinary service. In the past the nomads have been most
reluctant to partake of Government services of any kind, as they have been
extremely fearful that to do so would ultimately work to their own dis-
advantage. The post of veterinary scout would therefore be created as part
of a determined effort to actively involve the nomads in the development
process. These veterinary scouts would be selected from among the tribal
community they would be expected to serve, and would be given a 1-2 month
training course at the Project training centers (Annex 12). They would then
return to live and work within their respective communities, travelling
with them wherever they might go. On the average, it is expected that there
would be a ratio of 1 veterinary scout for each 400 nomadic families. Vete-
rinary scouts would be expected to carry out simple preventative treatments
(i.e. tick control, dehorning, castration, treatment of wounds, etc.) and
to give the necessary innoculations. They would receive a small subsistence
allowance plus an allowance for a camel to carry equipment. In addition
to these veterinary duties, the veterinary scout would also serve as a sort
of limited extension agent, helping to familiarize the people with the
objectives of the Project, and helping to introduce the various range mange-
ment and range control practices to be instituted by the Project. The active
participation of the nomad is essential for the success of these latter
programs, and the ability of these scouts to create a favorable climate for
their introduction will be crucial. Veterinary scouts would be supervised
by a vaccinator located at a field station who would keep him supplied with
the necessary drugs and chemicals for sale to stockowners.
9.        The details of the specific veterinary services to be provided in
each of the three sub-project areas are given below.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
Southern Rangelands (Annex 15, Tables 3 and 4)
10.       A veterinary extension service consisting of two veterinarians,
9 Animal Health Assistants (AHA), 9 vaccinators and 140 veterinary scouts
would be established throughout the range area. The Senior Veterinarian in
charge of the Veterinary Department would be located at Yabello. Permanent
field stations would be established at five locations - Teltalle, Dolo, Mega,
Moyale and Wachillo. Each of the veterinarians would be assisted by a single
AHA and two vaccinators, and one AHA and one vaccinator would be posted to
each of the five field stations. A diagnostic unit would be set up at Yabello
under the direction of an AHA, and another AHA would be posted to an existing
diagnostic unit at Neghelli.
Jijigga (Annex 15, Tables 24 and 25)
11.       Veterinary staff would include 2 veterinarians, 7 Animal Health
Assistants, 8 vaccinators and 100 veterinary scouts. A central veterinary
station would be set up at Jijigga complete with a diagnostic unit, and
field stations would be established at five locations throughout the
Project area. These field stations would be equipped with mobile sheep dips,
hand sprays, scales, drugs and other equipment. One AHA would be stationed
in each of five field stations, and 2 at Jijigga - including one in charge
of the diagnostic unit. In addition to the above, four cattle dips would
be established at various locations including Jijigga as a pilot operation
to test their effectiveness.
Northeast Rangelands (Annex 15, Tables 43 amd 44)
12.       The veterinary extension service would be under the direction of a
Senior Veterinarian stationed at headquarters in Alomata. Additional staff
would include a Junior Veterinarian, 4 Animal Health Assistants (AHA), 8 vac-
cinators, and 60 veterinary scouts. Four field stations, each under an AHA,
would be established at selected Project Development Centers (PDC's) throughout
the Project area. Smaller substations, each headed by a vaccinator, would be
set up at the remaining four PDC's. These field stations would be equipped
with large stores of veterinary supplies, mobile dips, spray facilities,
crushes, etc.

ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Water Development
A. General Ethiopian Background
1.        In many parts of the Ethiopian rangelands a lack of well-distributed
and adequate water supplies is one of the most important factors in determining
annual and seasonal patterns of human and livestock movement. The availability
of water and sufficient grazing in proximity to it are crucial to the survival
of the pastoralist people, and consequently disputes over water supplies are
frequent. and fierce. Long watering intervals of up to three days in the case
of cattle, and more in the case of smaller stock and camels are common in many
range areas of Ethiopia. However, in spite of this, in the dry season the
labor requirements necessary to lift and distribute water to livestock may be
very high and may effectively limit the livestock/human population ratio.
2.        The Ethiopian Government with the assistance of various international
agencies has made various attempts in the past to develop the water resources
of the rangeland areas. In southern Ethiopia about twenty stockponds were
constructed and a further twenty stockponds and six boreholes have more recent-
ly been developed as part of the Yabello Pilot Project. In the Jijigga area,
boreholes have been sunk in at least seventeen different places, and others
are still being developed. Experience with these programs has been mixed.
In some cases too little attention was paid to the possible effects of addi-
tional water supplies on the balance between livestock and feed resources,
while in other cases apparently insufficient consultation took place with local
people prior to the development; as a consequence the new water supplies were
either not used or quickly became unserviceable. In the case of the boreholes,
often either insufficient attention was given to the question of servicing
them, or the arrangements made broke down.
3.        In the past, responsibility for water development in the range
areas has been dispersed among a number of Government agencies. Since 1971
prime responsibility has rested with the Water Resources Commission, and its
Executive Agency, although geological surveys and investigations are still the
responsibility of the Ministry of Mines. The Executive Agency of the Water
Resources Commission is being strengthened, but it will be some time before it
is able to meet the large number of demands for water development services.
4.        Construction of water facilities (stockponds, dams, boreholes etc.)
is limited by the equipment and expertise that exist in Ethiopia. The Ministry
of Agriculture has some earth-moving machinery at Yabello in the Southern
Rangelands area; the Executive Agency of the Water Resources Commission has a
number of drilling rigs also in that area, but these are mostly old and in



ANNEX 5
Page 2
poor condition, and drilling speeds are very slow. Some private drilling
companies are currently operating in Ethiopia, but their number and efficiency
fluctuate. Drilling costs recently quoted by private companies have been
very high in relation to costs in other countries (e.g. Kenya).
B. The Project
I. Southern Rangelands
5.        A shortage of water is the most important limiting factor in the
development of the Southern Rangelands area. There are in all about 600
existing water points of various sizes (mostly wells), but most of these
are of use only for short periods and can handle only a certain number of
stock. Some limited development of this area is now underway. A number of
ponds have been constructed as part of the Yabello Pilot Project, and Govern-
ment has recently signed a water development agreement with USAID which calls
for the colstruction of 10 stock ponds of 20,000 m3 and 30 smaller stock ponds
of 6,000 m  (dug-out capacity); the first of these facilities are already under
construction.
6.        This development would be significantly expanded over the Project
period. It was estimated that in addition to the ponds already under con-
struction by USAID - 15 large stock ponds (20,000 m3) and 220 smaller ponds
(16,000 m3) would be required to ensure more efficient use of the available
grazing areas. The larger ponds hold water throughout the dry season and
are important in opening up new areas for dry weather grazing. The smaller
ponds hold sufficient water to prolong the wet weather grazing for one or
two months into the dry season before the stock must move on; these ponds
are grouped around the larger ones in an approximate ratio of 10:1 to facili-
tate grazing control. The development program would have to be closely
integrated with the range management program in order to prevent over-grazing
of hitherto protected areas; a further safeguard would exist by virtue of the
fact that the number of water points being provided is only about one-third
the number required for full development.
7.        An estimate of the costs involved in the Southern Rangelands water
development construction program is given in Table 1. These include
only recurrent costs, as it is estimated that the Government equipment al-
ready being utilized by USAID in their on-going program would be sufficient
to carry out the remaining construction. A list of this equipment is given
below:

years 2 through 5. It is expected that the followina local staff (now
employed by USAID) would be employed by the Project:

II. Jijigga
8.        During the rains there is generally sufficient water to allow
livestock easy access to whatever grazing is available. During the inter-
mediate period, however, there are times when water is scarce in areas
which offer plentiful grazing; this is mainly due to the fact that the earth
tanks and depressions are porous and do not hold water for long once the
rain stops. However, the introduction of impermeable berekedus (water tanks)
which will hold water for two or three months after the last rainfall is
rapidly changing this situation. The introduction of berekedus has been
particularly important in the eastern edge of the Project area, where other
sources of water are most difficult and relatively expensive to obtain.
9.        A general outline of the existing water sources in the Jijigga
area is given below. These include:



ANNEX 5
Page 4
(a) About 9 operating boreholes (many more are disused or were
abandoned as unsuccessful).
(b)  Berekedus (Birkas) - water tanks probably averaging 180 m 3
but of up to 3,000 m3 capacity. Approximately 3,500 of the
berekedus in the Project area are concrete-lined, and 3,000
are simply dug in fairly impermeable limestone. Some
berekedus hold water well into, and even throughout, the
dry season. They are (almost all) privately owned and
water is sold from them at prices ranging from Eth$2.50 per
m3 or less when water is plentiful, to Eth$15 per m3 or
more when it is scarce. Berekedus are concentrated in a
strip about 100 km long and 30 km wide, running parallel to
and along the border with Somalia, where soil conditions are
favorable for the fairly simple technology being used.
(c) Shallow wells. Watson estimated that there were more than
20,000 of these in the Project area in January 1973. However,
many of them only yield small quantities of water, and for
only part of the year. They are heavily concentrated in the
cultivated areas around Fugnanbira and east and south of
Jijigga, and along the Dakata, Faban and Gerer rivers.
Water Potential
10.       In determining the water development potential of the area, ref-
erence must be made to four physiographic units; the prospects for water
development in each area are relatively homogeneous.
Area 1.   East of the Jijigga-Dagabur road.  A flat undissected area under-
lain by thick Jessoma sandstone dipping southeast. Near the margins of the
sandstone (roughly coincident with the Jijigga-Dagabur road) penetration
into the Antalo limestone group may yield ground water and boreholes have
been sited at several points. This area's water resources, however, lie
mainly in surface catchment constructions, and construction of concrete-lined
berekedus, should be suitably protected from evaporation, favored.
Area 2.   West of the Jijigga-Dagabur road, the area is predominantly under-
lain by the Antalo limestone group (limestone with subordinate shales and
sandstones). The area is dissected by three main ephemeral rivers, the
Tug Dacata, Tug Fafan and Tug Gerer, all flowing south-southeast to the Wabe
Shebelli south of the Project area. The best opportunities for obtaining
water are probably along the major river courses.
Area 3.   North of the Babile-Jijigga road, the area is characterized by
extensive outcrops of basement complex (granites, schists, gneisses) with
relatively narrow incised drainage valleys. The headwaters of the main
southerly flowing rivers are located in this area. It is a poor source of
ground water except beneath wadi deposits where there is a recharge element.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
Area 4.   North of the Jijigga-Tug Wajale road, this area has a rolling
topography of low hills and broad valleys. Small but prominent hills are
volcanic in origin, but the bulk of the area is underlain by the Antalo
limestone group, below which occurs Adigrat sandstone resting on an irregular
basement surface. Boreholes have been reasonably successful, though there
have been some water quality problems. A trial borehole is being located at
Haroresa in this area.
11.       The water facilities to be provided under the Project are detailed
in Table 2. The costs of this program are given in Annex 15, Tables 26 and
27. These facilities have been selected on the basis of a long-term water
program which has been drawn up for the Jijigga area. It would include:
(a) development water supplies in the cultivated areas to
provide water for the smallholder fattening and back-
grounding schemes (Annex 9);
(b) construction of permanent water supplies in the
major dry season grazing reserves;
(c) construction of a few additional water supplies outside
the dry season grazing reserves to protect them from
over-use in times of inadequate rainfall.
The drilling of the Project boreholes would be carried out under contract;
the wells would be constructed by the JIRDU Technical Department, which
would also be responsible for the operation and maintenance of all completed
facilities.
III. Northeast Rangelands
12.       The Northeast Rangelands Project area covers about 75,000 km  in the
Wollo and Tigre Administrative Regions. The whole area may be classified as
semi-arid with four more-or-less distinct seasons. Numerous rivers drain
into the area, some of which are perennial throughout most of their length
within the Project area, and some seasonal. The highlands to the west drain
into the Awash River Basin, the Teiru and Danakil Depressions and other
enclosed drainage basins. The Awash delta, the Teiru depression and some
minor catchment areas provide the major dry season grazing reserves. Else-
where, such as in the south of the Project area and north of the Golina
River, the grazing areas are of a very low potential with extensive moonscape
areas of lava flows and volcanic ash. Thus, unlike the Jijigga and the Southern
Rangelands areas, there are virtually no areas capable of improvement by the
provision of water for stock. The very dry desert volcanic areas can only be
used during the rains when pools are available. The dry weather grazing must
thus depend on restricted grazing areas which by their nature have water
available all year round. In those areas where there is sufficient grazing,
the current water availability is adequate to meet the needs of the livestock



ANNEX 5
Page 6
normally located there. The Government Water Development Authority is,
however, instituting a tube well boring program to provide water for human
consumption.
Control over Construction and Use of Water Supplies
13.       In many areas (for example, Jijigga) the introduction of addi-
tional permanent water supplies is threatening to destroy grazing areas
which were hitherto exposed to only seasonal pressure. Thus it has been
demonstrated that the unregulated introduction of water facilities can,
where it is not accompanied by concurrent restrictions on the numbers of
animals entitled to use them, have a serious long-term detrimental effect
on the overall condition of the range. While the range management programs
being instituted under the Project are expected to help alleviate the pres-
sure on available feed and water resources, the program cannot hope to
succeed if the provision of water in any given area is not tightly controlled
by the Project authority. Consequently, the National Water Resources Commission
has agreed that once the Project Units (SORDU, JIRDU and NERDU) are established,
they would be appointed by Government as the water authority in each of the
three areas; they would thus have the power to regulate and license existing
privately owned water facilities and to control the development of any new
facilities.
July 22, 1975

Page 1
ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Northeast Rangelands
Irrigation by Water Spreading
A. General
1.        There are no accurate estimates of the present extent of irrigated
agriculture in Ethiopia, but the total net area irrigated annually probably
lies between 200,000 and 300,000 hectares; commercial agriculture (defined
here as covering farms in excess of 10 ha) accounts for between 50,000 and
100,000 hectares, and peasant agriculture for the balance. The main crops
grown by the commercial farmers are sugar, cotton and fruit. Peasant farmers
grow mainly cereals, but also some other food crops and cotton. The Awash
River Basin, south of the Project area, is the site of the most extensive area
of large-scale commercial irrigation in Ethiopia. No irrigated development
has taken place within the Project area itself with the exception of simple
water spreading programs now being successfully developed as part of the
Northeast Rangelands sub-project of an IDA-financed Drought Rehabilitation
Project started in 1974. The most fully developed of these (at Weranzo, see
Map) operated on a limited scale in 1974, and successfully established experi-
mental crops of cotton, maize, sorghum and pigeon peas.
The Project Area
2.        The Project area, although itself an area of low rainfall, lies at
the bottom of an escarpment to the east of and below a narrow strip of east-
ward sloping steep land, the western edge of which rises to altitudes of 2,500
to 4,000 meters: this strip of land has a mean annual rainfall of between
1,000 and 1,400 mm in the south to 700-900 mm in the north, and forms a catch-
ment area four hundred kilometers long and ten to sixty kilometers wide, all
of which drains into the Project area. Eight malor rivers (the Borkenna,
Mille, Ua/Loggiya, Golina/Ialla, Alamata, Hair Sablette, Shiket, Adi Aro/
Berber) and their tributaries stretch back into and drain this catchment area.
Of these, the Borkenna, Mille and lalla are perennial throughout all or most
of this length within the Project area. Other rivers are seasonal, being dry
over the bulk of the year, but experiencing silt-laden flash floods some 6 to
8 times a year which might vary from 5 to more than 30 hours each. Once the
rivers leave the escarpment at the western edge of the Project area, they tend
to become so deeply incised that diversion becomes expensive. In the Awash
basin south of the Weranzo River, the land is fairly broken so that there are
only very small areas of irrigable land that can be brought under irrigation
from a single offtake point. However, the plains around the Lower Weranzo,
upper Mille, Ua and lalla rivers, and the whole area of the Teiru Depression
appear to offer very good opportunities for commanding large areas of irriga-



ANNEX 6
Page 2
ble land from a single offtake point. Further north of the Teiru Depression,
no reconnaissance surveys have been carried out as yet, but the Shiket and
Kala plains appear to be areas wlhere several hundred hectares could be brought
under irrigation from single offtake points.
3.        In early 1973, Sir Alexander Gibb and Partners carried out a recon-
naissance survey of the original Northeast Rangelands project area, NERP1,
(see Map) for LMB. In late 1973, Intermediate Technology Services carried
out another short study of specific irrigation sites in NERPI. Also in 1973,
Peter Manning (of LMB) completed a study on the hydrogeology of NERP1 and its
potential for irrigation.
Soils
4.        Little is known about the suitability of the soils in the Project
area for irrigation. Close to the Awash river, soil surveys have been carried
out by FAO in the early nineteen sixties and by Gibb. While large areas of
land with soil suitable for irrigation were found, other large areas with
salinity and other soil problems were also found. At two selected irrigation
sites (Weranzo and Jeldi) within the part of the Project area that lies within
the Awash catchment, two soils samples were analyzed and no salinity or other
serious soil problems were found. Soils were mainly silt with a relatively
low clay content (10% to 15%), implying a high permeability and consequently
rapid infiltration. Grass grows abundantly on some possible irrigation sites
after flooding. Along the western edge of the Project area, individual Afars,
often in partnership with non-Afars, have made spasmodic attempts to start
farming. Traditional Afar attitudes look down on cropping, but the catas-
trophe suffered by the Afar tribe over the past few years has further weakened
traditional values and made Afar society more receptive to new ways of life.
Experience in the Assayita region of the Lower Awash Valley has shown that the
Afars are both willing and capable of undertaking irrigated farming.
B. Project Investments
5.        The irrigation component: of the Northeast Rangelands sub-project
would have three elements: the development of irrigated cropping areas; the
development of water dispersion areas to be used as improved pastures by the
Afars; and feasibility studies of the Teiru Depression and the Ialla River
Scheme.
I. Irrigated Cropping
6.        The Gibbs report investigated the possibilities of irrigation and
water storage in the catchment area of the Awash Valley Authority's (AVA) zone
of activities and in the associated highland areas. The rePort found that irri-
gated development such as the water spreading program proposed under the Project
would not affect the supply of irrigation water to the AVA. Under the Project
some 2,000 ha of irrigated crops would be developed through water spreading.
This would be done by constructing barriers 3-4 meters high of wire-mesh stone-
filled gabions or baskets around 4 x 3 x 2 meters. These would be placed across



ANNEX 6
Page 3
flash flood river beds. Simple diversion canals would be dug by hand to divert
impounded flood waters onto nearby level areas. Cropping areas would be divided
by 1.0 meter high earth bunds. The crest of silt-laden flood waters descending
the rivers from the high rainfall catchments in the highlands would pass over
the gabion barriers, but the following, less turbulent, flood waters would
fill successive bunded fields to a depth of a meter until the flood subsidies.
Crops are planted on seed beds after the flood water is absorbed. The crops
are subsequently reflooded as and when further floods appear.
7.        The Project would provide funds for (see Annex 15, Tables 45 and
46):
(a) wages for the Afars for earth moving and weir construction;
(b) salaries of engineers, surveyors and supervisory staff;
(c) a small amount of earth moving machinery to supplement the
intensive hand labor and necessary transport;
(d) equipment such as tools, wire mesh gabions and water pumps;
(e) loan funds for farmers to purchase seeds, fertilizer and
tools.
8.        This type of flood irrigation is erratic both in time and quantity
but crops such as maize, sorghum, pigeon peas, beans, sweet potatoes, fodder
crops and grasses show good promise. Yields will be variable with occasional
failures due to lack of floods. Consequently, yield estimates are conservative
(see para 18).
9.        Detailed plans and designs for these water spreading schemes are
not necessary, as no concrete or other permanent structures are involved.
The Irrigation Engineer would survey possible sites, prepare surveyed plans
with his survey team and produce necessary drawings, showing canal and field
sites and the placing of the stone gabion diversion walls. Experience in the
Aden area has shown that attempts to establish more permanent concrete weirs
and supply canals have little chance of success, as shifts in river beds and
occasional very large floods render them useless. The more simple gabion
weir and earth canal can, however, be repaired easily by the farmers themselves
once experience has been gained.
Development of the Irrigation Program
10.       The Project development program would be divided into two phases.
The first two years of the Project period would be devoted to completing the
1,000 ha water spreading program initiated as part of the Northeast Rangelands
component of the IBRD Drought Rehabilitation Project. At the same time, how-
ever, preliminary site selection and planning for the sites to be developed
under financing provided by the proposed Project would take place. The actual
sites can be determined only after the detailed site surveys are carried out,
but 10 sites suitable for surveying have already been identified.

11.       Once the actual irrigation sites have been identified, negotiations
would be opened with the tribal authorities within whose traditional grazing
areas the sites are located. 1/ These negotiations would determine:
(a) which individuals within the tribes would be allocated 1 ha
plots for cropping; and
(b) which individuals (or clans) would have access to the water
dispersion areas (para 19)
Traditional tribal methods of communal ownership of irrigated land holdings
developed by the Afars in the Awash would be encouraged. This is a system
whereby the individual farmer of a given plot is actually the appointee of the
tribal group; a "malak" is appointed by the group who would organize irriga-
tion rotation, ensure that essential maintenance work on the canals is carried
out, issue seeds and fertilizers and referee disputes. Any profits derived
by an individual farmer would be shared in an agreed upon ratio by the tribe
as a whole.
12.       During year 3 of the Project, work would begin on the sites previous-
ly identified (para 10). The Design Engineer would be responsible for survey-
ing the areas and carrying out soil tests. The construction of the irrigation
sites would be the responsibility of the Irrigation Engineer; he would be
assisted by paid labor recruited from among the eventual land users. Although
individual sites would range in size from 5 to 500 ha, the average site would

Maintenance and Extension
14.       Once constructed, the irrigation sites would be the responsibility
of the land users, who, through the organization of their "malak", would
maintain their own sites. However, in the case of major damage, through
flood for example, the "malak" can call on the services of the Project Irriga-
tion Engineer, who would inspect the damage and would be able to give free
technical assistance. Any materials or manpower required would be provided
by the farmers, or against payment. During the rest of his time, the Irriga-
tion Engineer would advise "malaks" on water distribution and site maintenance.
Crop Production
15.       After construction is completed, the farmers selected to crop the
individual 1 ha plots would be employed by the Project for the first cropping
season; during that time they would receive training in crop husbandry from
Project extension agents. Agricultural inputs including seed, insecticides
and sacking would be provided on credit to each farmer. An estimate of the
cost of these inputs is given below:

16.       Cotton.  Cotton is likely to remain the most profitable cash crop
that can be grown under water spreading irrigation, due both to its compara-
tive adaptability and the experience which has already been gained with it by
Afars in the Awash Valley irrigation areas. The date of planting in the
Project area would be determined by the occurrence of the first flood in
July or August. The seed bed would be prepared by ploughing twice by oxen
following the pre-planting irrigation, and planted by hand immediately to
avoid drying out. Two hand weedings and two inter-row cultivations using oxen
would be recommended to the farmers. Pests, notably American, Pink and Spiny
Bollworm, are the most serious threats to cotton in the lower Awash, with
aphids, jassids and whiteflies also important. Provision would be made in
the Project to provide funds for sprays and chemicals to assist in their
control.



ANNEX 6
Page 7
17.       Food Crops.  The exact mix of food crops to be grown by each family
would vary somewhat according to individual preferences, although the average
mix would be much the same. In spite of the strong comparative advantage to
be gained from production of cotton, the Afar farmers - like most peasant
smallholders - show a strong propensity to first ensure their own subsistence
requirements and only then to give attention to cash crops.

II. Dispersion Areas
19.       Associated with each irrigation site would be a dispersion area
where any diverted water surplus to requirements in the basins would be
directed. Each 100 ha irrigation site would have a 50 ha dispersion area,
with contour ditching to encourage seepage; this area would be developed as
improved pasture. The local indigenous star grass (Cyondon spp.,) would
initially be established by planting runners. This grass can stand a measure
of maltreatment, has considerable powers of recuperation, and planting material
for vegetative propagation is locally available. While this pasture is in
production it is expected to support 10 livestock units per ha (i.e., 500 l.u.
per irrigation site for a minimum of four months); control of grazing on this
area would be the responsibility of the "malak". While most of the livestock
utilizing this area would be selected from among the traditional pastoralist
herds, it is also anticipated that some sheep would be fattened in this area
using the surplus water dispersion areas as part of the smallholder fattening
program. These fattening trials would be supervised by the extension agents.
III. Feasibility Studies
20.       Under the Project feasibility studies would be carried out to deter-
mine the optimum potential utilization of the Teiru Depression and of the
Ialla River Scheme.
21.       The Teiru (Barantu) Depression is a vast enclosed drainage basin
of about 10,000 km2 which floods during the wet season. This site was iden-
tified during Project preparation as a possible site for a large-scale water
spreading program. Apart from its great importance for future irrigation
development, however, the area (now that the flood plains and grasslands of
the Awash area have been developed for commetcial irrigation) is the biggest
and most constant dry season grazing reserves remaining to the Afars. The
area is essential to the survival of the Afars, and to remove such an area
from the transhumant grazing rotation would have the effect of greatly reducing
the productivity of the rest of the associated rangelands, thus jeopardizing
the herds, and therefore the lives of the pastoralists for whom it is a vital
and strategic reserve. On the other hand, to ignore such a fertile and well-
watered basin would be to ignore one of the highest potential areas of the
rangelands; such a decision would also invite indiscriminate development to
the detriment of the pastoralist economy. The Project would therefore carry
out a study to investigate the feasibility of irrigated development in the
depression for the benefit of the present land users. The feasibility study
would begin in year 2 to allow time for the collection of some hydrological
data and a review of this data in relation t,o the already available
climatological information. Should the additional information show the
resources to be inadequate, the study would be taken no further. In any case,
provision has been made for a team of consultants to undertake the feasibility
study. The team would be led by a study leader (12 months) and would include



ANNEX 6
Page 9
a sociologist (six months); a range ecologist (three months); a soil scientist
(three months); a hydrologist (three months); an agricultural economist (six
months); two irrigation engineers (six months each) to investigate storage
sites and to design the irrigation works, and two LMB surveyors (six months
each) to assist them.
22.       In addition to the Teiru study, a feasibility study would be made
of the Ialla River storage scheme (identified by Gibbs in 1973). The approach
would be the same as above, and as the two studies would be carried out as
one contract, they would use the same staff, facilities and share some
common services. Consultants' time on the Ialla River Feasibility Study
would be 36 man months.
July 20, 1975
