ETHIOPIA
RANGELAND DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Fattening and Backgrounding Ranches
1.        One of the most important objectives of the proposed Project is to
encourage the nomadic livestock owners to restrict the numbers of their herds
to levels consistent with the carrying capacity of the ranges. The ranches
to be established in the southern rangelands area would support this objec-
tive by providing an assured outlet for surplus stock from the range areas;
they would thus represent a first step in the stratification of the Ethiopian
livestock industry. By offering an even flow of animals both for possible
fattening by the feedlot and by smallholders in the Awassa area, and for sale
on local markets, they would be able to offer higher prices to the local
cattle owners. These higher prices would provide a strong incentive for
reduced stock numbers, and would also result in a significant increase in
the cash incomes of one of the poorest segments of the Ethiopian population.
The ultimate goal of the Project is to increase offtake from the ranges from
the present level of about 6% to around 12%. The achievement of this objec-
tive will however depend not only on improved prices to the cattle owners,
but also on the development of an integrated cattle industry. The cattle
fattening operations supported by the Project would provide another link
in this overall development effort, as would the market and stockroute system
now being developed in the Project areas as part of the IDA-supported Second
Livestock Development Project.
2.        Under the Project provision would be made for the establishment of
up to four 25,000 ha "growing out" or fattening ranches on the Borana plateau
in the southern rangelands area. The ranches would be established in four
different areas, each controlled by separate tribal groups. Three of the
sites have been identified (Did Hara, Dedertu and Kajima), and agreesents of
intent signed with the authorities of the tribes having traditional grazing
rights over the ranch area. Negotiations with regard to the suggested site
of a possible fourth ranch (at Wollesa Leman) are currently underway, although
any decision to proceed with the actual development of the ranch would be
subject to the approval of the Bank. The ranches would remain the traditional
preserve of the pastoralists, although LMB would provide all the capital
improvement necessary for each ranch, and would also be solely responsible
for ranch management for a five-year period. At the end of this period (or
at some other mutually convenient time) LMB would hand over responsibility
for operation of the ranches to the pastoralists themselves. It is envisioned
that the ranches would be operated by the nomads as a cooperative society under
Ethiopian law, although the exact form of ownership would be determined at a
later date.
3.        The ranch design would be based on experience gained from similar
commercial ranches in comparable ecological zones elsewhere in East Africa



ANNEX 7
Page 2
and on the information derived from the operation of the Ministry of Agricul-
ture's pilot grazing control scheme at Yabello. A simplified rotational
grazing control scheme much like that employed at the Yabello pilot project
would be introduced; perimeter fencing with simple internal firebreaks and
night paddocks (initially protected by thorn bushes and eventually to be
fenced) would be established. In addition, those water facilities necessary
to allow the ranch to fully utilize the potential carrying capacity of the
range would be established; the water facilities would vary from a borehole
to stock ponds and small water holes. Veterinary services would be provided
by the Project veterinary service under the direction of the Senior Veterina-
rian at Yabello; these services would include regular dipping and innoculation
against the major diseases, and all cattle would be carefully checked both
on arrival at the ranch and prior to disposition. No pasture improvement
would be carried out, as experience elsewhere indicates that the incremental
returns from these activities do not justify the relatively large expenditures
involved. The ranches would be managed by trained Ethiopians under the over-
all direction of the Range Management Director.
Ranch Development
4.        Development would begin on one ranch per year for four years; the
development program has been made intentionally flexible, however, so that
the implementation schedule could be adjusted to meet changing conditions.
If it is feasible to develop all four ranches, this will be done; if on the
other hand the rate of development is slower than expected, the money allocated
for the fourth ranch would be transferred to other operations. An approxima-
tion of the expected level of both capital and operating costs is detailed in
Annex 15, Tables 5 and 6. Capital costs would necessarily vary from one
ranch to another, but they would generally represent the minimum which is
consistent with good animal husbandry practices in that area and which would
allow the ranch to develop as a viable commercial enterprise. Basic invest-
ments would include fencing, stock ponds, a borehole, access roads, tracks
and firebreaks, night enclosures, handling yards, ramps, vehicles and equip-
ment. Recurrent costs would be funded out of income and would include salaries,
vehicle running costs and maintenance.
5.        The ranches would purchase young male cattle from the pastoralists
in the surrounding area. The animals would be between 2 and 3 years of age
and would have an average liveweight of about 150 kg.  Payment for the animals
would be made in two stages.  A first payment equal to the current market
price plus a 5% bonus would be made at the time of the sale. Each animal
would spend an average of one year on the ranch with an average weight gain
of 100 kg, thus bringing the animal. to a final weight of approximately 250
kg. The animals would average a weight gain of 300 grams/day as compared
with an average gain of 150 gm/day for animals on the unimproved range. Once
the animal had reached the desired weight it would be available for sale to
the feedlot or to smallholders in the Awassa area for finishing. At the time
of disposition of the animal, the original owner would receive a second
payment reflecting the difference between the buying and the selling price
after operating expenses and a contribution to a sinking fund (to finance
debt repayments, improvement or expansion of the ranch) had been allowed for.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
6.        Given the projected carrying capacity of I LU/7 ha, the four ranches
would have an estimated throughput about 16,000 head by the end of year 5
(Table 1). About 8,000 of these animals would be sold to the feedlot, and
approximately 2,000 would be sold to smallholders in the Awassa area for
fattening on crop residues. About 1,000 additional animals would be trans-
ported to the Awassa area where they would graze on stover on land belonging
to the Awassa Agro-Industrial Share Company Farm (the farm on which the
feedlot would be located) for several weeks; at this point the animals would
be sold on the local market. Finally a limited number of other types of
animals (which the ranch would necessarily have to accept from the nomads in
order to guarantee a supply of the younger animals) would also be sold on the
local markets. The commercial feedlots are expected to show interest in pur-
chasing grown-out animals from the ranches, and they would be offered any suit-
able animals which were in excess of the requirements of the Project's feedlot.
Prices
7.        For purposes of this analysis, it has been estimated that cattle
would be purchased from local stock owners at an average price of 48 cents/kg
liveweight. Current prices for comparable animals in local markets range
between 42 and 45 cents/kg liveweight. The higher price used here reflects
the 5% bonus over current market price (para 5) plus an allowance for a
projected 1% increase in real cattle prices in Ethiopia during the period
1975-1980. From 1980-1985 prices in real terms for meat and meat products
are expected to increase by 2.4% both in Ethiopia and on the world market.
For that period and the remainder of the life of the Project, purchase prices
paid by the ranches have been estimated at 53 cents/kg liveweight. It is
likewise estimated that the average price of cattle sold from the ranches
would be about 69 cents/kg liveweight for the years 1976-1980. This price
is based on present market prices for comparable animals (which average between
60 and 66 cents/kg liveweight) and also reflects the projected real increase
in cattle prices. A price of 76 cents/kg liveweight has likewise been used
for the years 1981 and thereafter to reflect additional real price increases
expected in the future.
Benefits and Justification
8.        The ranches to be established as part of the Southern Rangelands
Project would provide a valuable demonstration of the advantages of commercial
range management and improved animal husbandry techniques. They would also
enable the stock owners to obtain substantially higher prices for their stock
than would otherwise have been the case--particularly when the second payment
is taken into account. The ranches by offering the nomads an alternative
to sale to local traders would help promote competition in the primary markets;
this would have the added benefit of making local prices more attractive
vis-a-vis prices offered across the border in Kenya, and would thus help to
reduce the outflow of cattle from the Project area. The major quantifiable
benefits from the ranches would be an increase in the offtake of young animals
from the national herd, incremental meat production due to the substantial
weight gain of the cattle on the ranches, and the higher prices accruing to
the cattle owners.



ANNEX 7
Page 4
9.        At full development (year 6), 16,000 head at an average of 250 kg
liveweight would be sold from the ranches each year. The benefits derived
from the ranches would be distributed to some 8-10,000 extended nomadic
families now living in the Project area; these people represent some of the
poorest of all Ethiopian families (certainly in the lowest 20% of all income
groups) who have until this time received virtually no development assistance
from any source whatever.
Economic Rate of Return
10.       Commercial ranches normally show a low rate of return on development
costs. However, preliminary calculations indicate that the proposed ranches
would have an economic rate of return of 17% over a twenty-year period. This
relatively high figure can be attributed to the comparatively low cost of the
purchased animals and the modest level of development to be carried out.
July 22, 1975

ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Feedlot Unit
Objectives
1.        The Project would include a pilot feedlot to be established at the
Awassa Agro-Industrial Share Company (AAISC) farm. The feedlot operation
would be designed to (a) assist in the stratification of the Ethiopian live-
stock industry by providing a regular supply of high value meat for both
export and local markets; (b) provide higher prices for local producers
through the development of new market outlets for quality beef; and (c) serve
as a pilot operation to provide information on the feasibility of establishing
feedlots elsewhere in Ethiopia. In the short term, the fattening of cattle
represents one of the simplest and most efficient means of increasing national
beef production. The economic aspects of intensive beef fattening have been
examined in considerable detail over the past five years by an FAO/UNDP pro-
ject near Nakuru in Kenya, and much additional information has been generated
by several commercial feedlots now operating elsewhere in Kenya. The proposed
feedlot would draw on the experience gained from these operations, as well as
from the two small-scale commercial feedlots recently established within
Ethiopia.
2.        The main objective of the proposed feedlot would be to add value
and weight to immature cattle through an intensive feeding regime. The
guarantee of a regular supply of high quality meat would enable LMB to inves-
tigate and establish export markets (especially in the Middle East) for higher
grade chilled or processed meat, and to obtain prices over and above those
prevailing for the present live export trade. These higher prices will
eventually accrue directly to the nomadic cattle owners, as 75% of the profits
of the feedlot would be returned to them in the form of higher prices for his
cattle or as bonuses on cattle already sold. These higher prices should work
through the system to encourage a higher offtake from the range, and the
premium paid for better grade animals should help ensure a regular supply of
immatures. The feedlot would also serve as a pilot project to develop basic
information on the operation of feedlots in Ethiopia by establishing costs
and revenues and through investigations into various feeding regimes.
Implementation
3.        The AAISC farm, now owned by Government, would lease the land neces-
sary for the feedlot to the Project, and would provide a number of administra-
tive and other services to the feedlot operation. This farm now produces a
wide range of crops for sale on the local market, including maize, beans, soya
beans, sunflower and sisal. A proportion of the farm's maize output each



ANNEX 8
Page 2
season would be sold directly to the feedlot, thus guaranteeing it an assured
supply of the most important feed component. This arrangement would allow the
feedlot to be built at the minimum scale necessary to test the market potential
for the higher grade beef, as the feedlot itself would not have to absorb
either the overhead costs of crop production or a number of administrative
staff costs. The initial plan would call for a feedlot with a rated capacity
of 8,000 animals per annum - 2,660 head at any one time, each animal to be
held in the feedlot for an average of 4 months (Table 1). The feedlot could
easily be expanded at a later date to a yearly capacity of 12,000 head; such
an expansion would require little more than yard construction and would
effectively economize on the initial installation.
4.        The Project would obtain a long-term lease on approximately 12.5 ha
of land on the "reservoir" hill on Unit IV of the AAISC farm. The feedlot
manager would prepare detailed plans and specifications for the feedlot
installation (an approximate plan is set forth in the attached Chart, World
Bank 9817). AAISC would undertake construction on behalf of the Project.
Construction would be completed by the end of year 2 of the Project. Total
investment capital would be about Eth$1.1 million. The main items provided
would include: cattle yards, pit silos, fencing, a platform scale, 1/ feed
handling equipment, offices, vehicles and machinery. The capital and recurrent
costs are detailed in Annex 15, Tables 7 and 8.
5.        The cattle would be fed a mixture of maize silage, molasses, maize
grain, oil cake and minerals. (The detailed feed requirements are given in
Table 3). This feeding regime is planned around a well proven system based
on high quality forage developed in Kenya. However, the feedlot is intended
to serve as a pilot operation, and the feedlot manager would be required to
investigate the availability and suitability of other locally available feeds
and by-products; these might include, for example, bean straw, cotton seed,
sisal waste 2/ or coffee bean hulls. In addition, there seems to be a
relatively good prospect of establishing water table alfalfa on the margins of
a swamp located near the feedlot site; the alfalfa would be a valuable addition
to the ration, particularly when the cattle are being started on feed. The
use of these alternative feeds would be advisable from the point of view of
the feedlot, as the local Boran cattle have a high tolerance to a large
percentage of good quality roughage in their diet; it would also serve as
a valuable outlet for estate products which would otherwise be poorly utilized
or wasted.
1/ Appropriate designs are available in an FAO report by Bond (AGA: SF/KEN/
60/520).
2/   The use of sisal wastes now available on the AAISC farm would depend on
whether or not these can be economically processed to reduce the water
content.



ANNEX 8
Page 3
Management
6.        The feedlot would be under the direction of a Feedlot Manager
responsible directly to the Southern Rangelands Development Unit (SORDU)
Project Manager. He would prepare detailed plans and specifications for the
feedlot installation, and would be responsible for its technical operations
and maintenance. The AAISC farm would provide a number of services to the
feedlot for which it would be reimbursed at rates negotiated between the
Feedlot Manager and the General Manager of the farm (see Table 4). These
services would include:
(a) Construction. AAISC would undertake construction of the
feedlot complex in accordance with the specifications provided
by the Feedlot Manager.
(b) Crop production. The company would grow forage crops in the
Unit IV fields adjacent to the feedlot.
(c) Crop harvesting. The Project would purchase specialized
forage harvesting equipment, and the company would use this
to harvest the crop and deliver it to the silo; the feedlot
would purchase these crops at a yearly price negotiated in
advance of the planting season.
(d) Administrative management. While the technical management of
the feedlot would be the responsibility of the Feedlot Manager,
the company would provide an Administrative Manager and staff
for the feedlot who would be paid for by the feedlot.
(e) Accounting and purchasing. AAISC would provide these services
on exactly similar lines as it does for its existing units.
(f) Machinery and vehicle repair. The repairs would be carried
out at the MAISC workshop as requested at an agreed commercial
scale of charges.
In order to implement these services, the AAISC would create a Feedlot
Management Unit within its Agricultural Production Department. The organiza-
tion of this unit and its relationship to the Project Feedlot Unit is illus-
trated in Chart, World Bank 9818. At a formal level the Feedlot Manager
would deal with the MAISC General Manager, but it is expected that he would
develop a productive working relationship with both the Administrative Manager
and the Manager of Unit IV (as this unit would devote a large proportion of
its efforts to producing crops for sale to the feedlot) on the many technical
matters of mutual interest.



ANNEX 8
Page 4
Financial Projections
7.        The feedlot would bring forward a regular supply of cattle of a
consistently higher quality than has heretofore been available in Ethiopia.
The feedlot would produce between 150 and 180 head of cattle per week; a
large proportion of these would be slaughtered at either the Debre Zeit or
the Malgue Wondo abattoirs for sale either on the Addis market or for export
to Arab countries. The remainder would be exported live through the port
at Assab. This output would represent the equivalent of between 27 and 32
tons of carcass beef per week, or a little over 15 tons of high quality meat
cuts. It is estimated that this level of output on a guaranteed regular basis
should be sufficient to test and develop new market outlets for this quality
beef.
8.        There is considerable difficulty in forecasting the prices to be
paid for live cattle ex feedlot. The feedlot is basically a pilot operation
designed to test the feasibility of other feedlot operations in Ethiopia, and
as such it will be very much concerned with developing new markets for quality
beef; until these markets develop, it is difficult to determine how much of
a premium this beef will command over the lower quality meat now generally
sold on the Addis market. Therefore, the estimated price used for Project
purposes is based on the current price on the Addis market for high quality
beef, as this was believed to be the most accurate guide to the price which
the feedlot cattle could expect to receive in the absence of meaningful sales
of feedlot quality beef from Ethiopia. High quality Harar beef (sold mainly
for consumption on holidays and other special occasions) now commands a price
of 100 cents/kg liveweight. In the calculations for the feedlot, however,
a conservative price of 95 cents/kg liveweight was used for the period
1978-80; these estimates take into account the possible initial difficulties
of organizing the broadening market for high quality beef in Ethiopia,
as well as the difficulty of penetrating the export market for sale of beef
on Middle East markets. A tentative cash flow is given in Table 2.
Distribution of Profits
9.        The feedlot would--together with the smallholder fattening scheme-
provide a second link in the stratification of the beef cattle industry in
the southern rangelands area. It would provide further value added in the
form of both liveweight gains and quality improvement. It would also provide
a guaranteed market for cattle coming off the backgrounding ranches, and
would enable the producers to obtain significantly higher prices for their
animals. The profits from the feedlot would accrue to the nomadic producers
in much the same way as would those from the ranches (see Annex 7). Profits
would be apportioned on a 25%/75% basis between the feedlot unit (for loan
repayment, expansion or the construction of additional feedlot units) and
the nomads themselves. It is not yet completely clear how this surplus would
be channelled to the producers. One method would be to calculate the payment
in advance and to include it in the second payment to be made to the producer



ANNEX 8
Page 5
when his animal is sold off the ranch; another alternative would be to wait
until the animal was sold by the feedlot and then to pay the producer through
the ranch when he appeared with additional cattle for sale. It is estimated
that this surplus accruing to the producer from the feedlot alone could be
up to Eth$28/head.
July 22, 1975

ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Smallholder Fattening Components
A. General Ethiopian Background
1.        The production of livestock for meat in Ethiopia is at the present
time almost totally unstratified, and with very few exceptions there are no
specialized sections of the industry dealing with breeding, rearing or fatten-
ing for market. An animal destined for final consumption as meat will usually
spend its entire life in the same area under the same ownership, management
and feeding routine. The only major exception to this rule is that the zones
of high-density cultivation tend to be importers of young male stock from
surrounding pastoral areas, or from areas of less dense cultivation. These
young males are first trained as plough oxen and subsequently sold as culls
for human consumption. Some first steps toward a stratified beef industry
are beginning to be taken, however. In Harar Province, up to 10,000 animals
yearly have a much shortened working life as plough oxen in order that they
may be fattened at the optimum time for sale as high quality meat for weddings
and festivals. One commercial feedlot has operated for a number of years
near Addis Ababa (mainly in order to produce better quality meat for Ethiopia's
foreign community) and another small feedlot has been set up near Asmara.
2.        As these first steps have been reasonably successful, it seems
appropriate at this time to enlarge the scope of this initial stratification
to include fattening of beef cattle by smallholder cattle farmers. The work
of the UNDP-supported Beef Research Station in Kenya has produced much evi-
dence on the practicability of such operations, and based on previous Ethiopian
experience, the expansion of this segment of the industry should not present
any real difficulties. However, it may be slightly more difficult to develop
an appropriate system for producing suitable feeder animals for entry into
final feedlots. Conditions on the communally-used rangelands are too harsh
for young animals to achieve growth to bring them up at the right weight and
right age for entry to feedlots. In Kenya a system has evolved of "growing
out" or "backgrounding" young animals procured from the rangelands soon after
weaning on large ranches. This system may be more difficult to establish in
Ethiopia on a sufficiently large scale because of the absence of suitably
large tracts of unoccupied land for use as ranches. In the Southern Range-
lands Area where sufficient land is available, provision has been made under
the Project for the establishment of such ranches (Annex 7). In other areas
however, a system of growing out young animals to a suitable age and weight
for entry into feedlots needs to be grafted on to the existing farming system.



ANNEX 9
Page 2
B. The Proiect
3.        Smallholder fattening schemes would be initiated in each of the
three Project areas in order to encourage stratification of the livestock
industry, to raise producer prices paid nomadic stock owners, and to provide
increased incomes for smallholder farmers in the fringe areas near the range-
lands. These programs would also increase the supply and quality of meat
available for sale both on local markets and for export. Although the basic
concept of the program in each area would be much the same, variations would
be introduced to accord with local customs and conditions.
Southern Rangelands
4.        A large number of small farmers are now located in the fringe
areas between the ranges and the highlands; these farmers cultivate mostly
subsistence crops and often keep a few head of livestock. In some instances
these farmers are also part of the pastoral society, sending their herds into
the rangelands during the wet season and allowing the nomads to graze their
livestock on the crop residues during the dry season. A number of these
farmers would be selected to participate in the smallholder fattening program
and would then be provided with either one or two suitable animals purchased
in the rangelands. The SORDU Fattening Department would purchase the animals
for resale on credit to the farmers. Each farmer would make a small cash
contribution for the purchase and fattening of each animal. Because of the
pilot nature of this program and the small size of the total credit require-
ment, it is proposed that the credit be handled directly by the Project it-
self. In the Southern Rangelands area the credit would be administered by
the SORDU Fattening Department. The existing highland small farmer extension
service (SORIDEP) would be responsible for the selection of suitable farmers,
and would also provide extension advice.
5.        The animals would be housed in simple thatched mud and wattle
buildings and tethered on grass patches and roadsides. Over a 6-9 month
period (normally beginning during the rains), the cattle would be fed sorghum
thinnings, green matter derived from weeding crops and grass. As the dry
season commenced, sorghum and maize stover would be substituted for green
matter, and this feed would be supplemented by small quantities of sorghum
grain, household food scraps, sweet potato tops and planted fodder crops.
Salt, minerals and veterinary medicines would also be provided by SORDU during
the fattening period. The animals would be purchased from the ranches (see
Annex 7, paragraph 1) at 250 kg/head, and then fattened over 210 days to an
average weight of 350 kg/head. 1/ The animals would be sold through normal
trade channels at local auctions at an estimated price of Eth$ 0.75/kg live-
weight; Project representatives would be present at the auction to collect
loan repayment. A suggested budget for a typical smallholder (Table 1) shows
a profit of approximately Eth$ 47 per animal.
1/   No data is available on daily weight gains, but final slaughter weights
of 400 to 500 kg have been achieved in some cases.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
Jij igga
6.        Two types of fattening operations would be carried out in the
Jijigga area: (a) fattening and (b) backgrounding of immature cattle by
selected smallholders. Under the fattening operation animals would be used
as work oxen for two years and then in the third year they would be fattened
for resale; this program would be an extension of activities already carried
out on their own by farmers in the Jijigga area, and the Project simply would
attempt to enlarge an already successful traditional operation. A second
operation - referred to variously as a "backgrounding" or a "stocker feeder"
program - would undertake much the same task as was performed by the ranches
in the Southern Rangelands area; cattle would be fattened from 130 to 150 kg
to 250 kg, and would then be suitable for further finishing in a feedlot.
7.        The program would be administered by the JIRDU Fattening Depart-
ment. The Fattening Department would enter into contracts with suitable
traders to provide animals for the smallholder programs. The traders would
visit markets established in the area by the ongoing Livestock Marketing
Project and would purchase immature male cattle in good condition. The Proj-
ect would contract to purchase animals from these traders when delivered at
designated Minimum Package Trade Centers. The trader's margin is estimated
at about Eth$9-10/head - sufficient to cover market fees, movement costs and
other expenses; traders would use their own funds.
8.        Prior to movement from the market, the cattle would be innoculated
by the Livestock Marketing Project's staff against Rinderpest, CBPP, Anthrax,
Blackleg, and Foot and Mouth "A", "0" and "C". Before the Project purchased
the immatures from the trader, they would be weighed and checked for suitabil-
ity within the terms of the trader's contract. They would then be branded
and immediately resold on credit to the farmers; no holding ground would,
therefore, be required. Credit in this case would be provided by JIRDU.
9.        The animals would be put on a feeding regime similar to that in
the Southern Rangelands. Veterinary services would also be provided to the
smallholders through the Project Veterinary Department.  Two Animal Health
Assistants and 10 Veterinary Scouts would be detailed to these programs.
Estimated farm budgets (Tables 2 and 3) show approximate profits of Eth$67/
head and Eth$35/head for the fattening and backgrounding operations respec-
tively. It was assumed that cattle from the fattening program would be sold
at an average weight of 350 kg at an estimated price of Eth$0.76/kg liveweight.
Likewise it was assumed that cattle from the backgrounding program would be
sold at an average weight of 250 kg at a price of Eth$0.66/kg liveweight.
10.       The object of the backgrounding program is to prepare animals
for entry to a feedlot (this operation substitutes for the development of
backgrounding ranches in areas where sufficient land for such is not avail-
able). Thus far feedlots have only been introduced in a limited scale in



ANNEX 9
Page 4
Ethiopia (Sabajian at Debre Zeit and ELIDCO at Melgue Wondo) but HVA (sugar
growers) have started feedlot trials in the Awash Valley and MAESCO (banana
growers) will shortly start trials in the Middle Awash irrigation area.
Trials being carried out by the Awash Valley Authority indicate that high
returns may also be obtained from fattening livestock in irrigated pastures.
In view of the general interest indicated in the establishment of feedlots
within Ethiopia, the analysis herein assumes that feedlot outlets for Project
cattle will become available by the end of the Project period. The sales
price of Eth$0.66 is based on current prices for good quality 250 kg animals
in the Harar area, and on the price a feedlot geared toward an export market
is expected to be able to pay.
Northeast Rangelands
11.       Two types of programs would be introduced in the Northeast
Rangelands area. First, a livestock marketing program would be introduced
to stimulate the volume of marketed offtake and to reduce the irregularity
of supply that is presently a major constraint to the proper functioning of
existing markets. Eight markets recently closed by tribal hostilities would
be reopened and provided with perimeter fencing. A Project Marketing Unit
(PMU) would be responsible for the initial functioning of these markets.
Secondly, the PMU would also set up a pilot grazier scheme whereby it would
purchase young male animals, treat them for disease, and then sell them on
credit to pastoralists for feeding on the range for one year; the stockowners
would be required to resell the animals to the PMU.  Although the stockowners
would initially concentrate on cattle fattening, it is expected that sheep
would also be fattened using the surplus water dispersion areas of the
irrigation sites. It is expected that 100 pastoralists fattening an average
of 10 livestock units each would participate in the first three years of the
program; by year 5 the total throughput of the program would be about 3,000
livestock units per annum.
May 20, 1975

ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Roads
I. Introduction
Communications in General
1.        Due to Ethiopia's vast size, its rugged topography and the extremely
dry climate, the provision of communications is both difficult and costly.
Transport facilities are poorly developed; many areas of the country are almost
completely isolated and rely mainly on pack animals for transport. The main
communications links are the roads between Addis Ababa - (the administrative
center) and the main productive and economic centers, i.e. Asmara in the north,
the major Red Sea port of Assab and Jima in the southwest. Addis Ababa is
also connected with the neighboring French port of Djibouti by rail. An
internal scheduled air service provides adequate links to the regional admin-
istrative centers. In addition commercial charter services are available to
a network of airstrips which have been built throughout the country. The
country has no oil pipeline at this time, but a recent pre-investment study
found that one would be economically viable; there is therefore a possibility
that one may be built in the future between Addis Ababa and Assab.
2.        The physical remoteness of most of the population has precluded
the rapid modernization of the rural sector, and has encouraged a subsistence
economy dependent upon animal transport. The cost of animal transport makes
inter-district trade in bulky foodstuffs uneconomic; trade is normally
restricted to salable livestock and to high value per unit weight commodities
such as hides and skins, salt, sugar, ghee, luxury food items and some consumer
durables. Consequently, communities are dependent upon their own food
resources and are unable to make use of possible comparative advantages.
Ethiopian Roads System
3.        The Ethiopian Highway Authority (formerly the Imperial Highway
Authority) was established in 1951, and given prime responsibility for the
development of a network of all-weather roads. The activities of the EHA
are supervised by a Board of Commissioners appointed by the Government. To
date the existing highway network (Map) consists of about 23,000 km of roads;
about 8,600 km of these are all-weather, including some 2,000 km which are
bituminous surfaced. The all-weather roads are classified as primary, second-
ary and provincial (feeder or tertiary) roads - a classification which reflects
principally design standards rather than use. The main road network extends
radially from Addis Ababa, providing a connecting link with the major population
centers; these roads however, need upgrading, extensions and further inter-
connecting links - all of which will require substantial expenditures. Many



ANNEX 11
Page 2
of the other primary roads are quite old, dating back to the 1930's, and
will also need extensive maintenance. In addition to this network, there
are also a number of well engineered roads constructed during the Italian
occupation and by the British forces during World War II. Unfortunately,
however, these roads have had almost no maintenance over the past twenty-five
years, and have been allowed to deteriorate into pack animal tracks.
4.        In addition to the roads developed by the EHA, the provincial
authorities, in cooperation with the local people, have also developed a
local road network in many areas. While these roads were constructed at a
low cost and serve an extremely important function, the problems involved in
obtaining adequate technical advice and in securing a regul;r flow of funds
to maintain the roads already constructed have not yet been resolved.
5.        The overall road density - including roads usable only during the
dry season, is about 19 km/1,000 km2 or 0.9 per 1,000 inhabitants. Although
this ratio is among the lowest in Africa, considerable progress has been made
over the past twenty years in extending and improving the main road network.
Development of the rural road system has not kept pace with this effort,
however. This problem took on a greater significance in recent years wqhen
it became evident that a number of proposed agricultural projects could not
be implemented without a substantial investment in rural trade roads and
administrative access tracks. The Third Five Year Development Plan (1969-
1974) recognized the failure of the two previous plans to give sufficient
emphasis to this aspect of the development effort and stressed the need
for an integrated construction program.
Road Transport
6.        The total number of vehicles in use was estimated at 53,700 units
in 1973, a 6.7% increase over 1968 levels. The fleet was comprised of 76%
cars, 18% trucks, tankers and pick-ups, and 6% buses. The Road Transport
Administration (RTA) in the Ministry of Communications, Telecommunications
and Ports (MCTP) has responsibility for the licensing of commercial vehicles,
and the regulation of national tariffs; 1/ overall, however, the trucking
industry operates under relatively free competition. Even though the trucking
fleet has almost doubled in the five year period 1968-1973, transport by
pack animals and porters is still substantial; in fact, due to the poorly
developed rural roads system, it is the only means of transport in many areas.
7.        Rural Roads Task Force.  As was noted above, the provincial authori-
ties have not been particularly successful in constructing and maintaining
an adequate rural roads system. In fact, many areas of the country are still
several hundred kilometers from the nearest all-weather road. The dimensions
of the problem became evident during the recent drought; several international
agencies (particularly IDA, UNDP and UNICEF) reported that the toll in human
lives would have been much less severe had there been more ready access to
the drought stricken areas.
1/   Fixed tariffs are in force only on the Addis Ababa - Assab road.



ANNEX 1 1
Page 3
8.        During the drought a number of Government agencies were called upon
to carry out emergency road works; 1/ this was supplemented by technical
assistance from a number of foreign governments 2/ and voluntary agencies. 3/
While the Relief and Rehabilitation Commission (RRC) was responsible for
coordination of these efforts, there was a pressing need for technical coordi-
nation of construction standards. This need was met by the secondment of
a highway engineer from the EHA to the RRC and the drafting of guidelines
by the RRTF on basic standards for rural roads in Ethiopia (Table 1). It
was recommended that all proposals to construct or improve rural roads or
tracks should be reviewed by the coordinator of RRTF who would decide the
standard to which the road works should be carried out.
9.        At the instigation of the IBRD, a study of local rural road needs
was completed during 1974. The study recommended the setting up of a Rural
Roads Task Force (RRTF) to determine the organizational and operational
requirements necessary for the development of the rural road system. This
task force has now been established under the direction of a Senior Ethiopian
highway engineer; preliminary assistance was provided under the IBRD Fifth
Highway Project. Under the Sixth Highway Project provision has been made
for further technical assistance to implement the rural roads organization.
10.       The RRTF recommended that in addition to the three basic road
standards (para 3), there should be three classes of rural roads which could
be progressively improved by stage construction to near the EHA standard.
Briefly the standards proposed are:
Class RRA - 6 m wide with 6 m surfacing and permanent
drainage structures. Maximum gradient 12%
over 200 i: minimum curvature 10 m in
exceptional circumstances; desirable radius
50 - 70 m.
Class RRB - 6 m wide over shoulders with 4 m selected
gravel when necessary; drainage structures
would generally be of temporary or improvised
construction. Other geometric requirements as
as Class RRA.
Class RRC - Width 4 m with passing bays every 300 m.
Essential drainage structures of temporary or
improvised materials (basically paved fords).
Geometric requirements as for RRB. Pavement
generally of natural earth with minimum of
surfacing in unsuitable soils.
1/   EHA, EPID, LMB and variuos Provincial Administrations.
2/   China, Germany, UK and USA.
3/   Terre des Homunes (Dutch), Stern Magazine (German), Catholic Secretariat
and Irish Concern Mission (EIRE).



ANNEX 11
Page 4
In general RRC is intended to be the absolute minimum standard of construction
for community development projects without technical guidance or funds.
II. The Project
A. Southern Rangelands
11.       The Southern Rangelands sub-project area covers a major part of
Sidamo Administrative Region; it extends about 275 km from north to south and
about 500 km west to east. The terrain in the north and west of the area may
be roughly described as hilly to mountainous, and in the east and south as
flat to hilly. The main access to the area is via the main road extending
south from Addis Ababa through Awassa, Dila and Yabello to Moyale on the Kenya
border; this road is being reconstr;icted to paved standard by German technical
assistance. The Addis-Awassa road has been paved for some time, and the Dila-
Yabello section is practically compLete and is open to traffic; the Yabello-
Moyale section is behind schedule and is not expected to be complete until 1976.
The Awassa-Dila section is scheduled to be paved as soon as development finance
is available. In addition to this road, there is also a rough stone all-
weather road from Awassa to Neghelli. These are the only all-weather roads
in the area; a number of roads shown on the official highway map of Ethiopia
and given route markers are former military roads which have not been main-
tained for 25 years and are now degenerated to dry season tracks negotiable
only with four-wheel drive vehicles., The condition of the remaining tracks
in the area is generally bad, although varying with the terrain; the hilly
and mountainous tracks are boulder strewn and cut by deep ravines which are
susceptible to flash floods and washi-outs. The red sandy/clay soils found
in the flat lands present no problems, but there are also large areas of
black cotton soil which became impassable after the least amount of rain.
Rivers in the flat areas tend to have wide sandy beds which are also diffi-
cult to cross after the crust of thE sand has broken up.
12.       The existing road network is insufficient to meet the immediate
needs of the Project as well as to sustain the long-term development of the
Southern Rangelands. The consultants 1/ who studied the Project area made
two basic recommendations, both of great importance to the development of
the Southern Rangelands: (a) the creation of a network of dry season tracks
covering the entire area to be developed; and (b) the rehabilitation of the
basic communications system of the area. Item (a) is essential for efficient
animal health control action, checking herd movements and the functioning
of extension and technical assistance activities; item (b) is necessary to
open up the area for administrative and trade purposes, and to facilitate
the importation of trade goods and relief supplies and the circulation of
local products. Government has recently decided to rehabilitate some of
I/   Agrotec CRG SEDES.



ANNEX 11
Page 5
the specific roads covered under the consultants' recommendations with
Relief and Rehabilitation funds; the remaining roads are included in the
Project proposals.
Project Construction and Rehabilitation
13.       The Project would provide for:
(a) the construction of 1,538 km of access tracks;
(b) the rehabilitation of 540 km of administrative and trade
roads; and
(c) the purchase of survey and construction equipment, hand
tools, etc.
The proposed road network is designed to link the basic production units of
the Project (i.e. pastoral units centered around permanent water points)
as well as to subdivide the Project area into territorial units which could
be better and more easily kept under control by the veterinary service. A
description of the access tracks and trade roads is given in Table 2. The
access tracks would be built to class RRC standard, slightly modified (Table
1), and the administrative and trade roads to RRB standards with 4 m carriage
ways and only spot surfacing. The effective length of tracks in terms of
route kilometers would be considerably higher than 1,538 km, however, for
as many of these tracks would be constructed over flat lands where nothing
more than bush clearance is necessary to allow passage by a light four-wheel
drive vehicle, only the difficult sections would require any actual construc-
tion work. The equipment required would be minimal as the bulk of the work
would be carried out by labor-intensive methods.
Execution
14.       The road program would be executed by the Southern Rangeland Develop-
ment Unit (SORDU) under the overall direction of the Project Manager. A Chief
Road Supervisor reporting to the Head of the Technical Department would be
responsible for the execution of the construction program. The program would
be largely labor-intensive and a minimum amount of equipment would be used
both in the construction stage and for subsequent maintenance. The impact
of the recent drought in the Southern Rangelands has been such that it seems
likely that the area will not be self-sufficient in food, and that grain
and other food items will, for some years, have to be imported into the area
on concessional terms. In the long term, the provision of extra employment
opportunities is essential in order to relieve human population pressure
on the range resources. In these circumstances the road program has been
planned to be as labor-intensive as possible, both in its development and
maintenance. In view of the fact that most of the track development would
be for improvement of existing roads rather than for new construction, the
amount of substantial earth-moving will be small. Minimizing the amount of
machinery required would also minimize problems of servicing and maintaining
machinery spread over a large area.



ANNEX 11
Page 6
Construction Standards
15.       The area is one of generally low rainfall; for this reason, and
because the volume of traffic passing along the tracks is likely to be very
low, high design standards are neither necessary nor desirable. The stand-
ards adopted would allow two-wheel drive lorries carrying seven tons to pass
in dry weather, and four-wheel drive vehicles in all except very wet weather.
Adequate visibility would be provided at corners and passing places in
difficult terrain, otherwise a track wide enough to take just one vehicle
would be sufficient. Average speeds of 30 kilometers per hour by light
vehicles would be the target.
16.       The proposed road network would take the topographr of the area
into consideration as much as possible. During the implementation stage,
route surveys would be carried out in advance of construction in accordance
with the nature of the terrain and physical obstacles and following the
natural contours of the ground in order to minimize construction and mainte-
nance costs. The bulk of the work of clearing, grubbing, rough levelling and
ditching would be carried out by manual labor with hand tools; the use of
heavy equipment would be restricted to reducing severe gradients in
mountainous terrain, ripping soft rock and improving approaches to river
crossing. The grid roller, drawn by a tractor, would break down soft rocks
and boulders to gravel size, thus reducing damage to vehicles and trees.
The rubber-tired roller (also tractor-drawn) would compact earth tracks and
would make them more serviceable and reduce rutting or wheel tracking.
Construction of the roads would provide about 600,000 man days employment
for local labor and maintenance a further 1,000 man days per annum.
Cost Estimates
17.       The cost of the road element of the Southern Rangelands sub-project
is estimated at Eth$5.21 million (US$2.5 million).  The total capital and
recurrent costs are given in Annex L5, Tables 9 and 10.  Those estimates for
construction or rehabilitation infer an average construction cost of US$1,000
per km for track roads. The consultants (AGROTEC) reviewed the construction
costs of Government and voluntary agencies for rural roads and concluded that
dry weather rural roads can be buill at less than US$2,400 per km and tracks
at as little as US$100-200 per km. In this case, construction costs for
individual sections of rural roads and tracks would vary according to topo-
graphy and construction standard within these limits. The equipment costs
are those current at the time of appraisal; further price contingencies are
added in the summary estimates to cover cost increases to time of delivery
and expected increases in transportation and delivery costs. Local costs
include port handling charges and delivery cost to Addis Ababa. Staff com-
pensation is based on current Government rates for contract employees and
daily paid labor.



ANNEX 1 1
Page 7
Recommendations
18.       It is recommended that the 540 km of Class RRB dry season roads
and about 1,538 km of Class RRC tracks be constructed roughly as shown in
the Map and listed in Table 2. Construction of the 1,538 km of tracks
would be spread over the network of approximately 3,000 route kilometers
shown on the Map. The Project Manager should have the authority to decide
which sections of tracks should be improved and on the timing of the con-
struction so as to meet the operational requirements of the Project. Should
any other agency decide to construct any of the Class RRB roads, any savings
to the Project should be applied to improvement of the tracks. If a provin-
cial highway maintenance authority should be established with the necessary
capability, it is expected that such an authority would take over responsi-
bility for the maintenance of the Project RRB roads.
B. Jijigga
The Project Area
19.       The Project area is situated in Harar Administrative Region on the
ealtern edge of Ethiopia adjoining Somalia, and covers an area of about 35,000
km . A main all-weather road (EHA Rt 4 and 30) dissects the area from Harar via
Jijigga and south to Dagabur and beyond. This road has recently been resur-
faced with crushed stone and is generally in excellent condition. From
Jijigga, it is about 105 km to the provincial capital of Harar and 150 km
to the railway at Dire Dawa. There is an international standard airport at
Dire Dawa with daily flights to Addis Ababa capable of taking medium sized
jets. An airstrip at Jijigga can take DC3 type aircraft, although there are
no scheduled air services. Jijigga is served by the national telephone and
telecommunications network.
20.       The area can be divided into four physiographic units:
(a) East of the Jijigga-Dagabur road is a flat undissected area
largely of black cotton soil underlain by thick Jessoma
sandstone. Practically no granular soils suitable for road
making are available in the area. The sandstone could be
quarried but only at considerable cost.
(b) West of the Jijigga-Dagabur road the area is primarily
underlain by the Antalo limestone which has been dissected by
three main ephemeral rivers running from north to south. On
the high ground between the rivers the limestone is very near
the surface, and outcrops in large sheets, weathered in places,
make existing tracks very rough going. The valleys are
characterized by riverain gravel overlain by alluvial deposits.
The tracks parallel with the rivers become impassable as soon
as the ground water rises.



ANNEX 11
Page 8
(c) North of the Babilo-Jijigga road there are massive outcrops
of basement complex with relatively narrow incised valleys
impossible for road location. However, there are some
reasonably passable tracks along the ridges to the north.
(d) The area north of the Jijigga-Tugwajale road has a rolling topo-
graphy of low hills and broad valleys. The low hills are
volcanic in origin but the bulk of the area is covered with
black cotton soil overlying the Antalo limestone. Some
weathered rocks are suitable for road gravel.
21.       There is a local road and track network of very low standard con-
nected to the EHA all-weather trunk road from Harar to Dagabur; this network
is passable by trucks and four-whee:L drive vehicles for most of the year, but
only with considerable difficulty and expenditure of time. East of the Jijigga-
Dagabur road there are a great many passable tracks opened by traders, smug-
glers, and Government vehicles which are not recorded on present maps. In
this eastern area there are very few places which cannot at present be reached
by four-wheel drive vehicles in fair weather and with a persistent effort.
The western portion of the Project area lying between the Gerer river and the
Babile-Fik road is extremely difficult to enter by vehicle from east or wert.
however.
Project Construction and Rehabilitation
22.       The Project would provide for:
(a)  the construction (where no track exists at present) and
rehabilitation of 738 km of administrative tracks to Class
RRC standard;
(b) the rehabilitation of 766 km of trade roads to Class RRB
standard; and
(c) the purchase of a small amount of transport equipment and
an adequate supply of hand tools.
A description of the trade roads anct tracks is given in Table 3. The roads
would reduce the cost and facilitate the transport of consumer goods into
the Project area, and would thereby allow the human population to move away
from a substantial dependence on livestock products for their basic food to
a more varied diet and consumption pattern. The access tracks would allow
Project staff access to areas into which they would otherwise be unable to
enter except at prohibitive cost in time and money. Trade roads would be
built to a standard able to take 6-10 ton two-wheel drive lorries in dry
weather; access tracks would take only four-wheel drive vehicles in reasonable
weather. The main area, at present inaccessible, is in the Jijigga-Dagabur-
Fik-Babile rectangle, especially the Gerer, Fafan and Dakata valleys. Motorable
tracks formerly established in this area have now become overgrown and unusable.



ANNEX 11
Page 9
Execution
23.       The Project would be implemented by the Jijigga Range Development
Unit (JIRDU) based at Jijigga, headed by a senior Project Manager. A Chief
Road Superintendent under the Head of the Technical Department would be
responsible for the execution of the road program. The Technical Department
would thus be responsible for the execution of the road program and would also
provide repair and maintenance facilities for Project vehicles and equipment;
it would carry an adequate supply of spare parts for immediate needs. Con-
struction and maintenance methods would be labor intensive both in order to
provide employment opportunities and to reduce problems of maintenance of
equipment in remote places. There is an adequate labor force in the area
experienced in the use of hand tools. Construction of the roads would provide
about 500,000 man days employment for local labor for three years and there-
after maintenance would provide about 200 man years of employment per annum.
Cost Estimates
24.       The cost of the road element of the Jijigga sub-project is estimated
at Eth$ 2.2 million (US$1.0 million). The total capital and recurrent costs
are given in Annex 15, Tables 30 and 31. The cost estimates infer an average
consturction cost of US$821 per km for trade roads (Class RRB) and US$411 for
access tracks. These costs are lower than in other areas because most of the
Class RRB roads are in existence and in use, thus clearing costs are at a
minimum. The area has a very low rainfall with no mountain catchment, so
that drainage requirements are also minimal. Costs for individual sections
of the rural roads and tracks would depend on the topography being traversed
and the condition of the existing roads or tracks to be rehabilitated. The
equipment used would be confined to that necessary for transport of supervisory
staff, tools and materials. Staff compensation is based on current Government
rates for contract employees and daily paid labor. If a provincial highway
authority should be established with the necessary capability, it is expected
that it would take over responsibility for the 766 km of Class RRB roads.
Economic Evaluation
25.       Some economic benefits from the road program would accrue from
reduction in vehicle operating costs on the existing level of motor traffic
in the Project area, but this level is not known and no attempt has been made
formally to take these benefits into the cost-benefit calculation. The bene-
fits arising from lower transport costs on traffic generated directly by the
Project is taken into account in calculating these levels of cost. As pre-
viously stated, the purpose of trade roads is to reduce the cost of trans-
porting consumer goods into the Project area, and especially thereby to
facilitate and make more attractive the switch by the human population from
substantial dependence on livestock products for their basic food require-
ments to a partial dependence on cereals. At the present time even part of
the basic energy requirements of humans may be supplied from consuming milk,
although the extent to which this is so depends on the clan and the part of
the Project area in which the clan finds itself at any time. In early 1973,



ANNEX 11
Page 10
for example, there was a price differential per quintal of sorghum between
Babile and Segag (a distance of 220 kilometers by road) of about Eth$ 8.0.
The legitimate difference, given a road of reasonable standard for a rural
area, would be less than Eth$ 3 (220 kilometers x Eth$ 0.012 per quintal/kilo-
meter). A reduction in the sorghum price at Segag would trigger some substi-
tution of sorghum for milk consumption by pastoralists in the Segag area.
Even a substantial substitution would do no harm from a dietary point of view,
but would be economically desirable, since milk is a very expensive method
of meeting energy requirements. As a raw material for ghee, milk has a value
of about Eth$ 0.13 per liter. A liter of milk supplies about 660 digestible
calories. To supply 2,500 calories (approximately an adult's daily require-
ment) from milk therefore costs Eth$ 0.49. Some 2,500 calories can be sup-
plied by 730 grams of sorghum, which at a Segag price of Eth$ 15 per quintal
costs Eth$ 0.11. Put another way, at the prices quoted, the increased con-
sumption of one quintal of sorghum in substitution for milk leads to a net
saving in real cost of Eth$ 52. Where the milk released by substitution of
sorghum is not made into ghee, the calculation is more complex, since it
depends on whether the extra milk is fed to young stock (the direct conversion
rate of milk into meat is not known) or leads to a change in herd structure
and a changed marketed offtake rate.
Recommendations
26.       It is recommended that about 766 km of Class RRB dry season roads
and 738 of tracks (Class RRC) be constructed as shown on the Map and listed
in Table 3. The Project Manager should have the authority to decide which
sections of the roads and tracks should be improved, and on the timing of
the construction thereof to meet the operational requirements of the Project.
Should any other agency decide to construct or improve any of the Class RRB
roads, any savings to the Project should be applied to improvement of the
tracks.
C. Northeast Rangelands
The IDA Drought Areas Rehabilitation Project
27.       Following the drought of 1971-74 in which the Administrative Regions
of Tigre and Wollo were particularly hard hit, the Association approved a drought
rehabilitation project for these areas. This comprehensive project
included support for rural roads, rural water supplies, a pilot settlement
program for the northeast rangelands, assistance for project preparation
and establishment of rural health centers, etc. Some of the project compon-
ents benefitted the immediate relief/rehabilitation effort, while others re-
lated more closely to longer term programs. The project was carried out in
one portion of the total area to be covered by the proposed project.
28.       The road component of that project (see Map) included equipment
for two mechanized road construction units capable of constructing and im-
proving 400-500 km of rural access roads in the two provinces over 2-3 years.



ANNEX 11
Page 11
Provision was also made for technical assistance and operating costs over
the same period. The estimated total of US$1,520,000 was comprised of
US$1,200,000 for equipment and spares, US$120,000 for consultant services
and US$200,000 for operating costs. It was proposed that the equipment be
procured by the Ethiopian Highway Authority (EHA) and construction carried
out by the Rural Roads Task Force (flnanced under Highway I and II projects).
In the case of Tigre which has a well established and proven provincial roads
organization - the Tigre Development Organization (TDO) - the equipment may
be handed over to this organization to operate at some future time. On the
other hand, Wollo has no such organization; at the present time British Tech-
nical Assistance (ODM) have instituted a pilot construction and maintenance
project with the aim of establishing and training a provincial roads organiza-
tion. Ultimately this organization will take over responsibility for all the
roads in the province which are not constructed or maintained by the EHA.
The Project Area
29.       The Project area covers more than half of Tigre and Wollo Adminis-
trative Regions lying below the escarpment east of the Dessie-Asmara road
(EHA Route 1).  It is roughly 400 km from north to south anq 200 km from east
to west in its widest parts and covers an area of 75,000 km . On the east the
area is bounded by French territory of Afars and Isars, in the northeast by
Eritrea, in the west by the foothills of the escarpment and in the south
by Bakenna and Awash rivers. The Project area, though itself an area of low
rainfall, lies at the bottom of the escarpment to the east of and below a
narrow strip of land steeply sloping east; the western edge of this area
rises from between 2,500 m to 4,000 m and has a mean annual rainfall of
1,000-1,400 mm in the south and 800-900 m in the north. The whole area
may be described as semi-arid with four distinct seasons. Numerous rivers
drain into the Project area, some of which are perennial throughout most
of their length within the Project area. Along the foothills of the escarp-
ment, flash floods are common, but most streams can be crossed after a com-
parative short waiting period (up to several hours).
30.       This vast and largely inaccessible area is traversed on the south
and east by Dessie-Assab Road (EHA Route 2) and on the top of the escarpment
on the west side by the Dessie-Asmara road (ERHA Route 1). At the present
time access from the top of the escarpment to the Project area down on the
plain is very precarious, but several more permanent access roads will be
built under the IDA Drought Areas Rehabilitation Project. A number of
primitive tracks cross the area which, when dry, are motorable with four-
wheel drive vehicles and large trucks only half loaded; river crossings,
rock outcrops, lava fields and black cotton soil make going very hazardous,
slow and expensive, however. Under the "Food for Work Program" a number of
tracks have been improved (notably Burka-Dawe and Burka, north-wards to Bilu
and continuing to Didigsala). The standard of the work is very poor, however,
due to the inexperience of the nomad Affar labor available for the work. This
particular route is due to be raised to Class B Standard under the IDA Drought
Areas Rehabilitation Project. The Haik-Bistima-Alabati route is also being
improved to all weather standard by the German "STERN" group.



ANNEX 1 1
Page 12
31.       Detailed geological and mineralogical investigations have not
been made, but preliminary investigations in Tigre province in the north
indicate high mineral potential in the "Horst" to the west of Dalol. Also
within the Danakil Depression, grotndwater is lost by evaporation leaving
behind extensive deposits of salts, comprised mainly of sodium chloride
and potassium carbonate; the deposits are known to be extensive. Adequate
road access to heavy drilling and prospecting equipment is essential in this
area to fully investigate and develop the natural resources.
32.       Airfields at Dessie and Mekele are used by Ethiopian Airlines for
scheduled flights, but since navigational aids are elementary and flight
approaches precarious, cancellations are frequent during inclement weather.
Landing strips are available at Dubti (near Loggia) and Assayita on the
plains and are available for use by light aircraft in all but exceptionally
wet weather. Telecommunication links are confined to the few small towns
on the main roads.
33.       Over most of the Project area, suitable natural soils for road
construction are not available. Silt is predominant in the flood plains
and deltas of the rivers, and in the flat lands there are large areas of
black cotton soils; in many areas there are deposits of riverain gravel or
medium sized weathered rocks. These are frequently used by maintenance
crews to fill in ruts in the black cotton soil tracks, but in effect are
quite useless and it is far better to abandon the old track and make a new
one. On the eastern side of the Project area there are a number of large
lava flows from extinct volcanoes, and there is also one active volcano.
The track north of Sardo to Lake Afrera passes through a particularly rough
lava field and it would be difficult to make a passable road at reasonable
cost. In the foothills soils are more varied and granular due to the
weathering of a variety of rocks - limestones, sandstones, quartz granites,
etc. Tracks and roads properly located in these areas will provide a reason-
able all-season service.
Project Construction and Rehabilitation
34.       The Project would provide for:
(a)  the construction of 165 in of Class RRB roads;
(b)  the construction/improvement of 380 km of Class RRC tracks;
(c)  the purchase of survey and construction equipment, hand tools,
materials, etc.;
(d) construction of a limited number of airstrips;
(e)  purchase of radio communication equipment.



ANNEX 11
Page 13
A description of the roads and tracks is given in Table 4. The lengths given
above are the route kilometers; actual construction would be confined to the
difficult terrain, since considerable lengths of road or track require only
the removal of bushes or scrub to define the actual route. River and gulley
crossings would be constructed with gabions, which are in effect wire cages
filled with boulders. These form a dam across the river or stream and when
covered with riverain sand and gravel maintain a fairly permanent river cross-
ing. As construction would be labor intensive, equipment has been kept down
to the minimum required to service the labor force. On the other hand a
liberal supply of hand tools, including hammers for breaking stones over the
lava flows, and a blacksmith's set for sharpening hand tools has been provided
for.
35.       At each Project Development Center and at Project Headquarters
a transceiver radio would be installed for regular and emergency broadcasts
between Project managements and the field service. The mobile veterinary
units and the regular vaccination units would also be supplied with transceiver
sets so that they could keep in constant contact with Project Headquarters,
the Development Centers and the Head of the Veterinary services.
Execution
36.       The road program would be executed by the Northeast Rangelands
Development Unit (NERDU) under the Project Manager. A Chief Road Superin-
tendent would be responsible for the execution of the construction program.
As the IDA Drought Areas Rehabilitation Project has provided for the construc-
tion of the major roads in the area, the proposed project roads will be entirely
labor intensive. There is already a large nomadic Affar task force engaged in
the construction of tracks under the "Food for Work Program." At the present
time the productivity and quality of work is very poor, but is expected to
iraprove in time as the nomads become accustomed to the work; both female and
male laborers are used. The construction program would provide for the employ-
ment of about 300,000 man days of employment during the life of the Project.
Maintenance of the completed roads and tracks would be the responsibility of
the Project organization until such time as a provincial maintenance organi-
zation is established.
Cost Estimates
37.       The cost of the road component of the Northeast Rangelands sub-
project is estimated at Eth$ 966,800 (US$483,000). The total capital and
recurrent costs are given in Annex 15 Tables 49 and 50. The above estimates
infer a construction cost of US$1,690 per km for the roads and US$510 for
the tracks. In this case, construction costs for individual sections of rural
roads and tracks would vary according to topography and construction standard
within these limits. The equipment costs are those current at the time of
appraisal. Further price contingencies are added in the summary estimates
to cover cost increases to time of delivery and expected increases in trans-
portation and delivery costs. Local costs include port handling charges,
insurance and delivery charges to Addis Ababa. Staff compensation is based
on current Government rates for contract employees and daily paid labor.

III. RECOMMENDATIONS
38.       It is recommended that 165 km of Class RRB and 380 km of Class RRC
dry season roads and tracks be constructed under the Project, roughly as
shown on the Map and listed in Table 4. These roads would directly comple-
ment the 400-500 km of roads to be built under the IDA Drought Areas Rehabili-
tation Project. The Project Director should have the authority to decide
which sections of tracks should be improved and on the timing of the construc-
tion to meet operational requirements of the project. Should any other agency
decide to construct any of the roads, any savings to the Project should be
applied to improvement of the tracks.

ETHIOPIA

Training
Current Government Programs
1.        Extension.  Since the 1950's Ethiopia has had a nationwide general
agricultural extension program under the auspices of the Ministry of Agricul-
ture. In practice, acute shortages of staff and funds, as well as the
absence of well-researched innovations and of an effective credit program
have sharply limited its utility. Since the late 1960's, there has been a
switch away from emphasis on the general nationwide program towards regionally
or project-based extension efforts run by, and in connection with, specific
projects such as CADU, WADU, the Minimum Package Program, and the Dairy Devel-
opment Agency. With the exception of the latter, these agencies are prima-
rily concerned with crop production, although CADU and WADU have small live-
stock programs. The Veterinary Division of the Ministry of Agriculture has
also been ready to give general advice on livestock development to Ethiopia's
farmers as well as specialist advice on animal health, although their role
so far has been limited. The village-level workers of the Ministry of
National Community Development have also provided some agricultural extension
facilities to farmers in certain areas.
2.        Training.  The training of Government agricultural workers is
formally the responsibility of the Ministry of Agriculture's Training and
Information Department. Training of agricultural graduates (B.A.) takes
place at the Alemaya Agricultural College of the National (Haile Sellassie I)
University, and of the sub-B.A. level (secondary education plus two years'
professional training) at Ambo and Jimma Agricultural Institutes (general
agricultural workers), and at Debre Zeit Animal Health Training School
(Animal Health Assistants). Training of more junior agricultural staff (e.g.
Assistant Extension Agents) has been done by the specific project (e.g. CADU)
employing them. Some residential training courses for farmers have been run
by CADU and by the Dairy Development Agency. The Training and Information
Department of the Ministry of Agriculture has a program for the building and
operating of farmers' training centers in a number of places. Radio programs
on agricultural techniques and development are drawn up by the Department,
and broadcast by Radio Ethiopia.
Project Training
3.        The development of the rangelands and the gradual changing of the
traditional practices of a nomadic people will be a slow and difficult process,
and the success of this program will depend almost entirely on the willing
cooperation of the stock owners themselves. It will also depend on the enthu-
siasm and expertise demonstrated by the various levels of Project staff, parti-



ANNEX 12
Page 2
cularly those who would be working directly with the nomads. Consequently the
Project training program would be a two-tiered effort aimed both at training
local Project staff and at educating and integrating the tribal groups in the
development process.
4.        The training program would be specially tailored in each of the
three Project areas to allow for varying customs, languages, levels of devel-
opment, availability of trained staff, etc. Basically, however, the program
would consist of four elements:
(a) training of local staff in improved extension, range manage-
ment and veterinary practices;
(b)  training and education of local tribal leaders;
(c) training of local stock owners; and
(d) short courses for all Project staff in their particular
fields, and the provision of a limited number of fellowships
for study either elsewhere in East Africa or abroad.
In order to carry out this program, a training center would be established
in each of the three sub-project areas (2 in the Northeast Rangelands area);
provision would also be made for a mobile training center to move throughout
the more remote parts of each area. The center would be staffed with a
Senior Training Officer and a smal'L number of supporting personnel.
5.        Practical Staff Training.  The practical training of Project staff
would include but not be limited to the activities described below:
Staff Training
