In addition to this formal instruction, regular follow-up courses would be
given, with special emphasis on the training of range assistants and veterinary
scouts.
6.        Training of Tribal Leaders.  Near the beginning of the Project
period, tribal chiefs and elders would be invited to spend four or five days
at the Project training center. This would be done to familiarize them with
the objectives and methods of the Project, and to enable them to begin to
develop a good working relationship with the staff who would be operating in
their area. This introductory program would be followed up by further con-
tacts by the mobile training center, as it is believed that these people would
probably be more receptive to new ideas when they were in their own familiar
environment.
7.        Training of Stock Owners.  Training of stock owners (both transhu-
mant and sedentary) under the Project would be mainly the responsibility of the
veterinary scouts, who would in addition to their duties in the direct treat-
ment of animals also function as extension workers. They would instruct stock-
owners:



ANNEX 12
Page 4
(a) in how to hand-dress and dose stock against ecto- and endo-
parasites;
(b) in the use of simple dressings in case of injuries;
(c) in the trimming of sheep's feet;
(d) on the animal facilities (vaccinations, dipping, other treatments)
available under the Project, including where and at what price
the stockowners can buy supplies or get treatments.
The veterinary scouts would receive some instruction in how to impart infor-
mation (techniques of instruction), and would be issued with suitable instruc-
tional kit, including a transistor radio on which they could receive broad-
casts about livestock matters and pass them on to their tribal groups.
8.        Supplemental Training Programs.  In addition to the regular training
programs described above, certain other special training would be available
to meet the special needs of a particular area:
(a) language training;
(b) preparation of livestock information programs to be
broadcast in the local dialect over the regular radio
stations;
(c) public relations programs and dissemination of information
about the projects;
(d) training of Afars in irrigated crop production (Northeast
Rangelands); and
(e) training of laborers to serve as foremen and supervisors
and in irrigation and road construction programs (Northeast
Rangelands).
9.        Short Courses and Fellowships.  Three types of training would be
provided:
(a) Professional courses. In the early years of the Project
three high school graduates from each sub-project area
would be sent abroad for professional training in range
management, veterinary medicine, sociology or animal hus-
bandry for an average of four years each.
(b) Intermediate Level and Diplomate Training. During the
first three years five staff members of each sub-project
would be sent each year for a refresher training course
averaging three months in duration at an appropriate
training institution.



ANNEX 12
Page 5
(c) Stock Owner Training. Twenty nomadic cattle owners would
be selected in each of the first three years of the Protject
and sent on a 30-day tour of cattle ranches, rangeland
development projects, and fattening schemes in neighboring
East African countries.
Project Costs
10.       The costs of the Project training programs are detailed in Annex 15,
Tables 13, 14, 32, 33, 51 and 52.
July 22, 1975

ETHIOPTA
NOMADIC RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Trials and Studies
1.        The program of trials and studies proposed under the Project would
be designed to carry out technical and socio-economic investigations both in
order to provide immediate answers to technical problems and to assess the
effects of the different components of the Project.
General Ethiopian Background
2.        The Ethiopian Government's Agricultural Research Institute (ARI)
is responsible for carrying out much of the agricultural research in Ethiopia,
as well as for coordinating the efforts of other research organizations. In
addition to the work carried out by ARI, the other major agricultural develop-
ment programs (CADU, WADU, Minimum Package Program, Dairy Development Agency)
have their own programs of trials carried out with varying degrees of coopera-
tion with ARI. The Yabello Pilot Project in the Southern Rangelands area
initiated research on range enclosures. The Alemaya Agricultural College of
the National (Haile Selassie I) University has for many years carried on a
program of agricultural research based on food and cash crops. Socio-economic
studies have received much less attention, but in the course of Project prepa-
ration over four years, LMB has gathered a considerable amount of pre-Project
statistics and socio-economic data. Some academic work on rural social and
economic life has been done by anthropologists in Ethiopia, but few studies
have as yet been carried out by agricultural economists.
3.        The Project program of studies and trials would have three purposes:
(a) to provide technical information for the execution of the
Project. An example would be trials on cactus varieties
to determine which kind should be introduced under the
Project as a "fodder bank" for periods of low rainfall.
(b)  to provide more general information for strategic long-
term decisions as to how this and other projects should
be implemented.
(c) to provide regular periodic information on Project achieve-
ments.
Little justification is needed for the first of these purposes, since trials
of this nature are a common feature in agricultural projects. As far as the
second is concerned, this Project would be the first full-scale range develop-
project in Ethiopia; it is therefore important that it should be made to yield



ANNEX 13
Page 2
the maximum amount of data, so that these experiences can serve as a guide to
the further development of the livestock sub-sector. Thirdly, a flow of
regular information is required in order to monitor progress and to assist
Project management in the exploration of successful development.
Project Investigations
4.        A description of the main areas of investigation is provided below.
5.        Range Management.  Studies would be initiated under the Project to
determine trends in range production as well as vegetative changes and to
investigate those practices which appear capable of maintaining range produc-
tivity and of giving the most profitable sustained yield of livestock products.
Specific items for study would include:
(a) the establishment of enclosures for the testing of
various range management practices;
(b) range ecology studies using transects;
(c) introduction of important forage-producing cactus species;
(d) introduction and preservation of browse and fodder-producing
trees and shrubs; and
(e) fodder crop trials.
6.        Socio-Economic Studies.  Not only is this Ethiopia's first full-
scale range-management project, but similar projects in other parts of the
world have not been adequately monitored and evaluated as they progressed.
As a consequence too little is known about their effectiveness. It is
proposed to incorporate in the Project an evaluation unit which would not
only evaluate the effects of this Project, but would also develop an effective
methodology for evaluating similar projects in the future. A certain amount
of base-line data has already been collected during the course of Project
preparation, particularly on livestock populations, herd structures and
marketed offtake. This would be supplemented by further base-line studies,
and changes from the base-line situation brought about by the Project would
be monitored.
7.        The following additional base-line studies would be carried out:
(a) motor traffic density surveys on roads within the Project area;
(b) livestock weights, by class and season;
(c) household consumption patterns;
(d) crop yields and values;



ANNEX 13
Page 3
(e) a list of the tribaI groups in the Project area which are
mainly dependent on the rangelands for a livelihood; and
(f) more detailed information than has been obtained so far on
migration patterns, their causes, extent, timing and frequency.
8.        During the course of the Project further studies would be undertaken
to investigate changes which have occurred during the life of the Project;
these would include:
(a) herd structures;
(b)  marketed offtake;
(c) livestock weights by class and season;
(d) livestock populations;
(e) motor traffic densities;
(f) household consumption patterns;
(g) the degree of contact between the pastoralists and the
Government's services (veterinary, extension, training,
water supplies, etc.);
(h)  prices for livestock;
(i) crop yields and incomes of farmers participating in the
smallholdar fattening programs;
(j) migration patterns.
9.        Management Information.  Information to assist management would
be provided in two ways:
(a)  by a series of regular reports produced at quarterly intervals,
with more detailed evaluation at annual intervals; and
(b) by special studies on specific activities not covered by the
"impact" studies of paragraph 8 above.
July 22, 1975



ANNEX 14
Page I
ETEIIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
IMB Headquarters Staff
Livestock Development Department
1.        The three range development projects would be managed as separate
entities within the Livestock and Meat Board (LMR). It is evident, however,
that certain administrative, technical and financial services would be most
efficiently provided to all three projects by a single unit located at LMB
headquarters in Addis Ababa. For this reason a Livestock Development Depart-
ment would be established within LMB to coordinate the projects and to advise
the Project Managers on overall policy matters. Some of the staff for this
department would be provided under the Project or through bilateral aid;
others would be made available through a reorganization of the existing staff
of LMB's project office and from the staff of the former Animal Production
Division of the Ministry of Agriculture (which has been transferred intact
to IMB). The Livestock Development Department would provide a number of
common services to the three sub-projects; it would:
(a) serve as a communications center for the three projects:
(b) assist with activities for which the projects would need a
representative in Addis Ababa, i.e., preparation and
presentation of budgets, attendance at committee meetings
and arrangements for travel and visitors;
(c) act as a central clearing post for recruitment and training
of Project staff;
(d) provide office space and administrative services to Project
staff while in Addis;
(e) provide technical services and advice in the fields of
hydrology, hydrogeology, and water engineering;
(f) provide technical range management advice as well as advice
on range ecology and range economics and sociology: and
(g) provide assistance and advice with the dissemination of
information on the projects through mass media and other
forms of communication.



ANNEX 14
Page 2
2.        In order to help the Department to carry out these functions, the
following staff would be provided under the Project:
(a) Head of the Department who would be an internationally
recruited Administrator, Economist or Engineer;
(b) two Technical Managers - one each for Project Management
and Veterinary Services;
(c) a Personnel Officer;
(d) a Water Engineer;
(e) a Sociologist;
(f) a Mass Media/Training Specialist;
(g) supporting staff.
3.        In addition to these, it is expected that the following staff would
be provided outside the Project under bilateral aid:
(a) one internationally recruited hydrogeologist for four years;
(b) one internationally recruited range ecologist for four years;
(c) one internationally recruited sociologist or economist for
four years; and
(d) one irrigation engineer (stationed in the Northeast Rangelands area).
Supplemental Staff to be Provided within LMB
4.        LMB would also provide certain other financial and procurement
services to the three range projects. In order to assist in these tasks, as
well as to enable LMB to improve the efficiency of its own operations, supple-
mental staff would be provided within both the Finance Department and the
Procurement Division.
5.        Finance Department.  As the Livestock Development Department would
not have a special financial unit within it, LMB's Finance Department would
provide certain services to the Project; these would include:
(a) running of a post-factum internal audit of the various
activities of the three projects;
(b) establishment of sound accounting systems and financial
procedures for the individual projects, and training of
Project staff in the implementation of these;



ANNEX 14
Page 3
(c) collation, processing and despatch of IDA/ADF reimbursement
applications for all Project activities;
(d) ensuring that the bank accounts of individual range projects
are kept adequately maintained with the necessary funds;
(e) integration of the annual budget forecasts of individual
range project into the overall IMB budget;
(f) advising the General Manager of LMB on the financial implica-
tions of the activities of individual range projects, and to
provide him with regular financial forecasts and statements
of accounts in respect of all Project activities; and
(g)  to obtain from IDA/ADF and Government the funds allocated
for Project activities.
6.        In order to better enable LMB to carry out these duties, the follow-
ing additional staff would be provided within the Finance Department:
(a) one internationally recruited Financial Controller;
(b) one Internal Auditor (reporting directly to LMB's General Manager); and
(c) two accountants to deal with reimbursement applications and
the collation of Project accounts and forecasts.
7.        Procurement Division.  Each of the three range projects would have
a procurement officer stationed at its Project Headquarters. It is expected,
however, that he would handle mostly local procurement and that all foreign
and national procurement would be carried out on a consolidated basis from
Addis Ababa. This latter task would be taken over by LMB's Procurement
Division under LMB regulations, but with the specific requests channelled
through by the individual project procurement officers. To carry out these
tasks, the following additional staff would be provided within the Procurement
Division:
(a)  a Senior Procurement Officer; and
(b) one junior procurement officer.
Payments for urgent items to be procured through LMB would be made on the
strength of the individual project's request from LMB's own funds, and
recovered later from the individual projects.
October 30, 1975

RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Project Beniefits
1.        Benefits from the Project would be derived from:
(a)  increased value of meat from range cattle due to increased
offtake together with an increase in weight for all animals
marketed;
(b)  increased production of milk from range cattle;
(c)  increased value of meat production attributable to cattle
marketed from the ranches, the smallholder fattening pro-
grams and the feedlot;
(d)  increased numbers (Northeast Rangelands only) and value
of meat production from sheep and goats and increased value
of meat from camels;
(e)  incremental value of crops grown as part of the water
spreading program.
These increases are demonstrated in the table below, and detailed supporting
data are available in Tables 11-29.
Value of Incremental Production
At Full Development (Year 7)

(b)  Sheep and Goats                                        1.9
(c)  Camels                                                 1.2
(d)  Milk Production                                        1.2
(e)  Backgrounding and Fattening Ranches                    1.6
(f)  Smallholder Fattening Program                          1.8
(g)  Feedlot                                                1.3
(h)  Crop Production                                        0.9

Page 2
Range Cattle, Shoats -  and Camels
2.        The value of increased meat production from range cattle has been
assumed to be derived from increased offtake and an increase in weight for
each animal in the existing herd. In addition, however, as a result of
improved water, range management and veterinary services, etc., the Project
would also allow an increased carrying capacity on the ranges, and hence
increased total stock numbers. There are, however, no reliable data which
would allow this increase to be measured. Therefore, in the calculation of
Project benefits only the increased meat production attributable to increased
offtake and liveweight gains has been allowed for.
3.        The value of increased offtake and weight of cattle from the
ranges in the three sub-project areas (see Tables 11, 18 and 24) was derived
using available data as to:
(a) existing herd sizes and composition (Tables 1-9); and
(b)  marketed offtake (Table 10).
There are no reliable statistics on which detailed projections of herd
numbers, stocking rates, etc., can be accurately measured, but during
Project preparation various surveys were carried out to try and obtain
some indications of the appropriate parameters. The most accurate figures
available are derived from surveys using small airplanes doing visual counts
and photographing random samples (Tables 1-4). These figures were fairly
accurate for the day the survey was carried out, but consecutive surveys at
different times of the year made it clear that due to migration, great
variations would be noted in the livestock numbers in any particular area
depending on the time of year, rainfall and the condition of the pasture.
However, within the boundaries of a given zone which has little or no move-
ment of stock into neighboring areas, it is evident that although livestock
numbers fluctuate, they do so around a static norm. Thus, for purposes of
Project calculations static herd numbers have been assumed for both "With"
and "Without" Project situations. These estimates are given below:
Estimated Normal Traditional Herd Numbers (in '000)
----------N.E.R.----------    Project
S.R.    Jijigga            (After drought)  Full Herd       Totals
Cattle   1,000        500                  (134)           500         2,000
Shoats     300        970                  (750)           800         2,070
Camels      90        160                  (130)           200           450
1/   This term has been used to indicate sheep and goats.



ANNEX 19
Page 3
4.        The Northeast Rangelands estimate presented a special problem,
for as a result of the recent drought the existing herd sizes are at present
substantially below normal levels. In general, it can be estimated that
prior to Project implementation herd numbers would be roughly at 50% of the
static herd size prior to the drought (see Table 4). However, Tables 5 and
6 demonstrate that in either a "With" or "Without" Project situation, these
herds are expected to regenerate themselves and to reach the static norm over
a period of between 7-10 years. 1/ Some of the factors which make such a
drastic recovery possible are:
(a) After a bad drought the surviving cattle almost exclusively
consist of adult females, since calves and yearlings tend
to die first from exposure and mature males and unproductive
cows will have been consumed or sold;
(b) When the drought breaks and grasses recover, the stocking
rate will be very low and the surving cattle will reach a
very high nutritional level and a consequent high level of
fertility; calf mortality will be low and growth rates high;
(c) Herd increase will slow down when numbers again outstrip
the available grazing, at which point low nutrition, lower
fertility and high mortality rates will combine to bring
about the static herd of the standard model.
The benefits from the Northeast Rangelands sub-project have therefore
been expressed as in the other sub-projects, that is, using the normal
static herd as a baseline figure. It should be noted that an increase in
the Northeast Rangeland shoat flock of 25% has been credited to the Project,
as the water spreading program would, through the provision of additional
pasture, allow the flocks to increase beyond the static norm.
5.        The static herd estimates were used as the basis from which the
liveweight gains and the incremental offtake attributable to the Project
were computed. These are shown below:
1/   The assumptions underlying this model of the recovery of the herd after
a drought are confirmed by an example from Kenya, where the Masai cattle
herd suffered losses estimated at 50% in 1963 and again in 1972 and in
the interval still managed to recover to the limit of the capacity of
the ranges.

/1   The impact of the JP-15 Rinderpest Control Program has been allowed for
in Southern Rangelands and Jijigga by crediting an initial increase in
offtake of 3% for three years to JP-15 and thereafter any further
increase to the Project on the assumption that if the Project were not
implemented and the follow-up vaccinations not carried out, herds would
then revert to their pre-vaccination situations. For the Northeast
Rangelands it is assumed that the campaign would be carried out by the
Project veterinary service and consequently all increase in offtake
accrues to the Project.
The offtake rates depend on birth, death and calving rates together with
the owners' preference for sale. The composition of the traditional cattle
herd and sheep flock are shown in Tables 7 and 9 for both "With" and "Without"
the Project.
Milk Production
6.        It has also been assumed that from year three onward, the same
factors which contributed to liveweight gains for range cattle would also
result in an increased production of milk per lactation. Using the standard
model (Table 7), it has been assumed that 43% of the cattle population would
be cows, and that these would have a 60% calving rate; it was then assumed



ANNEX 19
Page 5
that each of these cows would show an incremental milk production of 15 kg
of milk annually (10% of the estimated body weight increase of 15 kg expressed
as milk). Although this milk would be consumed by calves or by the nomadic
livestock owners, it has been valued as if sold on the market. 1/
Fattening Schemes
7.        The incremental liveweight gains and the value of the incremental
meat production attributable to the ranches, the feedlot and the smallholder
fattening programs are easily quantifiable; these figures are found in Tables
13, 14, 15, 16, 21, 22 and 27.
Crop Production
8.        The main crops grown on land irrigated by water spreading would
be cotton, wheat and sorghum. The total value of this incremental crop
production attributable to the Project is shown in Table 28.

1/   This nmdee  is confirmed by an FAO survey on Herd Composition carried out in
the Democratic Republic of the Sudan as the FAC Perspective Study for Livestock
Production (FAO ESP/AGA/SUD/7h/9) which yielded the following parameters:
(a) 25% of the herd is composed of males of varying age;
(b) no female culling takes place except for very old cows;
(c) 20% of the cows in the herd are 9 years of age or above;
(d)  reproduction rate is 45-55% and the number of calves per cow
reproductive life is 3 or less;
(e)  average age at first calving is about 4 years of age;
(f) average mortality rates are:
calves       10-15%
young stock   7-8%
adults         5-7%
(g)  most male animals are sold at the age of 4 years or later
May 6, 1975

jl The surveys were conducted in order to estimate of ftake rates. One was a
a census of trade routes, the second, an interview with traders and market
officials and the third, an incluiry among livestock owners to obtain
figures of livestock sold, bartered or slaughtered from their herds.
Hollyer's figures are much higher as they included animals which passed
through the markets but came from sources outside the Project area, such
as the continguous highlands around Harar. The Livestock Owner Survey is
probably the most accurate beirg based on actual life histories of some
2,500 head of livestock.

ANNEX 20
Page 1
ETHTOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Markets and Prices
A. Markets
Trade Stock Flows
1.        Ethiopia can roughly be divided into livestock consumption and
livestock production areas. In the south the main production areas with sur-
pluses for export are the southern rangelands and the area to the east of the
Rift Valley, The main flows of trade stock are from these producing areas to
the three main consumption centers: (i) Addis Ababa, with its satellite mar-
ket at Nazret; (ii) the Melgue Wondo Meat Packing Plant; and (iii) the densely
populated coffee growing area around Dilla. There is also a long established
export trade of live animals moving south into Kenya. In the north, surpluses
are available in general throughout the whole area, and there are four main
consumer centers: the meat factories at Gonder, Kembolcha and Mekele, and
the Asmara meat factories and town. The trade stock pattern is:
(a) from the western parts of Tigre Administrative Region to
Gondar or direct to Asmara;
(b) from the remaining highland areas to convenient meat plants;
and
(c) from the eastern lowlands (Danakil area) mainly west to
Kembolcha and Mekele.
In the east, the main producing area is the Ogaden between the Wabi Shebelli
River and the Somalia border. The focal point for cattle consumption is
Dire Dawa with its meat factory and its rail outlet to Addis Abaha and Djibouti.
In the past two years, Saudi traders have exported cattle to Jedda via Djibouti.
For sheep and goats, however, the trade stock movement is across the border
at various points east and southeast of Dire Dawa to traders in Somalia who
re-export the stock to Arabian countries through the Somalia port of Berbera.
A small official export trade in shoats has recently developed, with fattening
in the Awash valley and export via Assab to Saudi Arabia. In addition to the
above trade stock movements there is a constant flow of immature male cattle
from the main producing areas for resale as work oxen in the more heavily
cultivated highland areas throughout the country.



ANNEX 20
Page 2
2.        Trade stock including immature work oxen are normally moved on the
hoof without veterinary restriction along traditional routes to the consumer
areas. These traditional routes, which often pass along the side of an
existing road, do not constitute legal rights of way and have no fenced and
controlled grazing and watering facilities, nor fenced night resting paddocks.
Journeys between watering points are, therefore, often much longer than desir-
able, and animals are only able to graze sporadically as they move along the
route. This unsatisfactory situation results in heavy weight losses, excessive
feed costs (particularly in closely farmed areas), and in fines for trespass
and damage to crops. Surveys carried out by LMB indicate a mean weight loss
of 1-1.5% liveweight per head per day of movement. This amounts to some
Eth$ 15.00 per head per trip if valued at the market price paid per kg at the
terminal market. The value of theft and mortality is indicated Eth$ 1.80 per
head per trip. The IDA financed Livestock II Project is designed to assist
these flows to internal markets by the construction of improved stock routes
including night paddocks, water points, bush markets and abattoirs.
3.        At present, railways transport only a small percentage of marketed
livestock, mainly because railway routes do not cross the major livestock
districts. A noticeable exception is the transport of prime quality Harar
cattle from Dire Dawa to Addis Ababa. More recently, railways have been used
to transport live cattle and sheep from Dire Dawa to Djibouti for export to
Saudi Arabia. Trucking of animals is very much of an exception to usual prac-
tice, although in view of the rapid development of the highway network, this
could play an increasingly significant role - especially for sheep and goats
which are not as easy to trek as cattle. In fact, trucking is increasingly
being used for transporting sheep from the Ogaden region to Dire Dawa for
further export to Saudi Arabia. The Second Livestock Project is expected
to encourage trucking of livestock by LMB and private traders.
Market Channels and Facilities
4.        The domestic market for livestock is, by and large, relatively
simple. Livestock markets may be grouped into three categories:
(a) Primary markets, where stock enters the trade, and which
are attended by any number of small dealers.
(b) Secondary markets, located in cropping areas on the
route towards the final destination.
(c) Terminal markets, located in the main consumption areas -
towns and cities, coffee-growing areas or industrial abattoirs.
Animals are normally traded by direct and time-consuming bargaining with
limited public awareness of actual price levels. In the smaller markets the
buyers, apart from a few local butchers, are usually small livestock traders
sometimes purchasing on behalf of bigger traders and sometimes purchasing
with the sole objective of re-trading the stock in another market further
down the stockroute. In a few markets, auctioneers have established themselves



ANNEX 20
Page 3
and operate on a tribal basis, but this method of selling is not widely prac-
ticed. Direct bargaining is also the rule in the bigger markets or near con-
sumer centers where the buyers are predominantly butchers and butchers' agents.
Sales of slaughter stock to the meat factories are sometimes made direct by
the producer, but the more usual system is for the factory to support a few
major traders who themselves buy at the bigger markets and have arrangements
with smaller traders and agents at smaller markets. Municipalities throughout
the country charge a fee for the use of the "market", commonly between Eth$
2.00 and Eth$ 3.00 per head for cattle and camels and Eth$ 0.30 - Eth$ 0.40
for sheep and goats; only a proportion of the fees are actually collected,
however. LMB has arrangements for improved fee collection in the newly devel-
oping bush markets now being constructed under the Second Livestock Project.
Because of a lack of adequate market facilities, a significant percentage of
trade takes place outside markets, both in areas around the market place where
buyers and sellers meet informally, and in the cattle producing areas between
cattle owners and itinerant traders.
5.        It is expected that the bulk of Project production would be marketed
through existing marketing channels. The amount of additional exports generat-
ed by the Project is difficult to estimate, but it is expected that 75% of the
feedlot production (6,000 head of cattle) will be exported either as live ani-
mals or as meat. In addition, 25% of the animals (about 3,000) fattened by
smallholders are projected to be exported through existing private channels.
Ranches will sell their animals primarily to the feedlot and smallholders;
unsuitable animals will be sold in the local market. The establishment of
the ranches, the smallholder fattening programs and the feedlot would intro-
duce new and modern elements in the traditional production and marketing sys-
tem of the Ethiopian livestock sector. It would provide new and profitable
market outlets for nomadic stockowners and thus improve competition in the
livestock market. Considering these improvements, the existing network of
markets and traders is reasonably efficient and adequate to meet local and
export needs (see Chart, World Bank 9816).
Domestic Markets
6.        According to a recent LMB Study, internal consumption of meat in
Ethiopia absorbs approximately 90% and 95% of the total offtake of cattle
and shoats respectively.

The study also indicate that urban consumption absorbs some 19% and 8% of
cattle and sheep offtakes respectively; however, the relative importance of
urban consumption is increasing rapidly (at least in the case of beef) and
it is expected that this trend will become more and more marked over the coming
decades. In view of the growing significance of urban demand, a rapid devel-
opment of marketing activities is to be expected.
7.        Domestic consumption of meat per capita is well above levels in
most other African countries; however, as total meat production is growing
more slowly than the population, per capita consumption is decreasing. In
1967/68 it was estimated at 15.3 kg, but by 1972/73 it had fallen by 9% to
13.9 kg. Meat production is estimated to have grown by 4.3% during these
five years, while the population increased by 11.2%. This estimate was
also affected by the fact that exports grew by 53% in this same period.
More than 50% of domestic consumption consists of beef, while the rest is
largely sheep, goat and poultry meat.
External Trade
8.        Among the various commodities exported by Ethiopia, livestock and
livestock products are second in importance only coffee, and have contributed
about 10% - 15%0 to the total export earnings during recent years. Total
official exports of livestock and livestock products from Ethiopia increased
from Eth$ 7.9 million in 1968 to Eth$ 25.3 in 1973, representing an annual
increase of 267; this reflects both an increase in quantities of recorded
meat and animals sold as well as rising world market prices.

as all the extensive unrecorded export of meat is live.
The most significant feature in the development of meat exports is the rapid
increase in the share of processed and fresh chilled and frozen meat (FCF).
Indeed, the number of livestock going to export abattoirs, and from there to
canning plants or directly for export as fresh, chilled or frozen meat has been
growing, especially since 1970. Sales to export abattoirs totalled an estimated
68,000 head of cattle in 1970/71 (13%' of the commercial cattle trade), 90,000
head in 1971/72 (18%7 of commercial trade), and 130,000 head in 1972/73 (25% of
commercial cattle trade). The recorded export trade in live animals is quite
variable and erratic. While exports were very high during the early sixties,
they tended to decline until the beginning of the seventies. In 1973, however,
they reached a new height, mainly reflecting increased official exports to
Saudi Arabia, Ethiopia's main market for live animals.
9.        Ethiopian's FCF meat is not acceptable to many importing countries
due to the presence of endemic diseases. Despite the veterinary bans, FCF
meat exports more than tripled during past years. Italy took up the great-
est volume of this trade--most of it in lower quality meat--and further
quantities went to Belgium and the Netherlands. The higher value beef was
sold to the territory of the Afars and Issas and in smaller quantities to
Israel, Lebanon and other Middle Eastern countries. Italy is the main
established purchaser of Ethiopia's processed meat. Israel and Japan are
rapidly becoming important buyers.



ANNEX 20
Page 6
10.       In addition to the meat exports, there is a large traditional
export trade in live animals, most of which is unrecorded.  Estimates indi-
cate the unrecorded annual movement of cattle from Ethiopia to Kenya may
amount to as much as 50,000 head, most of it originating in the southern
region of Ethiopia. This trade is encouraged by Kenya's more efficient
marketing system and the higher prices paid to producers. The annual flow
of livestock to Somalia is apparently even greater. It has been variously
estimated at from 4,000 to 20,000 head of cattle, 3,000 to 10,000 camels and
250,000 to 1,000,000 sheep and goats. In fact, Somalia is maintaining a
lively trade with Arab countries based on Ethiopian livestock (particularly
sheep and goats) through its modern port of Berbera. Better organized markets,
modern slaughter facilities, higher prices and generally lower prices for con-
sumer goods on the Somalian side of the border are the major reasons for this
clandestine trade. It is estimated that the value of unrecorded trade usually
exceeds that of recorded trade. The development of improved stockroutes,
markets, slaughter facilities and the payment of higher producer prices to
stockowners in southern and eastern parts of Ethiopia is expected to substan-
tially reduce unofficial trade.
B. Prices
Domestic Prices
11.       The slow rate of growth of meat production and the rising demand
from increasing population, exports, and purchasing power in the expanding
urban sector has resulted in rising meat prices. The following table gives
an indication of the prices paid on average at terminal markets.
Cattle: Average Price/kg Liveweight
(Eth cents)

ANNEX 20
Page 7
Prices paid in 1974 (for example) for average to good quality animals were
1/3 less to the producer; in that year prices averaged about 45 Eth cents,
the price used in Project calculations. Average prices for locally consumed
cattle as well as for export of live animals have increased by 70%-80% over
the last few years. The most striking feature of the cattle price structure
is the low prices paid by processing plants and the high increase of these
prices since 1970/71. The relatively low prices reflect the fact that pro-
cessing plants cannot compete for average quality animals and are therefore
buyers of poor quality stock, mainly aged work oxen and culled cows. Until
1972/73 the increase in prices can be explained by the low level of Ethiopian
meat prices which were well below those in comparable countries (but which
are now rising nearer world market average prices). The rise up to 1974/75
is mainly explained by the scarcity of animals due to the recent drought.
Faced with a more limited and a more expensive supply and with falling world
market prices for their products, processing plants are having real problems
in obtaining sufficient animals at a price which they can afford, and often
are now working well below their full capacity.
12.       There is a small domestic market for high-quality beef  concentrated
in Addis Ababa and, to a small extent, in Asmara. One aspect of this trade
is the demand for large (370 kg and upwards) well-fattened oxen (which come
mainly from Harar Province) for weddings and other feasts. It has been
estimated that as many as 10,000 of these animals may be produced annually,
and that they sell in Addis Ababa at prices from Eth$ 1.00 to Eth$ 1.50 per
kg liveweight during peak demand periods. The major part of this trade is,
however, aimed at the international community and the high income Ethiopian
consumer in Addis Ababa and Asmara.
13.       Local prices vary with the distance to major consuming centers.
The price paid at country markets in both the north and south for average
quality animals destined for local consumption varies between 40 and 45
Eth cents per kg. Prices in or near major towns are somewhat higher.
Prices also vary with the season. In the north, for example, where there
is a marked dry season, there is a more pronounced seasonal fluctuation
in prices; at the Asmara market the range is apparently from about 40 Eth
cents per kg in October to January just after the rains, to 60 Eth cents
per kg in June/July at the end of the dry season.
14.       Both sheep and cattle prices are determined by the demand for local
consumption. Generally mutton prices are double those of beef and there are
larger variations between regions and over time. The price gradient, reflect-
ing the distance from consumption centers, is greater than for cattle,
presumably as sheep are more difficult to trek and as other forms of trans-
port require cash outlays. Goat prices are up to 25% less than sheep prices
for an animal of the same size and weight.
Export Prices
15.       In line with world market prices, prices of exported meat products
significantly increased until 1972/73. Since then export prices for meat
products have tended to fall on European markets, while prices for live
animals exported to Saudi Arabia have remained fairly stable. (There are



ANNEX 20
Page 8
indications, in fact, that prices in the Jedda market fluctuate less than
world prices.) Although export prices have tended to rise in line with
world prices, price levels are below world market prices on average. While
in the Middle East prices for Ethiopian exports approach those of other
exporting countries, the unit value of exports from Ethiopia to Africa and
to the E.E.C. is well below the world average (see Table 1 and Chart, World
Bank 9952). This is probably explained by a penalty incurred for the disease
risk of Ethiopian exports. Despite more comparable external prices for the
export of beef on some markets, there is a large divergence between Ethiopia
and other countries when internal liveweight prices are compared (Table 2).
One would expect that Ethiopian prices would be comparable with Kenyan prices.
In fact, Ethiopian prices are significantly lower than those in Kenya,
reflecting the heavy costs and large margins incurred in getting livestock
from the producer to the final market. However, with the expected improve-
ment of the marketing system, veterinary services and the production of
better quality animals, it is likely that Ethiopia will participate in the
higher valued trade and that export prices will increase at a greater rate
over the coming years to reach world market levels. Whether these price
increases will be passed on to the producer will depend on a better integra-
tion of stockowners into the market economy and Government's export and
pricing policy.
16.       Despite the rapid increase in livestock exports, the total quantity
and value of exports has been relatively low in relation to the potential of
the Ethiopian livestock industry. The development of the industry has been
handicapped by the presence of endemic diseases, marketing problems resulting
in low producer prices, and a lack of organization in the export trade of
cattle. The future development of the livestock export trade will to a
large extent depend on Government's ability to overcome these difficulties,
the future needs of the domestic market, and on Government's pricing and
export policy.
C. Supply and Demand Projections
17.       At present there are no extensive data available on present meat
consumption patterns in Ethiopia and little is available on matters such as
herd dynamics, offtake rates, etc. The following projections are therefore
of necessity somewhat speculative in nature, and are intended to describe
only broad future tendencies in Ethiopia's meat markets. 1/
18.       Recent LIB studies indicate that over the period 1974-1984 beef
production is projected to rise at 2.08% annually to reach a volume of
260,000 tons in 1984 (compared to 210,000 in 1974). This increase will be
1/   All projections are made in constant prices.



ANNEX 20
Page 9
due to a slight increase in cattle numbers (0.30%/year), a higher increase
in the offtake rate (1.06%/year), and a very slight increase in carcass
weight (0.16%/year). The modest increase in these parameters is attribu-
table in part to the effects of the JP-15 rinderpest campaign as well as to
livestock projects; these are expected to affect about 25% of the cattle
population by 1984/85. It was assumed that the JP-15 campaign (which has
been operating in Ethiopia for the past five years) would result in an
increase in the offtake rate and therefore in total production rather than
in an increase in herd size.
19.       The LMB studies made similar assumptions for sheep and goat meat
production. Data on the present rate of expansion of the national shoat
flock are not available. Evidence suggests, however, that the rate of
increase is very small and is limited by the available grazing. The proj-
ected rate of increase will, therefore, be very small, and is estimated at
0.36% per annum. In general the offtake rates will be assumed to remain
static; however, in areas affected by LMB projects, the present offtake
rate is expected to increase by 10%. Since the shoats in these areas repre-
sent about 15% of the national flock, the national offtake rate is projected
to increase by 0.15% per annum. LMB projects will also result in a small
increase in the average carcass weight (about 0.20% per year). Thus, shoat
production between 1974 and 1985 will rise from 114,000 tons to 122,500 tons,
an increase of 7.5,% over the 10-year period. These projections seem to be
realistic, provided LMB projects are implemented without major delays and
difficulties.
20.       The main factors determining future demand are population growth,
increases in income and the relationship between income and the demand for
meat. The annual growth rate of the Ethiopian population is 2.5%, with an
increase of 2.1% annually in the rural population and 6.6% in urban areas.
On the basis of these figures, the total Ethiopian population at the end of
1974 was about 26,567,500, with 11.5% of the population being urban. No
changes are expected in these growth rates during the next 10 years.
21.       Ethiopia's economic growth prospects are seriously affected by the
stagnation (if not recession) in world output and trade. Ethiopian exports
are declining, while import prices are rising, thus resulting in a consid-
erable deterioration in the country's terms of trade. In addition, the
proposed massive redistribution of land and the ensuing fundamental changes
in the productive and power structures in the rural areas are expected to
have in the short term a detrimental effect on agricultural production and
income. Hence, the mission believes that per capita income will grow at
an annual rate of only 0.5% until the end of this decade. During the early
eighties, however, it is expected that growth will accelerate so that per
capita income is likely to increase by 1.5% per annum. Furthermore, during
both periods it is assumed that--as in past years--incomes of the rural
population will grow less rapidly than those of the urban population.
22.       Reliable figures on income elasticities for Ethiopia are very
scarce. Studies made in Ethiopia and other African countries, however,
indicate that the income elasticity of demand for meat in Ethiopia ranges



ANNEX 20
Page 10
between 0.7% and 1.2%, depending on the type of meat. To simplify demand
projections an income elasticity of 1 has been assumed; consequently domes-
tic demand for meat has been estimated to increase by 3% until i980 and 4%
thereafter.
Export Demand
23.       As previously mentioned, exports of livestock and livestock
products have increased rapidly in recent years. Although export demand
for Ethiopian livestock is presently being depressed by unfavorable world
market conditions, it is estimated that in the medium term exports will
continue to rise. Internally, existing abattoirs and export organizations
will be seeking to increase their output. Several enterprises have plans
for setting up new export oriented livestock ventures, and it is anticipated
that others will become involved in feedlot operations aimed at the export
market. However, the future of these companies will very much depend on
Government's policy toward foreign private companies. At present, it is
difficult to forecast to what extent nationalization of these enterprises
will limit their export performance. Furthermore, as a result of marketing
development in the southern and eastern parts of Ethiopia, the level of
illegal exports can be expected to fall. In addition, the implementation
of LMB projects in these areas (including the establishment of ranches and
a feedlot, better veterinary services and grazing conditions) will improve
the quality of animals and thus open new and higher return markets for
Ethiopian meat products.
24.       Although world market prices for meat fell dramatically in 1974,
it is projected that the long-term upward trend in prices (in constant terms)
will continue at the end of the 1970's. 1/  In fact, world market prices
are forecast to increase by 2.2% per year during the period 1978-1985.
Europe's beef deficit is projected to grow by 1980, but even more impressive
is the expected increase in import requirements for live animals in the
Middle Eastern countries. Because of diseases such as rinderpest and foot-
and-mouth, it is not possible in the short or medium term to envisage
Ethiopia being able to export fresh, chilled or frozen meat to the high-
return markets of Europe. It is unlikely that the next few years will be
sufficient in which to institute the basic veterinary improvements necessary
to remove this trade obstacle. Hence, in the medium term the bulk of
Ethiopia's meat exports will be in the form of live exports. However,
Ethiopia should be able to gradually penetrate the export market for beef
in the Middle East as well as in some European countries. In fact, it is
expected that the strong preference of Arab Middle Eastern countries
(mainly Saudi Arabia) for live animals will relax, and enable an expansion
of the chilled or frozen beef trade. The industrial development of these
countries now being financed by oil revenues should be well advanced by 1980
and should help ease these restrictions. Furthermore, it is projected that
1/ IBRD Price Forecasts for major primary commodities - June 19, 1974.



ANNEX 20
Page 11
demand for beef for the processipg industries in most importing countries
will rise even faster than that for meat as a whole, thus providing good
export prospects for high quality beef coming from Ethiopian feedlots.
However, the opening of these new markets will very much depend on the
establishment of a more sophisticated meat processing industry and on
Government's export policy. Overall, considering the favorable demand
outlook in world markets--in particular in Near and Middle Eastern countries
--Ethiopia should not encounter any major difficulties in finding profitable
outlets for its meat exports, especially as the share of Ethiopian exports
in these markets will remain very small.
25.       LMB estimates that exports of cattle are expected to increase
from 230,000 head in 1974 to 480,000 head in 1984, an average rate of in-
crease of 7.6% per annum. Legal exports of shoats are now around 150,000
head per annum and illegal exports are estimated at around 500,000 head.
The level of legal exports can be expected to rise as a result of anticipated
improvements in marketing, and investments in fattening and finishing opera-
tions. Although the growth in this export trade is difficult to predict,
it will most likely grow at a rate of 3.3% per annum until 1984. This should
result in legal exports of 600,000 head and illegal exports of 300,000 head,
giving a total of 900,000 head per annum in that year.
Conclusion
26.       Given these supply and demand projections, it can be concluded
that despite the modestly projected increase in per capita income over the
coming years, demand will tend to exceed available supply of meat in
Ethiopia. Hence, the declining trend in per capita meat consumption in
Ethiopia is likely to continue until the mid-1980's. However, due to the
faster rate of increase in the urban population and to higher urban revenues,
it is likely that consumption of urban inhabitants will decline less than
that of the rural population. Furthermore, because of the relative scarcity
of meat, there will be a steady rise in the level of prices for both cattle
and shoats, estimated at between 1% and 2% up to 1980 and 2%-3% until 1984.
These price projections assume that there will be no import of meat and no
ban on exports.
27.       Faced with growing consumer demand and rising meat prices the
Government has instituted a system of price controls. However, these con-
trols only apply to three broadly defined categories of animals and permit
prices to be negotiated between buyer and seller over a range from Eth$ 150 to
Eth$ 350 per animal. Smaller and larger animals have recently been exempted
from the controls. Thus, it is considered that actual prices will be deter-
mined by market conditions.
28.       Whatever policy Government does follow, it is imperative that
projected price increases for meat in the domestic as well as in world
markets be passed on to the producer. Indeed, providing a financial incen-



ANNEX 20
Page 12
tive will be necessary to stimulate the offtake from the national herd and
channel it into the market stream. Moreover, the small producer, presently
a reluctant seller, may change--his cultural outlook towards cattle if given
greater financial incentives. Thus, a better remuneration for the stock-
owners will be an essential factor in the development of the livestock sector
in Ethiopia and certainly a key element for the success of this Project.
October 30, 1975

ANNEX 21

ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Government Cash Flow
1.        Government would finance Eth$ 10.50 million of the total Project
costs. After the implementation period, Government would be required to
cover annual operating costs of about Eth$ 1.9 million as well as debt service
payments to IDA and ADF. As no fees are expected to be charged for Project
services, the Project units would have no direct income. Government would,
however, recoup these expenditures in a number of ways. The most important
of these would be through:
(a) taxes (import duties, transaction taxes, turn-over tax)
levied on Project inputs;
(b) an income tax levied on salaries paid to Project staff;
(c) market and export fees levied on the Project's incremencal
output (accruing both to Government and to LMB);
(d) a tax on the net incremental income accruing to stock owners
as a consequence of the Project.
At full development the Project would generate about Eth$ 3 million of tax
revenues annually; these would cover the Government's debt service payments
to IDA and ADF as well as the operating costs of three implementation units,
and would also allow Government to recuperate part of its initial investment.
Because of the large investment experditures required by the three sub-
projects, the Government cash flow would, however, remain negative (Table 1).
Consequently, Government is now giving consideration to the recommendation
that it levy a special development tax in the form of a head tax on the
inhabitants of the Project areas; such a tax would be instituted only at the
end of the five-year implementation period. As long as the tax is set at a
moderate level, it is believed that this method of raising additional Govern-
ment revenues in the rangelands areas would be justified.
2.        The estimates of income to be derived from taxes on Project inputs
are based on prevailing import duties, transaction and turn-over taxes mainly
for vehicles, fuel, furniture and equipment. Income tax revenue estimates
are based on the income tax laws and the staff list and salary scales used
by the three sub-projects. Expatriate staff is tax exempt. Income taxes
represent about 8.5% of total salaries and wages.
3.        Livestock marketed from the Project area will, for the most part,
be consumed within Ethiopia. If marketed in official markets in the Project



ANNEX 21
Page 2
areas, they will be subject to a market fee; it is assumed that 50% of such
market fees from incremental livestock marketed from the Project areas can
be regarded as a net increase in Government's revenue (the remaining 50%
will be consumed by increased costs). If exported, livestock will be subject
both to an LMB export levy and to a Government export tax; all of these can
be regarded as net additions to Government revenues. The scale of revenues
forecast per animal is as follows:
Cattle    Sheep      Goats
(Eth$)--------
Market Fee (50% of total)                  1.00       0.15      0.15
LMB Export Levy                            1.00      0.20       0.20
Government Export Tax (flat rate}          5.00          -         -
Government Export-Transaction Tac
(at 2%)                                 2.50       0.60       0.60
9.50      0.95       0.95
4.        The incremental number of animals marketed from the Project area
is given in Annex 19. Total fiscal revenue generated by this incremental
offtake will depend on the proportion of animals going through the official
domestic and export markets respectively, as well as the level of future illicit
export trade. Rather arbitrarily it las been assumed that 25% of the incremental
member of animals marketed in the first year of the Project would pay the full
amount of market fees due; this would rise to 45% by the fifth year and to 75%
in the seventh and subsequent years. It is also projected that the percentage
of incremental official exports of animals will slightly increase from 27% at
the beginning of the Project to about 30% at its maturity. Both figures re-
present national average of actual and estimated official exports calculated
by LMB.
5.        The average incidence of all taxation in Ethiopia on gross monetary
national product is about 8%-10%. Figures are not available on inter-regional
differences in the incidence of taxation. It is likely that the marginal
incidence for increments in income is somewhat above the average incidence.
On the other hand, because of the generally low levels of income in the
Project areas, the high level of illicit contraband trade, and the absence
of an effective income tax system among pastoralists, it is probable that
the incidence of general taxation on increments in stockowners' incomes and
expenditure in the Project area will be quite low; it has arbitrarily been
assumed at a rate of 5% of increments in net cash income. It has been assumed
that through better tax collection and increased commercialization in the
Project areas, this percentage would gradually increase to 10% at Project
maturity.
October 30, 1975

ANNEX 22

ETHIOPIA
RANGELANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Evaluation
1.        The internal economic rate of return of the proposed Project would
be 24%. The basic assumptions used in this analysis were:
(a) The economic life of the Project was assumed to be 20 years.
(b) Price contingencies, import taxes and duties were excluded
from the cost estimates.
(c) Foreign exchange costs and benefits were valued upward by
30% to a rate of Eth$ 2.70 per US$1.00 (as compared with an
official exchange rate of Eth$ 2.07 per US$1.00) to more
accurately reflect the value of foreign exchange to Ethiopia.
(d) Unskilled labor was shadow priced at Eth$ 300 per man-year
in view of the substantial under-employment in the range
area; this represents approximately the annual income of
a family in the subsistence sector.
2.        Individual rates of return were calculated for each of the three
sub-projects. These were estimated at 22% for Southern Rangelands, 25%
for Jijigga, and 26% for Northeast Rangelands. Rates of return were also
calculated for specific components within the Project; these are estimated
at 17% for the ranches, 29% for the feedlot, and 13% for the water spreading/
irrigation program.
3.        If market prices were used in this analysis the overall Project
rate of return would be 22%. Likewise the rates of return for the three
sub-projects would be 20% for Southern Rangelands, 24% for Jijigga, and 25%
for Northeast Rangelands.
4.        The sensitivity of the rate of return to changes in some of the
basic parameters is illustrated below:

(a)  10% Increase in Costs                    21%
(b)  10% Reduction in Benefits                21%
